II
Preface
This master thesis of 30 ETCS points was written at the International 
Centre for Indoor Environment and Energy (ICEE) at the Department of 
Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark in collaboration 
with EKJ. EKJ is currently involved in the design of the Natural History 
Museum to be built underneath the Botanical Gardens of Copenhagen. 
The thesis will not simulate the new museum but is a general research of 
the building typology. However, the simpliﬁ ed walls and ﬂ oors used for 
simulations were based on early sketches by EKJ
This research involved several diff erent studies including indoor climate 
in museums, user behaviour, energy simulation and soil temperatures. 
These studies were used for the ﬁ nal simulations but can be studied 
individually. Even though this project was written as an engineering 
thesis about underground museums, certain aspects should thus be 
of relevance for those involved with museums in general, underground 
buildings or energy simulation.
A large number of museums provided data on visitor presence. 
Furthermore, user behaviour was investigated at M/S Maritime Museum 
of Denmark based on surveillance tapes.
The thesis was supervised by Senior Researcher Rune Korsholm Andersen
and Professor Carsten Rode.

IV
Abstract English
Several underground museums have recently been built in Denmark 
and currently four new underground museums are being designed. It 
is therefore relevant to study whether underground museum has the 
potential to lower the often very high energy consumption of museums. 
The standard methods for simulating underground buildings in the 
building performance simulation programme IDA ICE is too simpliﬁ ed to 
use as a basis for detailed energy simulations. Furthermore, users will 
have a large inﬂ uence on the energy consumptions in museums as they 
aff ect both the heating-, cooling and ventilation systems. Unfortunate-
ly, user patterns and the number of visitors have previously not been 
thoroughly studied.
A method combining results from 2D heat transfers simulations with 
a building performance programme was made in order to make more 
precise simulations of underground buildings. Entrance data from 23 
Danish museums was used in order to study visitor patterns. This data 
was also used to study the correlation between summer weather and 
number of visitors in 7 museums in order to determine if visitor presence 
can be predicted. No museums could provide data on the user pattern 
on an hourly basis or had data on the occupancy densities (pers/m2). A 
study on the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark was, therefore, made 
in order to study these parameters based on one month of surveillance 
tapes. The user patterns from these studies were implemented into the 
building performance simulations. A study was made on the requirements 
for the indoor climate in museums in order to have realistic settings for 
the simulations. Additionally, the requirements from 9 Danish museums 
was used in order to study how the indoor climate set points was deﬁ ned 
in practice.  
In all cases, the simulated underground museum used less energy than 
a similar museum above ground. The inﬂ uence of the stable tempera-
tures underground was largest if very little insulation was used. Even 
if an above ground museum had walls with large thermal capacity and 
300 mm of insulation an underground museum would use 2-3 kWh/m2 
less per year. By using 500 mm of insulation the diff erence between 
the energy use of an above- and below ground museum was very small. 
The occupancy densities and the settings for the control systems had 
an often larger inﬂ uence on the total energy consumption than if the 
museum was placed above or below ground.
It can be concluded that the energy use of an underground museum will 
be lower than for the above ground museum. How large the diff erence 
between above- and below ground will be, vary greatly depending on the 
construction, the user pattern and the control systems. Placing museums 
underground, thus, has some potential beneﬁ ts in regards to energy use, 
but it is not the short cut for making museums energy effi  cient.
==PAGE:7==
V
Abstract Danish 
Indenfor de sidste par år er ﬂ ere underjordiske museer blevet bygget i Danmark 
og ﬁ re nye er på nuværende tidspunkt på tegnebrættet. Siden nogle museer 
har meget højt energiforbrug er det relevant at undersøge om det at placere 
et museum under jorden kan være med til at mindske dette forbrug.
Programmet IDA ICE kan bruges til at beregne energiforbruget i bygninger, 
men metoderne til at simulere underjordiske bygninger er for simpliﬁ cerede 
til at kunne bruges til en detaljeret analyse. Derudover vil brugere have en 
stor indﬂ ydelse på energiforbruget i museer da de påvirker både varme-, køle- 
og ventilationssystemerne. Desværre er der ikke tidligere lavet grundige un-
dersøgelser af antallet af besøgende og brugeradfærd i museer.
En metode der kombinerede resultaterne fra simuleringer af 2D varmetrans-
port med simuleringer i et bygningsenergiprogram blev lavet for at kunne 
udføre mere præcise simuleringer af underjordiske bygninger. På baggrund af 
data om antallet af besøgende blev besøgsmønstre undersøgt i 23 danske 
museer. Dette data blev også brugt til at analysere sammenhængen mellem 
vejret om sommeren og antallet af besøgende i 7 museer for at undersøge 
hvorvidt besøgstallet kan forudsiges. Ingen museer kunne udlevere data om 
besøgstal på timebasis eller havde data omkring tætheden af personer (pers/
m2). Derfor blev disse parametre undersøgt vha. en måneds overvågningsvideo 
fra M/S Museet for Søfart. Besøgsmønstre fra disse undersøgelser blev brugt 
til simuleringer af bygningers energiforbrug. Derudover blev indeklimakravene 
i museer undersøgt for at have realistiske værdier til simuleringerne. De relle 
indstillingsværdier fra 9 danske museer blev brugt til at studere hvordan 
kravene blev deﬁ neret i praksis.
I alle tilfælde brugte det simulerede underjordiske museum mindre energi 
end et museum over jorden. De stabile jordtemperaturer havde dog større 
indﬂ ydelse hvis der blev brugt mindre isolering. Selv hvis et overjordisk 
museum havde vægge med stor termisk kapacitet og 300 mm isolering 
ville et underjordisk museum bruge 2-3 kWh/m2 mindre om året. Ved at 
bruge 500 mm isolering, blev forskellen på energiforbrug af museum 
over- og under jorden meget lille. Tætheden af personer og indstillingerne 
for kontrolsystemet havde ofte en større indﬂ ydelse på det totale ener-
giforbrug end om museet lå over eller under jorden.
Det kan konkluderes at energiforbruget for et underjordisk museum vil 
være mindre end for et museum over jorden. Men hvor stor forskellen 
i energiforbrug på at placere et museum over og under jorden vil være 
afh ænger af konstruktionen, brugeradfærd og kontrolsystemet. At 
placere et museum har derfor nogen potentielle fordele mht. energifor-
brug, men det er ikke en genvej til at gøre et museum energieff ektivt.

VIII
==PAGE:11==
1
01 Introduction
In Denmark 30-40% of the energy is used on heating, ventilation and 
lighting of buildings (Danish Energy Agency, 2016). Regulations on 
the energy use of buildings are, thus, an eff ective way of lowering the 
overall energy consumption. It is expected that the Danish energy frame 
for new buildings is set to 20-25 kWh/m2 per year from 2020 (Building 
regulations a, 2016). Meanwhile, an energy consumption of 400-500 
kWh/m2 per year is not uncommon in many museums (Larsen & Padﬁ eld, 
2011). If museums are to meet the requirements research is needed for 
ﬁ nding ways to lower the need for energy. 
The current trend in museum building, especially in Denmark, is to place 
the museum underground. Currently four new underground museum 
buildings are being designed (National History museum of Denmark, The 
Museum of Danish Resistance, Blåvand Bunker museum and Himmelhaven 
at Ordgrupgaard). As a researcher, engineer, curator, architect, planner or 
any other profession involved in these projects, it is highly relevant to 
ask the question whether or not this current trend has the possibility 
to make museums more sustainable? Could the stable ground temper-
atures and thermal mass of the ground be used for lowering the large 
energy needs? As the current methods for simulating the underground 
conditions in tools such as IDA ICE are very simpliﬁ ed, a more precise 
method is needed for answering those questions. By incorporating two 
dimensional heat ﬂ ows from COMSOL into a simulation work ﬂ ow with 
IDA ICE, better calculations can be made. 
By making energy simulations of museums with a constant amount of 
users, the need for energy can appear much lower than it actually will be. 
The reason is that by using average values the extreme cases are cut off  
even though these are the most energy consuming. Users of museums 
are typically employees and visitors. Employees can be assumed to be 
present during work hours at certain parts of the museum. However, the 
presence of visitors is diffi  cult to predict since it in most cases is based 
on the will of each individual, rather than a need to visit museums. No 
standardized user schedules are developed and thus research are needed 
on how to make realistic assumptions. Based on data from several Danish 
museums as well at data obtained through surveillance tapes at the M/S 
Maritime Museum of Denmark, a method for making a dynamic yearly 
schedule is developed for in order to make simulations closer to realistic 
conditions.
The HVAC engineer often works with a set of conﬂ icting goals of 
achieving good indoor climate for users with a minimized energy use. 
The optimal solution will therefore need to be a compromise between 
the goals. However, working on indoor climate for museums is adding 
to the complexity. Often the indoor climate suitable for preservation is 
not comfortable for the users. Furthermore, the preservation of certain 
objects can result in a need for extremely stable climate conditions 
causing a very large energy demand and large running costs. The term 
museum is also used for range of widely diff erent buildings with very 
diff erent functions. The result is that, even though guidelines exists, the 
indoor climate at museums diff ers greatly. As to be seen in this thesis 
using diff erent requirement for the indoor climate can result in very 
diff erent energy consumptions.
This thesis will add to the knowledge of user behaviour in museums, 
the impact of indoor climate requirements on the energy consumption 
and the potential beneﬁ ts in regards to energy use of placing museums 
underground. Hopefully, this work can help the developers of the new 
museums to make well-founded decisions as well as inspire others to 
continue the research in museum energy, underground buildings and user 
behaviour.
==PAGE:12==
2
02 Objectives
RESEARCH QUESTION:
The subject of this thesis is energy consumption in underground 
museums. In Denmark, many underground museums have been built 
in the last couple of years and currently four new Danish underground 
museum buildings are be designed. There are several reasons why 
museums are currently being placed underground, but so far sustainabil-
ity has not been a deciding factor. Museums can have very large energy 
consumptions and it is therefore relevant to ask: 
Is it beneﬁ cial to place museums underground in regards to 
energy use?
To carry out this calculation, a set of parameters have to be accurately 
studied in order to get realistic results. 
Underground temperatures
Underground museums will thermally perform diff erently than on ground 
museums. The soil has more stable temperatures than the ambient air 
and when taking the thermal mass of the soil into consideration the 
need for heating and cooling could be minimized. Investigations are to be 
made on how the temperatures and thermal mass inﬂ uence the energy 
consumption. Underground buildings can potentially utilize a greater 
thermal mass by using the soil to store excess heat. 
 
User behaviour
Each user has little inﬂ uence on the indoor climate of a museum, since 
one is usually not allowed to open a window or change the settings of 
thermostats. But since the number of users in museums varies greatly 
throughout the day, week and year users have a big impact on the ability 
of the active systems to maintain the desired indoor climate. Studies 
will determine user patterns as well as estimations on realistic loads 
that will be used for simulating the energy use of museums. Admission 
statistics from a number of Danish museums as well as an analysis at the 
M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark in Elsinore will form the basis for this 
research. 
Set points
All energy calculations are done based on a certain set of values for a 
range of indoor climate parameters. However, the set points for the indoor 
climate in museums diff er from one museum to another due to the type 
of building and collection. The basis for calculating the performance of 
the museums are thus diff erent. The aim of this thesis is not to evaluate 
if these set points are set correctly, but rather to investigate the impact 
on the energy use.
Figure 1. Important factors in calculation of energy use of underground 
museums
Museum parameters
Underground 
building parameter
Energy use
Underground 
temperatures
User behavior
Set points
+/-

==PAGE:13==
3
Many more interesting and relevant studies could be made in this ﬁ eld. 
There has been made several interesting studies on museum storages 
(for instance Padﬁ eld et al., 2006, Ryhl-Svendsen et al., 2011).  There-
fore, this thesis will focus on the exhibition area instead. This area of 
museum is of great importance as it often accounts for a large part of 
the energy use. 
This thesis will study the running energy use and will not deal with the 
embedded energy of building underground, life cycle assessments or 
any structural or economic considerations. The inﬂ uence of ground wa-
ter ﬂ ows will also not be addressed. The use of renewables to counter 
the often large energy use is an interesting but comprehensive, subject 
also not included in this work. Moreover, the focus is set on underground 
museums in Denmark as much diff erent results could be obtained in 
other climates. 
Method
The diff erent parts of the thesis are presented in the roadmap (Figure 
2). After a short description of cases a series of studies are done on the 
indoor climate at museums and the visitor presence. As the standard 
method for calculation an underground building in IDA ICE are to simpli-
ﬁ ed an alternative method is developed. 
In the end these studies will be used for simulations of the energy use 
of simpliﬁ ed museums buildings with wall, ﬂ oor and ceiling construc-
tions having resemblance to early sketches of the new National History 
Museum of Denmark. All simulations in this thesis are done using COM-
SOL for heat transfer simulations and IDA ICE for energy simulations 
(introductions to the programs are found in appendix 01).
Cases
Why and how are museum placed underground?
Indoor climate for preservation
What is indoor climate for preservation and what are the 
guidelines for set points?
Set points in practice
What are the set points in museums?
Relative humidity
How is the vapour balance in museums 
and how to control it?
Visitors
Can admittance data from museums be used
to estimate when people go to museums?
Visitor presence - At M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark
Where and when are people present in a museum?
Lighting loads
What are realistic lighting loads for a new museum?
Ground temperatures
How to calculte the temperatures of the soil?
Combine COMSOL and IDA ICE
How to combine simulations of these programs?
Simulations
Is it possible to save energy by placing museums underground?

==PAGE:14==
4
03 Cases
The following section will brieﬂ y describe some of the underground 
museums built in Denmark as well as some of the underground museums 
to be built in the near future. The diff erent cases will show the variety 
within this building typology and provide a basic knowledge of the 
museums that will be referred to in the thesis.
As seen on the following pages, four underground museums in Denmark 
are in the process of being designed. This thesis was done in collabora-
tion with EKJ, currently being involved in the development of the Natural 
History Museum of Denmark.
Additionally, the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark collaborated in order 
to gain knowledge from a built case. The museum was used for measure-
ments of user behaviour as well as providing valuable experience on how 
to run a new underground museum in Denmark (appendix 04).
The high number of underground projects recently build and under 
development shows that the research done in this thesis is highly 
relevant. To get a perspective on the calculations and simulations that 
is presented in this thesis, it is useful to understand how and why the 
underground museum is being built. 
M/S MARITIME MUSEUM OF DENMARK
Architects: BIG
Engineers: Rambøll
Size: 17,500 m2 (Politiken, 2014)
Opened: 2013
The Maritime Museum of Elsinore was situated at Kronborg Castle for 
98 years, but due to donations from 11 diff erent funds it was possible 
to build a separate museum. It was designed by BIG and opened up in 
October 2013 (Andersen, 2013). The new building has caused the 
museum to make a transition from a traditional and little known museum 
(Andersen, 2013) to put Elsingor on the New York times list for places to 
visit in 2014 (The New York Times, 2014), being one of ﬁ ve ﬁ nalists for 
the 2015 Mies van der Rohe award (Archdaily,  2015) and winning the 
Archdaily award for best culture building (Politiken, 2014).
The museum is placed in the walls of a former dry dock and no part of the 
building is placed above ground. In the middle of the museum are windows 
surrounding the old dry dock providing daylight to the underground 
museum.
Figure 3.  M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark (BIG a, 2016)
==PAGE:15==
5
NATURAL HISTORY MUSEUM OF DENMARK
Architects: 
Lundgaard & Tranberg Arkitekter, Claus Pryds, Steen Høyer
Engineers: EKJ
Size: 20.900 m2 where 8.300 m2 are existing buildings to be incorpo-
rated (SNM competition brief, 2011)
To open: 2020
The new Natural History Museum is planned to be placed underneath 
the botanical gardens of Copenhagen and is set to open in 2020. 
The funding comes from four private funds, the Danish state and the 
University of Copenhagen and is estimated to cost 950 million Dkk. 
The Natural History Museum of Denmark will combine the Botanical 
Museum, the Geological Museum and the Zoological Museum all owned 
by the University of Copenhagen as well as four research institutes. It 
is excepted that the number of visitors will increase from 150,000 to 
400,000 a year. Furthermore, it is expected that the percentage of 
tourists will increase from only 2% at the moment to 33% (New Natural 
History Museum, 2016).
Figure 4. Section of the new Natural History Museum of Denmark (Lund-
gaard & Tranberg Arkitekter, 2016)
BLÅVAND BUNKER MUSEUM
Architects: BIG
Engineers: Rådgivende ingeniører Johansson & Kalstrup P/S
Size: 2400 m2 (Varde Museum b, 2016)
To open: 2017
The municipality of Varde wanted to build a museum hidden underneath 
the sand dunes, combining a bunker museum, an amber museum, a 
special exhibition gallery and a “histolarium” being an interactive gallery 
about the history of Blåvand (Byggecentrum, 2014). Due to the special 
site chosen, it has been important to make a building that is both “breath-
taking and invisible”. The existing bunker on site is to be ﬁ tted with a 
transparent canon replica, functioning as a skylight as well as showing 
where a canon was originally placed. (Varde Museum, 2012). It is currently 
being designed by BIG in collaboration with the local engineering 
ﬁ rm Johansson & Kalstrup (Varde Museum, 2016). The access to the 
underground museum buildings is provided by four cuts into the dunes. 
These cuts will function as inner walls in between the four museums 
parts and will be ﬁ tted with windows to provide daylight. One of these 
cuts is shown in Figure 3 (BIG b, 2016).
Figure 5. Blåvand bunker museum (BIG b, 2016)
==PAGE:16==
6
ORDRUPGAARD EXTENSION
Architects: Snøhetta 
Engineers: Dansk Energi Management & Esbensen and EKJ 
Size: 1.600 m2
To open: unknown 
The art museum Ordrupgaard is situated in an upper class family home 
from 1917 (VisitCopenhagen, a 2016). Ordrupgaard was in 2005 
extended with a 1,150 m2 building done by Zaha Hadid (Ordupgaard a, 
2016), but now the museum plans to enlarge the area even further by 
having an underground building done by Snøhetta called “Himmelhaven”. 
This will, eventually, be the ﬁ rst building done in Denmark by the interna-
tionally known Norwegian architectural ﬁ rm (Ordrupgaard b, 2016). 
Figure 6.  Himmelhaven by Snøhetta (Ordrupgaard 2016)
MOESGAARD MUSEUM
Architects: Henning Larsen Architects and landscape architects Kris-
tine Jensens Tegnestue
Engineers: COWI
Size: 15,500 m2
Opened: 2014
Moesgaard Museum is an archaeological and ethnographical museum 
from 1970 and is known for the mummy Grauballemanden. The museum 
was originally placed in an old manor, now situated next to the new 
museum. The old manor is now used for teaching by the University of 
Aarhus. On October 2014, the offi  cial opening of the new 15,500 m2 
and 393 million Dkk museum took place (Rottensten, 2015). Due to 
Moesgaard being an archaeological, museum the museum is being placed 
under ground. It also ensured not to destroy the beautiful landscape in 
which the manor is built. The roof is earth covered and follows the natural 
sloping of the site, making the large building volume become part of the 
landscape (Bundegaard, 2014).  
Figure 7. The new Moesgaard museum (Henning Larsen Architects, 2016)
==PAGE:17==
7
THE MUSEUM OF DANISH RESISTANCE
Architects: Lundgaard & Tranberg Arkitekter
Engineers: EKJ, Dansk Energi Management & Esbensen and ÅF Light-
ing – Hansen og Henneberg
Size: 1600 m2 
Opening: end of 2018
The old Museum of Danish Resistance caught ﬁ re 28 April of 2013. 
It was too damaged to be rebuild, but all of the exhibition was saved 
(National Museum of Denmark, 2016). A competition for building a new 
Museum of Danish Resistance at the same site was therefore made in 
2014 (National Museum of Denmark, 2014). The museum is planned 
to be 1600 m2, where 1300 m2 is planned to be placed underground. 
The only spaces placed above the ground is an ellipse shaped entrance 
building and the waste storage (Indreby Lokaludvalg, 2016). The entrance 
building in 2 stories will contain shop, info desk, cafeteria and auditorium, 
and the rest of the building site is to be a lowered gravel garden. Below 
ground the museum is planned to be a big rectangular exhibition space 
with the ellipse shape in the middle containing stairs, elevators, a smaller 
exhibition space and small niches (National Museum of Denmark, 2015).
Figure 8. The gravel garden and the entrance building visualized by Lun-
dgaard & Tranberg Arkitekter (National Museum of Denmark 2016)
CISTERNERNE
Architects: Københavns Vandforsyning
Engineers: Københavns Vandforsyning
Size: 4320 m2
Opening: 1856
Cisternerne is an old water reservoir from 1856. The placement in a hill, 
made it possible to provide pressure to the water to reach the top of 
the newly build tall town-houses in Copenhagen. The water was pumped 
from nearby lakes into the reservoir. The Cholera epidemic in 1853 caused 
4.300 deaths in Copenhagen and clean water supplied by reservoir was 
one of the means to avoid similar epidemics. The reservoir was open 
towards the ambient as a pond in front of the nearby castle until 1891, 
where a grass covered concrete roof was constructed to avoid contami-
nation. In 1933, the reservoir was no longer used, but it was not emptied 
from water until 1981. As a part of Copenhagen being the culture city 
of 1996, the reservoir was turned into an exhibition space. Cisternerne 
consist of 3 big 4.3 m high rooms with pillars and a RH constantly close 
to 100%. Stalactites are naturally present at Cisternerne adding to the 
cave-like atmosphere. The walls are made of granite and ﬂ oor of concrete 
and neither is insulated. There is no heating system and is, thus, closed 
from December to February (Cisternerne a, 2016).
Figure 9. Interior view of Cisternerne (Cisternerne b, 2016).
==PAGE:18==
8
OTHER UNDERGROUND MUSEUMS IN DENMARK
Many underground or partly underground museums exist in Denmark. 
The following is a description of some of the some of them but and not 
all of the Danish underground museum spaces will be mentioned.
A great number of other Danish museum buildings have extended their 
building volumes with underground extensions. In 2014, the listed 
museum building, Den Frie, had 1000 m2 ﬂ oor area added underground 
by Bente Lange Arkitekter. This space is used for a bookstore, storage 
and additional exhibition space (A.P Møller Fonden a, 2014). In the 
dense center of Copenhagen, Lundgaard & Tranberg Architects designed 
additional exhibition space below ground for The Worker's Museum in 
2005 (Lundgaard & Tranberg Architects b, 2016). The Danish Castles 
Centre had a new exhibition building designed by Lundgaard and 
Tranberg Architects in 2013 (A.P Møller Fonden b, 2014). Most of the 
Figure 10. The unbuilt underground Jorn museum by Utzon (Silkeborgarkiv, 
2016)
1200 m2 building is placed underground. The use of corten steel for the 
visible part of the building and the placement below ground level gives 
the volume more of a ruin- than building appearance that ﬁ ts with the 
area (The Danish Castle Centre, 2016).
During the renovation of Glyptoteket, the museum was also added 
underground exhibition space (Bygtek, 2006) and The Black Diamond 
by schmidt hammer lassen architects built in 1999 (DAC, 2016) has the 
National Museum of Photography placed in the basement (VisitCopenha-
gen b, 2016). One unbuilt, but well known reference is the underground 
Asger Jorn museum by Utzon, proposed in 1964. It was meant to be built 
in Silkeborg, but was never constructed due to technical diffi  culties and 
in 1982 an entirely diff erent museum was constructed (Museum Jorn, 
2016).
Another type of underground museums are the ones exhibiting 
underground attractions such as excavated ruins. Examples of these 
are The "Gråbrødrekloster Museet" being placed 3 m below a pedestrian 
street in Aalborg (The Historical Museum of Northern Jutland, 2016) 
and Sankt Laurentius Museum placed 2 m below the ground in Roskilde 
(Sankt Laurentius, 2016). For these exhibitions an above ground museum 
is obviously not an option.
Figure 11. Undergrond gallery at The Danish Castles Center (Lundgaard & 
Tranberg Architects c, 2016)
==PAGE:19==
9
DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
Based on the presented cases it can be seen that underground museums 
are a highly relevant subject. The reasons for building museums 
underground are numerous and will diff er from case to case. Being placed 
in a beautiful landscape, at a historical site or dense urban areas can 
cause at least parts of the museums to be placed underground. It is not 
the potential for a lower energy use which have made designers build 
underground but rather architectural visions or limitations of the sites. 
The two Danish architecture ﬁ rms, BIG and Lundgaard & Tranberg 
Architects, can be seen to have had a great impact on the use of this 
typology, as both architectural companies have designed underground 
museums as well as being in the process of designing two new 
underground museums each. The typology is popular among the next 
generation of architects as the underground museum is a popular 
topic among many master thesis projects at the architecture school of 
Copenhagen (according to Ryhl-Svendsen appendix 02).
In the past, ideas like the Jorn museum did not turn into built projects 
due to technical diffi  culties. But now, a large amount of underground 
museums is being build. This thesis will investigate this building typology 
from an energy perspective. Due to the large amount of projects recently 
being build, it would be useful if these projects were monitored and 
researched thoroughly in order to identify initial as well as long term 
challenges and beneﬁ ts in regards to both structure, maintenance and 
building energy.
UNDERGROUND MUSEUMS WORLDWIDE
The underground museum is not a Danish typology, as many fully or partly 
underground museums are built or are planned to be build world-wide. 
One of the most famous is the underground extension of Louvre done 
by M Pei in 1989 (Goldberger, 1989) and another well-known example is 
the underground passageways underneath The Jewish Museum of Berlin 
done by Daniel Liebeskind (berlin.de 2016). 
The underground museum is, in the near future, going to be relevant 
to discuss internationally. A master-plan done by BIG architects for the 
Smithsonian will connect the existing museum buildings by underground 
connections, galleries, a store and an auditorium. The big project is 
expected to costs 2 billion US$ and the construction is to start in 5-7 
years and take up to 20 years to complete (The Washington Post, 2014). 
An 80 million GBP underground museum is planned in Dorset, England 
(BBC, 2015) and in 2017 an entirely underground museum in New Delhi 
is to exhibit art, culture and architectural history (Verma, 2015).
Figure 12. The glass pyramids of Louvre serving as skylights for the under-
ground parts of the museum (PlanetWare, 2016)
==PAGE:20==
10
04  Indoor Climate for   
preservation
In order to estimate set point values for an indoor climate at a museum a 
short review of the environmental factors was made. It was not possible 
to ﬁ nd set point values that can be used for all museum collections, but 
guideline values of 40-60% RH, no more than 25 ºC and limited daylight 
are suitable for many museums.
When designing museum buildings, it is important to remember that the 
indoor climate demands are not solely based on user comfort, but also on 
preventive conservation of museum artefacts. Preservation is a separate 
ﬁ eld of study mainly practised by conservation specialists. However, as 
an engineer a basic knowledge of the subject is needed in order to work 
with energy use of museums. The aim of the preservation is to limit the 
need for conservation work on the objects as the treatment will change 
the artefacts and reduce the authenticity (Ryhl-Svendsen and Padﬁ eld, 
2003).
Diff erent materials have diff erent requirements and taking this into 
account can maximize the lifespan of the museum collection (Erhardt, 
Tumosa and Mecklenburg, 2007). At the same time, there is a lack of 
scientiﬁ c evidence for strict environmental standards for preservation 
(Padﬁ eld, 2007). Recommendations has been based mainly on building 
regulations, limitations of the HVAC systems and costs of implementation 
and maintenance and only recent research has provided a scientiﬁ c basis 
for the recommended values (Erhardt et al., 2007). Recent suggested 
values for ﬂ uctuations allow for much larger deviations in temperature 
and RH than traditionally used in many museums (Atkinson, 2014)
HUMIDITY
The relative humidity (RH) is a very important factor in preservation. 
When designing a indoor climate for museums three factors are to be 
determined in regards to the RH. The ﬁ rst is the set point value, the 
second is the allowable ﬂ uctuation and the ﬁ nal parameter to specify is 
the seasonal drift (Erhardt et al, 1995).
Earlier on, recommendations were based on a RH of 55%, but this was 
mainly based on the value being the midpoint value between the higher 
limit for avoiding mould at 70% and the lower limit for avoiding embrittle-
ment at 40%. There is no evidence that 55% is the optimal RH for many 
types of materials and, furthermore, strict limits on the ﬂ uctuations of 
+/- 4-5% has been based on limitations of the ventilation systems rather 
than knowledge of preservation (Erhardt, Tumosa and Mecklenburg, 
2007). Studies have shown that much larger deviations such as 35-60% 
RH could be used in many museums having 40% RH +/-5% in winter and 
55% RH +/-5% in the summer (Mecklenburg and Tomusa, 1999). 
The shrinking and swelling of wood will be aff ected by both temperatures 
and RH but according to The Smithsonian Center for Materials Research 
and Education (SCMRE) the latter is considered the most important. This 
is due to temperatures having a more even eff ect on the wood all the 
way though the material whereas the RH will aff ect the outer layer of 
wood ﬁ rst. The stress created in the wood due to the uneven humidity 
levels can then cause cracking. Painting or other surface treatments to 
the wood will then be damaged as well (Olstad and Haugen, 2007).
Care is also to be taken with micro climates near the artefacts. Behind 
e.g. paintings small air gaps can be found in which the micro climate diff er 
from the rest of the room. Due to the condensation on exterior walls 
the RH can thus reach 100% behind the painting. It is possible to have 
ﬂ uctuations from 30 to 100% in these air gaps while having the interior 
of a room at a constant 50% RH. Consequently, the stretcher expansion 
can cause cracking of the painting. At 80% RH and above, the paint is of 
risk of delamination of from the canvas due to the glue not working as a 
bond, the paint ﬁ lm becoming very ﬂ exible but weak and if being loose 
the canvas would be exposed to shrinking (Mecklenburg, 2007). 
==PAGE:21==
11
Furthermore, most insects rely on high relative humidity and high moisture 
contents in the materials to survive. Finally, eggs and young larvae of 
furniture beetles do not survive at RH below 65% (Child, 2007). However, 
some objects might have been exposed to a high RH for centuries and 
will be damaged by lowering the RH to the traditional guideline values 
(Padﬁ eld, 2013)
The governing body of culture in Denmark prescribes that the RH must 
be in the range of 40% to 60% but recommends a 45% to 55% range if 
possible. (Agency for Culture and Palaces a, 2016). The RH may never 
exceed 70% but certain metals, photographic materials etc. will need a 
lower RH, having recommended ranges as low as 15-30% (Agency for 
Culture and Palaces b, 2016). Fluctuations in RH is considered more 
harmful than temperature ﬂ uctuations. Therefore, the temperatures can 
be used as a mean for controlling the RH (Agency for Culture and Palaces 
a 2016). 
TEMPERATURE
Increasing the temperature will decrease the RH. Using heating systems 
in previously unheated buildings can, thus, cause damage to e.g. wooden 
artefacts if the lowering of RH is not taken into account (Olstad and 
Haugen, 2007). However, even when the inﬂ uence of temperatures on 
the RH is not regarded, the temperature is an important factor. Higher 
temperatures can accelerate chemical reactions leading to faster deteri-
oration (Hornbeck, 2009). 
The temperature also plays a large role in the control of insects. These 
will primarily live in the micro climates behind pictures, embedded in 
materials etc. Insects are cold blooded and are therefore often depend on 
external heat sources. Small insects have a large surface to body volume 
ratio and will have an increased dependency on the climate conditions. 
Most insects have optimal living conditions between 20 and 35 ºC with 
higher activity levels and reproduction levels with higher temperatures. 
However, at temperatures above 35 degrees for longer periods of time 
insects will often dry out. Temperatures below 15 ºC tend to limit ﬂ ying, 
mating and egg laying (Child, 2007). Below 10 ºC insect movements stop 
and they start to hibernate. Insects at all stages will die at temperatures 
below -10 ºC and this principal is therefore used for pest control. Other 
treatments such as insecticides are not eff ective below 20 ºC due to the 
lowering of activity and are therefore less likely to get in contact with 
the poison.
For many centuries paper has been the predominant media for storing 
knowledge, and several of medieval artefacts are proof of the durability 
of the media. However, during the 19th century there was an increased 
demand for paper and high quality materials were replaced by inferior 
(Balažic et al., 2007). The use of acid in paper for bleaching causes 
paper to degenerate rapidly. However, a decrease of temperature of 5 ºC 
increases the longevity of paper by a factor 2 (Balažic et al., 2007). As 
a result, temperatures are recommended to be in the range of 2-18 ºC 
(Agency for Culture and Palaces b, 2016 and ISO 11799, 2015).
Due to the comfort of the users the temperature levels are kept at the 
comfort range in the exhibitions at the expense of long term preserva-
tion (Mecklenburg & Tumosa, 1999). However, the prioritizing between 
comfort and preservation will diff er from museum to museum (Ry-
hl-Svendsen, appendix 02)
LIGHT
Since light on museum subjects are needed for the visitors to see the 
exhibition, light damage cannot be avoided. Daylight is the natural 
available light source and is emitted at a wide range of diff erent wave 
lengths. However, only radiation in the range between 380 to 780 nm 
can be seen and is, therefore, what is regarded as light. Below 380 nm 
it is considered UV and above 780 nm it is considered infrared light (IR) 
(Jacobs, 2004). Some harmful chemical reactions are in need of light 
and especially UV-light (Grzywacz, 2006). UV light cause fading and 
degradation of organic material but can be almost fully ﬁ ltered by glass 
panes. The long wave IR light cause heating which can result in damages 
by shrinking due to dehydration. With the use of coated glass, most of the 
==PAGE:22==
12
IR spectrum can be ﬁ ltered as well (Huber, 2007). The governing body of 
culture in Denmark has made speciﬁ c demands for maximal light (lux) and 
UVmax (μW/lm) in regards to diff erent exhibited materials (Agency for 
Culture and Palaces b 2016). It is however recommended that no UV light 
is present in exhibition areas and UVmax is therefore only to be used in 
cases where UV light can not be avoided.
The Canadian Conservation Institute has implemented some dynamic 
lighting guidelines in which it is allowed to have higher light levels for 
some time if the overall light exposure is compensated with time with 
lower light levels (Druzik and Eshøj, 2007) thereby it is e.g. allowed to 
increase light levels from 50 lux to 150 lux for an hour if the light is 
turned off  for the next 2 hours since the sum in both cases are 150 lxh. 
The Danish regulations can allow a similar approach since it is stated that 
“light dosage” can be used as an alternative method (Agency for Culture 
and Palaces a, 2016).
Whether to choose sunlight or electrical lights for museums depend 
on many aspects and preferences that aff ect both conservation of the 
exhibition as well as the energy consumption. The exhibition should be lit 
in order to be viewed by the public and daylight can be used by museums 
in order to lower their energy consumption (Schollmeyer, 2007). However, 
all light and especially daylight is harmful to most types of collections. 
Therefore, the solar gain must be controlled if natural light is used and 
the quality of light in the exhibit area is to be regarded (Bazianac and 
Winkelmann, 1989) 
POLLUTANTS
The pollutants can be in both solid, liquid and gaseous forms. It must 
be noted that pollutants that are harmful to people are not necessarily 
harmful to exhibited materials and vice versa. Many types of pollutants in 
the indoor air can cause harm to the exhibited objects. Some gases tend 
to form acid through chemical reactions and others, like ozone, causes 
oxidation. All of these chemical reactions happens faster when the 
temperature is higher. Some reactions like hydrolysis requires humidity, 
but all decay is a function of the quantity of pollutants in contact with 
the material (Ryhl-Svendsen, 2005). 
Particles are suspended in the air, but depending on the size they will 
deposit at diff erent times. Large particles will fall down much faster than 
small particles due to gravity (Ryhl-Svendsen, 2005). Larger particles 
(dust) can be visible when depositing on surfaces and cleaning can be 
time consuming as well as potentially dangerous for fragile artefacts 
(Lloyd et al., 2009).
Pollutants can come from the exterior air, like SO2 or from the interior 
like the compounds released by building materials, furnitures etc. 
These compounds can be divided into primary emission, i.e. drying of 
paint, and secondary emissions, i.e. material decomposition. Primary 
emissions will decrease relatively fast. Whereas secondary emissions 
will continue through the lifetime of the product. Carboxolitic acids are 
the main group of compounds causing deterioration and are released 
from paint glue etc. It can cause corrosion in metals like copper as well 
as in lead glass. Calcareous materials like shells and pottery can as well 
be corroded by carboxyl (Gryzwacz, 2006). 
There is a relation between the concentration of pollutants and the air 
exchange rate. Large spaces will have higher air exchange than micro 
environments and there is, therefore, higher concentration in display 
cases and storage cabinets than in the general storage area. The gallery 
spaces have the highest air exchange and, thus, the lowest concen-
tration of pollutants (Grzywacz, 2006). The governing body of culture 
in Denmark does not have limits for the amount of pollutants allowed 
in museums. However, as a museum space should be able to achieve 
category II indoor climate of max 500 ppm CO2 above outdoors (DS/EN 
15251, 2007), ventilation will be used for removing some pollutants. 
==PAGE:23==
13
GUIDELINES
Indoor climates for museums are not based on regulations but on 
guidelines, as the requirements will diff er based on the museum 
collection. The museum has the largest knowledge of the collection and 
have to pay for the instalment and running of the systems The require-
ments will therefore be set by the museum. (Ryhl-Svendsen, appendix 
02). The chosen set points values are a compromise of preservation and 
human comfort. The recommended climate for conservation of certain 
materials and the recommended climate for comfort have only very few 
overlaps as seen in Table 1 and Table 2. Some materials like fur require 
very strict indoor climate conditions whereas materials like stone has 
very low demands in all categories. Generally, the temperature range for 
conservation are below the temperature range for occupants. The Addi-
tionally, much higher light levels are recommended for occupants than 
for the museum collection. Working areas are required to have at least 
a 2% daylight factor, but this rule can be dispensed if compliance would 
be detrimental to the operation of the activity (Building Regulations 
b, 2016). However, for conservation of all materials low daylight is 
recommended. The daylight requirements for comfort and preservation 
are thus completely opposite. The RH set points for preventive conser-
vation is in this sense unproblematic, since most materials require RH 
ranges that comply with comfort needs.
Due to the lack of matching temperature and lighting ranges for comfort 
and conservation, the values will be a compromise. Certain museums will 
favour the requirements of the occupants, whereas other museums will

No single international guideline has been elevated to a status of being 
the offi  cial standard and thus many diff erent can be used (ICC, 2014) 
although many of the guidelines propose similar indoor climates (Table 3). 
If the guideline values should be set in accordance to the local climate in 
order to lower energy use is in an ongoing discussion in the conservation 
community (Atkinson, 2014).
CONCLUSION
The maximum allowed temperature of 25 ºC and a RH ranging from 
40-60% or 45-55% from the Agency for culture and Palaces complies 
with international guidelines (Table 3). 
Since many artefacts consist of many types of materials and that ma-
terials respond diff erently to RH, temperatures, light and pollutants the 
speciﬁ cation of appropriate levels of these factors are very complex. No 
indoor climate set points can be used for all museums and all collections. 
The values will thus have to be estimated in each case by the conserva-
tors but standard values should be usable in most cases. 
In the continued work of this thesis 40-60% RH and 20-25 ºC will be 
used as set points. It must, however, be noted that certain zones could 
be in need of either smaller bandwidths depending on the exhibition. If 
possible larger bandwiths could be used for at least part of the exhibition 
areas where no sensitive objects are placed in order to lower the energy 
use.
==PAGE:25==
15
05  Set points in practice
Based on information from museum personnel, engineers as well as 
articles a study on the actually used set points in museums was made. 
As to be seen the values diff er greatly due to the diff erent types of 
collections and diff erent types of museums. Some museums allow only 
for very small deviations in temperature and RH whereas other museums 
have no limits for indoor climate parameters. 
The energy consumption of a building is to a certain extend a result of 
the passive systems (insulation, windows, wall thickness etc.) and the 
active systems (ventilation, heating etc.). However, determining the set 
points for the indoor climate, and thereby the performance goals of the 
building, has a big inﬂ uence on the energy use. If no requirements for the 
indoor climate are set, then there is no need for any energy use of the 
systems. Furthermore, two identical museums with the same systems 
may have very diff erent energy consumptions if the requirements for the 
indoor climate a set diff erently. 
The 2015 building regulations requires an energy use of new buildings to 
be below 41,0 kWh/m2 per year not including electricity for appliances. By 
2018, this requirement is expected to be lowered to 25 kWh/m2 (Building 
Regulations, 2016). However, an annual energy use of 400-500 kWh/
m2 is common for museums even in moderate climates. Such a large 
energy use is naturally a large part of the museum budget, minimizing 
funds for other museum activities (Larsen & Padﬁ eld, 2011). Allowing for 
larger ﬂ uctuations in the indoor climate, great energy as well as ﬁ nancial 
savings could be made.
An example of this is Cisternene which has no insulation, temperature 
control, lighting or ventilation. No set points are set as the indoor climate 
is not actively controlled. Only a small local heater is sometimes placed 
next to the ticket sale stand in the small entrance building on the ground. 
The temperatures are passively controlled by the thermal mass of the 
concrete walls, ﬂ oors, ceiling and the adjacent layers of soil. The RH is 
constantly close to 100% and the humidity results in a deformation of 
the concrete and causes stalactites to be formed. There are no skylights 
and electrical lighting is usually only sparsely used in the installations. 
The low light levels, temperatures and high RH is therefore a deliberate 
part of the museum experience. The indoor climate limits the possible 
types of exhibitions but is also a part of what makes Cisternerne a unique 
museum. The museum is often used for special art installations in which 
artiﬁ cial lighting such as candles or lamps are an integrated part of the 
exhibition. 
The exhibitions will sometimes need some electricity but in general 
the energy use is extremely low. Based on the energy bills provided by 
Cisternerne for this thesis, it can be seen that the annual energy use 
was in the range of 2.4-2.9 kWh/m2 from 2012-2015. Thus, It is possible 
to achieve an energy use close to zero without using insulation or 
renewables. The goal is achieved by simply not having indoor climate 
requirements. However, this method does have some quite signiﬁ cant 
limitations. The museum has to be closed in the winter and the indoor 
climate would not be acceptable if visitors were to stay there for longer 
periods of time. But since there is typically only one large installation, 
visitors are not meant to be in Cisternerne for longer periods of time and 
the indoor climate conditions, like the lack of lighting, is often an essential 
part of the art work.
A similar approach has been used for Rudolf Tegner's Museum as it has 
no external energy supply. Thick concrete walls with no insulation keeps 
temperatures stable and suffi  cient lighting is provided only by a glass 
roof. A large ceiling height and natural ventilation is the only measures 
used to ensure suffi  cient air quality. The RH is high most of the year, but 
due to the robustness of the exhibition this not an issue. Like Cisternerne, 
the lack of systems limits the season as Rudolf Tegners Museum is only 
open in the summer (Larsen and Padﬁ eld, 2011).
If the requirements for the indoor climate can be relaxed in certain areas 
==PAGE:26==
16
of a museum building large energy savings could be made and it would 
lower the initial costs of system components due to downsizing. With 
the use of recent and more ﬂ exible guidelines, the cost of the construc-
tion of the Udvar-Hazy Center annex to the National Air and Space 
Museum of the Smithsonian Institution was reduced by approximately 
$10 million (10%). Furthermore, implementation of the new guidelines 
in the Smithsonian museums resulted in $2.7 million $ of energy savings 
for the second half of 2006 out of 32 million $ of energy costs for the 
whole of 2006 (Erhardt et al., 1999). It has been stated that: “Up to 50 
percent of construction costs for new museums and archival storage 
facilities may go toward highly overbuilt heating and cooling systems. 
Most museums can adequately protect their collections with commer-
cially available technology, such as the heating and cooling systems used 
in grocery or retail stores.” (Schultz, 1994). Similar tendecies could be 
Through design notes recieved by Rambøll it can be seen that the 
competition brief for the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark contained 
strict requirements for the indoor climate. The requirements were, 
however, not set according to the the needs of the collection. As a result, 
the set points for indoor climate was adjusted several times during the 
design. In the competition brief, the requirements were set to be 20 
ºC+/- 1 and 50 %RH +/- 3. However, later in the design it was determined 
that the indoor climate should only be set according to the comfort of 
visitors and the employees. Since the conservation of the exhibit should 
not be taken into account it was possible aim for 20 ºC–26 ºC and a RH of 
30%-70%. Today, there is no control of the RH in the museum and tem-
peratures are within the range of 20-24 ºC. The initial requirements were 
therefore signiﬁ cantly diff erent than what is used in the end. 
An example of having deliberately relaxed indoor climate requirements 
from the early stages of design in order to lower the energy use is the 
new Museum of Danish Resistance. In the competition brief (The National 
Museum of Denmark, 2014) the requirements for the indoor climate are 
set and the building is to live up to the requirements of energy class 
2020. The building is divided into two zones. Zone 1 being the service 
area is to live up to the standards of the building regulation concerning 
the employees. Zone 2 is the exhibition area and in this area large ﬂ uc-
tuations are allowed. The RH is allowed to be in the range of 30-70%. 
The temperature settings are relying on the ambient temperatures and 
are set to be min. 15 ºC in the winter and max. 25 ºC in the summer. The 
competition brief speciﬁ es that no mechanical cooling is to be used to 
keep the 25 ºC threshold. The broad range of temperatures and RH is, 
thus, used to get a low energy consumption. However, daylighting in 
Zone 2 was not preferable and in the assessment panel report the four 
teams not winning the competition received criticism for having allowed 
daylight to enter into the exhibition area. The argument for this critic 
was that the staging of a ﬂ exible exhibition room should be done by 
the exhibited artefacts and not by room itself (The National Museum of 
Denmark, 2015). By not using daylight, the total energy consumption 
could be higher, although it will lower the cooling demand. By specifying 
realistic values for the set points in the competition brief, the architects 
and engineers get a possibility of designing active and passive systems 
for the museum which match these requirements.
There is a great diff erence in the allowed temperature and RH ranges in 
Danish museums in practice, which are both much above and below the 
guidelines (Table 4). There is a tendency for art museums to have more 
strict requirements for the indoor climate than cultural- or natural history 
museums even though the exhibition might consist of the same type 
of materials. This could be due to art museums tending to exhibit more 
expensive artefacts than other museums (Ryhl-Svendsen, appendix 02). 
The art museum Louisiana has the set points closer than required in 
relation to the guideline values in order to make sure to stay within 
the limits. In order to ensure that the RH is always within 45-55% 
RH, the system is set to keep the RH between 47-53%. Likewise, the 
temperature is set with a very narrow dead band of 20-24 ºC with no 
seasonal ramping. If the system for some reason is not able to cope with 
very high loads, then the temperatures are allowed to be increased half a 
degree in order to keep the RH within the limits.
==PAGE:27==
17
Table 4.  Set points for indoor requirements in Danish museums buildings. 
Data from Liselund is based on Larsen and Padﬁ eld (2011) and data of the 
Museum of Danish Resistance is based on the competition brief. All other 
data is provided by the staff  of the museums or engineering companies. 
Museums not only contain historic artefacts but can be historic objects 
themselves. In old museum buildings such as Thorvaldsens Museum or 
the country house in Liselund, it is not possible to maintain a very stable 
indoor climate. Thorvaldsens museum cannot achieve yearly stable tem-
peratures but keep the RH very stable. The country house in Liselund has 
no heating but a dehumidiﬁ cation system is used to keep the RH at 60%. 
This rather limited modiﬁ cation of the indoor climate results in an energy 
use of 20 kWh/m3 per year (Larsen and Padﬁ eld, 2011).
One thing is to specify what to aim for, another is how to measure it. 
RH can be diffi  cult to measure precisely (Padﬁ eld, 2013). Even the best 
sensors might have a calibration error of around +/-1 % but are prone 
to much larger deviations if not calibrated properly (Schellenberg, 2013). 
In between these calibrations the accuracy will drift. Understanding the 
amount of drift is more important for making measurements than the 
initial precision (Appel, 2010). The measurements are also prone to errors 
caused by condensation in the instrument or contamination of chemicals, 
cleaners sponges etc. Finally, the measurement of RH, temperature 
etc. are aff ected by the position of the sensors in the room. By moving 
sensors in a room very, diff erent results can be obtained and deﬁ ne if 
the indoor climate values seem to be within the prescribed range (Kiær, 
appendix 04).
DISCUSSION
The general requirements for a museum are loosely deﬁ ned in the 
guidelines and will in practice diff er greatly depending on the type of 
collection, the type of building and the museum´s approach to indoor 
climate.
In theory, all buildings could have a very low energy use if all systems were 
turned off . But it would result in very poor indoor climates and is, thus, 
not advisable. It is, however, advisable to specify the set points based on 
a thorough analysis of the use of the building. If e.g. users are allowed to 
wear coats in the exhibition and/or if user are expected to have a higher 
than normal activity level the temperatures could be lowered. 
An integrated design approach including all actors is needed in order to 
establish the overall goals of design. This is needed in order to achieve 
the smallest energy use while still providing a suffi  cient indoor climate 
for both users and preservation. Knowing the goals early in the design 
process can result in much lower energy consumptions and costs as early 
optimization initiatives has much more impact than initiatives done near 
the end (Löhnert, Dalkowski and Sutter, 2003).
CONCLUSION
It is clear that the actual indoor climate at museums diff er from the 
guidelines due to the museums being very diff erent types of buildings 
with diff erent exhibitions. Therefore, some museums have very large 
energy consumption whereas others use very little energy. In the design 
of a new museum, care should be taken to determine the required indoor 
climate for the exhibition at an early stage in order to design the ideal 
active and passive systems.

The vapour pressure will consequently increase if the mass of vapour

The ventilation system is a mean of controlling the RH in the air. By 
supplying dry air to a humid room, the RH will decrease due to the mixing 
of the air. If humidity loads, such as humans, are present in a room, a RH 
of the supply air being lower than the RH of the room can be used to 
equalize the vapour production of the occupants. Such calculations can 
be made using the following formula:

06  Relative humidity
As people are a source of both vapour and heat, the number of visitors 
have a large inﬂ uence on the RH and total energy use of a museum. 
Studies on moisture accumulation indicated that cellular concrete could 
be used to lower the need for humidity control as long as the air change 
rate is low. The humidity calculations in IDA ICE was analysed and a wall 
component capable of moisture transport was implemented.
Controlling RH can be very energy consuming. Knowledge on the factors 
inﬂ uencing RH is, therefore, important as the control in reality can be 
very complex. The relative humidity is a term describing the ratio of the 
vapour pressure in relation to the saturated vapour pressure at a given 
temperature (Rode, 2016):
(1) 
where: 
The saturated vapour pressure indicates the maximum amount of vapour 
that can be stored in the air. If more vapour is present, it will condensate. 
The RH can consequently never be larger than 1. The saturated vapour 
pressure at any temperature can be found as:
(2)
where:
The RH will therefore decrease if the temperature is increased. The 
vapour pressure is related to the temperature and the mass of water 
==PAGE:29==
19
The humidity supplied or extracted by the ventilation system can, based 
on Eq. (4), be used to keep a stable RH. But many factors have an inﬂ uence 
on the RH of a museum space (Figure 13). Users will aff ect both the heat 
balance and the vapour balance. Evaporation and respiration will cause 
water vapour and the metabolic rate causes heat. Moisture buff ering 
in materials can stabilize the RH by adding or removing moisture from 
the room similar to thermal mass keeping temperatures stable. The 
temperature of the room is inﬂ uenced by many factors. The heat ﬂ ux to 
and from the building parts, the heating- and ventilation system and heat 
loads such as humans.
If the RH is outside the limits of the set points values, the ventilation 
system can be used to add or remove moisture. Ventilation can be both 
natural, mechanical or a combination. The type of system used will 
naturally have a large inﬂ uence on the energy use. Based on measure-
ments of the temperatures and the RH, the set points of the control 
system will adjust the ventilation and heating systems. 
The simpliﬁ ed balance (Figure 13) has two active systems (heating and 
ventilation) and three uncontrollable sources (heat transfer, moisture 
buff er and user behaviour). The heat transfer is depending on the passive 
systems as well as the internal and external climate conditions. 
Energy is only used by the active systems. In order to obtain a low energy 
use, the need for these systems will have to be as low as possible. By 
relaxing the set points, the systems will need to be activated less and 
the energy use will be lowered. This can off  course only be done if a more 
unstable indoor climate is not causing damage to the museum collection 
or discomfort for the occupants. The passive systems should be designed 
in such a way that the need for active systems is limited. A stable heat 
transfer will result in more stable temperatures and, in the end, a more 
stable RH. Similarly, a good moisture buff ering capability can minimize the 
need for ventilation to control the amount of vapour. As user behaviour is 
a large source of both heat and vapour, it will have a large eff ect on the 
total energy use. This factor is, however, the hardest one to control as

people enter museums when they want to and not necessarily when it

museums are therefore done (Section 08 and 07) in order to make more

Many museums do not allow visitors to bring jackets, trolleys, bags and 
similar objects into the exhibition areas partly due to theft. However, by 
prohibiting these potentially wet objects from the exhibition area, the 
humidity load can be decreased. To what extend wet clothes has an eff ect 
on the humidity loads has not been found in any studies. 
Standard values of evaporation by people are known for certain activities 
(Table 5). It can be seen that the loads diff er considerably depending on 
the type of activity done by the user. Of course these are only to be used 
to give an estimate as the actual amount of heat emitted is also depending 
on draught, temperatures, clo values etc.
HUMIDITY CONTROL 
The RH will usually be controlled by RH sensors placed either in the room 
or in the return air duct. Several methods can be used for humidifying and 
dehumidifying. There are three major types of dehumidiﬁ ers: desiccant 
dehumidiﬁ ers, spray dehumidiﬁ ers and refrigeration dehumidiﬁ ers.
The refrigeration dehumidiﬁ ers will dehumidify by cooling the air. The 
dehumidiﬁ cation is done by lowering the temperature of the air using 
a cooling coil until enough moisture is removed and then the air can 
be reheated using a reheating coil to avoid to low supply air. If used in 
combination with humidiﬁ cation, this system is called "constant-tempera-
ture, constant-humidity cycle" (ASHRAE, 2003).
Desiccant dehumidiﬁ ers use absorbing materials like silica gel to remove 
humidity from the air. Heat are then added in order to regenerate the 
absorption material (ASHRAE, 2003).
Spray coiled humidiﬁ ers works by ventilation air being passed through a 
chamber with water being sprayed. Dehumidiﬁ cation will occur when the 
temperature of the water is lower than the dew point of the air, whereas 
humidiﬁ cation will occur if the temperature is higher (Machinery Equipment 
Online, 2016). However, maintenance costs will usually make this system 
less desirable than the two previous mentioned systems (ASHRAE, 2003).
Several technologies can be used for adding humidity to the air including 
"evaporative" where dry air passes over a wet surface and make water 
evaporate (systemair, 2016). Steam humidiﬁ ers heat up water in order 
to produce steam and rotary humidiﬁ ers is based on a pad rotated from 
water to the inﬂ ow air in order to humidify (Brumbaugh, 2004). Ultrasonic 
humidiﬁ ers use a metal diaphragm vibrating at a high frequency and thus 
propelling moisture into the air (DAN FUGT, 2016). 
In addition to the internal humidity loads, there is also external loads to 
consider in order to supply air within the required boundaries. A visual tool 
for expressing the relation between RH, temperature and the speciﬁ c 
humidity is the Mollier diagram. This was used to study changes of the 
air through an Air Handling Unit (AHU) using the "constant-temperature, 
constant-humidity cycle". As an example of the diff erences in conditions, 
a summer and a winter case was made (Figure 15):
In the winter case (blue), the ambient air was -10 ºC and the RH was 
100% (1). When this air was heated up in the AHU, the temperature 
increased to 20 ºC and was blown into the exhibition space. If no humid-
iﬁ cation was made, the speciﬁ c humidity would remain the same but the 
RH would decrease to 11% (2). If the museum required at least 45% RH 
then humidiﬁ cation was needed. By applying 4.93 g/kg of vapour to the 
air, the RH was increased to 45% (3).

Figure 15.  Changes of temperature, Speciﬁ c humidity and RH of ambient 
air through the AHU if 20 ºC air and RH of 45-55% is needed. The red dots

In the summer case (red) where the ambient temperature was 28 ºC 
and the RH was 85%, then the speciﬁ c humidity was 0.020 kg/kg (1). 
When this air was cooled to 20 ºC then the RH was changed to 100% 
(2). However, since no more than 0.015 kg/kg of vapour can be stored 
at 20 ºC then 0.005kg/kg water would condensate. To get to a limit of 
55% RH at 20 ºC the air needed to be cooled even further to get more 
water to condensate and then be reheated to 20 ºC. Therefore, the air 
was cooled to 11 ºC to get the speciﬁ c humidity to be 0.008 kg/kg (3). 
This resulted in a RH of 55% when reheated to 20 ºC (4). To dry out the 
air is thus a very energy consuming process, since the air will be cooled 
down 17 ºC and then reheated 9 ºC, rather than just cooling down 7 ºC if 
the RH had been appropriate. Such a system will be more effi  cient if the 
reheating of air can partly be done by excess heat from the cooling coil, 
heat exchangers or similar methods for utilizing "free" heating.
MOISTURE BUFFERING
The sources of vapour in a museum space are typically the users and the 
supply air. The vapour is to be removed by the exhaust air, as no moisture 
sinks are typically present at museums (Padﬁ eld, appendix 03). However, 
some vapour might be absorbed or desorbed by the room or furniture 
surfaces. The amount of moisture in a room at any given point in time is 
a result of the balancing of the mentioned factors (Figure 14).
Typically, only the vapour production and ventilation is used in the calcu-
lations for indoor climate. But moisture buff ering could be used to lower 
the need for humidity control by removing the large peaks in vapour 
loads from the exhibition area. This principle is already used in storage 
facilities where e.g. large book collections will provide a large vapour 
buff er capability (Padﬁ eld and Larsen, 2004). Furthermore, studies have 
been done in which moisture accumulation in museum storages were 
used to keep the RH more stable (Padﬁ eld and Jensen, 2011). Moisture 
buff ering is also widely used in the exhibition areas, but in a smaller scale, 
as many displays contain a certain amount of silica gel in order to stabilize 
the RH. Display cases are very airtight with an Air Changes per day (ACD) 
typically in the range of 0.5 to 20 ACD (Keepsafe, 2016). Therefore, the 
peaks in humidity in the room will not cause large humidity ﬂ uctuations 
in the display cases. Furthermore, rather large amounts of silica gel is 
used as 20 kg/m3 is advised for display cases with an ACD below 1 (Yu, 
Klein and Reindl, 2001).
Supply
Absorbtion
Exhaust
Desorbtion
Production
Figure 14.  The vapour ﬂ ow in the exhibition space.
==PAGE:32==
22
However, at room scale not enough studies are made on this subject in 
order for companies to actively use moisture buff ering in the design of 
buildings. No commercial and certiﬁ ed products are available, as is needed 
in order be used in the industry (Ryhl-Svendsen, appendix 02).
As the ability of a material to absorb and desorb moisture is based on 
both the vapour permeability, density of material and the sorption curve, 
it can be diffi  cult to estimate a good buff er material. For this purpose, 
the Moisture Buff er Value MBV (kg/m2 %RH) was developed (Rode et al., 
2005). Several types of wood and concrete as well as gypsum and brick 
was tested. Besides spruce boards, cellular concrete provided the best 
buff ering material whereas regular concrete had the worst properties for 
moisture buff ering. Brick was also not very useful possibly due to the low 
moisture sorption (Masea, 2016).
A calculation based on the Eff ective Moisture Penetration Depth (EMPD) 
method was done in order to estimate the eff ect of three types of 
materials using Eq. (4) in combination with a moisture accumulation term 
(Rode, 2016):
(5) 
where:
The calculations were done based on a 1000 m2 room with 6 meter 
ceiling height with a varied number of occupants, diff erent Air Changes 
per Hour (ACH) and three diff erent materials (Figure 16). The initial RH 
of the room was 45% and the inﬂ ow air was 40%. Occupants were 
present for 8 hours and the museum was left empty for 6 hours with the 
ventilation running. The equivalent indoor climate category in regards 
to DS/EN 15251 (2007) was noted in order to estimate the ventilation 
needs based on CO2 concentration at steady state. 
High density concrete and oak had a very similar performance in all 
cases. Untreated wood is usually not used in museums due to unwanted 
de-gasiﬁ cation of compounds and will therefore need some type of 
surface treatment. This would lower the permeability and make the 
diff erence between wood and high density concrete even smaller. Cellular 
concrete did provide a smaller increase of RH especially at low ventilation 
rates. These diff erences were more distinct with larger humidity loads. 
However, by increasing the ACH, the inﬂ uence of humidity buff ering 
decreases. At an ACH of 1.2, there was very little diff erence in RH no 
matter the type of wall surfaces used. As a number of museums tend 
to have air change rates as high as 6-8 ACH (Valentín, 2007) moisture 
buff ering will have an little eff ect. But in situations where low ventilation 
rates can be used, then the surface materials could be of use in regards 
to moisture. However, it was evident that the storage areas of the 
museums are much better suited for beneﬁ ting of the buff ering capa-
bilities as the ACH are as low as 0.04 h-1 (Padﬁ eld et al., 2013). Since no 
humidity loads from visitors are present in storages, moisture buff ering 
will be more signiﬁ cant.
In museums, it is important to remove as much dust as possible to avoid 
particles to be settled on the collections. High quality ﬁ lters should 
therefore be used for the ventilation system, but thorough cleaning of the 
exhibition area is also crucial (Lord, Lord and Martin, 2012). The challenge 
when using cellular concrete or many other permeable materials is the 
accumulation of dust. If the surface is coated in order to be easily cleaned 
it could signiﬁ cantly decrease the moisture buff er capabilities. 
Therefore, due to the need for smooth and easily cleaned surfaces and 
large ACH, moisture buff ering does not have the same potential in the 
exhibition as in storage spaces. Below ground buildings will typically 
==PAGE:33==
23
have much less permeable outer walls resulting in the external vapour 
pressure not inﬂ uencing the internal vapour ﬂ ow. 
Low density cellular concrete did have the largest eff ect on the RH (Figure 
16). In the case with the worst indoor climate, buff ering had the largest 
eff ect. Higher ventilation rates compared to the internal loads resulted in 
the moisture buff ering having less of an impact. With a category II indoor 
climate, as could be used in most museums the, diff erence between using 
the best and the worst moisture buff er material was around 2%. At Cat 
I the diff erence between the materials was very small as the ventilation 
controlled the moisture content with a large ACH. In the total energy 
simulations in the end of this thesis, high density concrete was used as 
the default interior surface material but an energy simulation using low 
density concrete was also carried out in order study the potential for 
lowering the total energy use.

IDA ICE HUMIDITY
Since IDA ICE was used as the simulation tool for the energy use in this 
thesis, it is important to determine how the program calculates humidity. 
These types of calculations are done within the zone part of the math-
ematical model in which the code can be studied. Important parts of this 
code is discussed in this section but a more detailed description can be 
found in appendix 06.
In IDA ICE, the humidity load from users are calculated based on the 
activity level and the number of users present. The amount of occupants 
can vary over time based on a schedule. If the mathematical model is not 
changed, then the metabolic rate is set as one value for all users at all 
times. In a museum, the activity level is more or less the same at all times 
and an average value can be used. Depending on the type of museum, 
the expected metabolic rate of the users could diff er, but a value of 1, 
equivalent to walking 2.5 km/h, could be used in most exhibitions (ISO 
8996, 2004). The total heat load from an occupant is calculated as:
 
QOccMet[i] := nOccAct[i] * 1.8 * M[i] * 58  
Where nOccAct[i] is the number occupants present at a given time and
1.8 is the body area (m2). M[i] is the metabolic rate and 58 is value for 
converting 1 met to W/m2 (ISO 7330, 2006).
Since the body area is written in the IDA ICE code as a constant and not 
a changeable parameter, it not possible to change this value. According 
to ISO 8996 (2004), 1.8 m2 surface area corresponds to that of a 175 cm 
tall and 75 kg man. A 60 kg and 170 cm tall woman has and body area 
of 1.6 m2. According to the National Institute of Public Health (2007), an 
average Danish male is 83 kg and 177 cm and an average Danish woman 
is 68 kg and 167 cm. Thus,1.8 m2 is a fairly good estimate of the average 
area of an adult, if an evenly distributed amount of men and women are 
present. However, it is not an even distribution of men and women that 
visits museums and the value of 1.8 m2 does not take into account the 
large number of children being present at museums or the many museums 
in which mainly women tend to visit such as museums of art and cultural 
history (Jensen and Lundgaard, 2015). Danish museums have detailed 
information on the gender and age of the visitors due to the large yearly 
national user survey done by Gallup and a very large number of Danish 
museums (Jensen and Lundgaard, 2015). This data could be used in order 
to make estimations of the internal loads. As the body area is ﬁ xed in the 
code, the heat load could be adjusted by altering the metabolic rate.
Sweat is calculated as being the diff erence between the total heat load 
and the parts used for convective- radiant- and vapour loads. The vapour 
ﬂ ow from occupants is then calculated as:
 
 
VapFOcc2Zone := SUM i=1, nOp                       
QOccVap[i] + QSweat[i]                     
END_SUM / 2430000
The vapour ﬂ ow from occupants (kg/s) is calculated as the sum of the 
vapour load from occupants (W) and the sweat from occupants (W) 
divided by 2430000 (J/kg) being the latent heat evaporation of air (Genç 
and Porter, 2003). The vapour load is calculated based on the metabolic 
rate and vapour pressure.
HumFact * MassAir * XHumLoc’ = VapFOcc2Zone 
                       - SUM i=1, nLocUnit Wf[i] END_SUM 
                       + SUM i = 1, nUnit   
                       schedEquip[i] * (VapFSrcEquip[i] + EvapVapFSrcEquip[i])
                           END_SUM
                         + VapF_0 + SUM i=1, nTerminal VapF[i] END_SUM; 
The mass of the air (kg) times a factor that increases the humidity storage 
capacity of air (-) times the humidity in the zone (kg/kg) is equal to:
The vapour ﬂ ow from occupants (kg/s) and the sum of condensation from 
the AHU (kg/s) as well as the humidity load and evaporative humidity 
load from equipment times the equipment schedule (kg/s) +/- the vapour 
ﬂ ow from the terminals (kg/s).
