25
The humidity can therefore either be provided by the AHU, the occupants 
or the equipment and is to be removed by the AHU. Moisture accumula-
tion in building materials or in the inventory is not included in IDA ICE. 
The running values of VapFocc2Zone is not possible to be exported from 
IDA ICE. However, if the humidity load is needed QOCVAP and Qsweat 
can be used in combination with equation (7). By using this method on 
the standard building in IDA ICE consisting of one person of one met 
in a room, the humidity load over a year will be in the range of 47-80 
g/h. These corresponds well to the estimates from Table 5. If moisture 
from the clothes of users was to be included in the simulations, then the 
equipment schedule could be used for adding additional moisture loads.
However, this method would not be very suitable for moisture accumu-
lation in the surfaces as the moisture ﬂ ow in and out of the wall is not 
a parameter possible to predeﬁ ne before the simulation. It is a result of 
pressure diff erences and will thus change depending on the results of 
the previous steps in a simulation. However, a wall model to include in the 
mathematical part of the IDA ICE was developed by Kurnitski and Vuolle 
(2000). This model has been further been developed by Patrik Skogqvist 
based on work by Kûnzel (1995). This work resulted in the HMwall in 2013. 
This wall component is not a part of the IDA ICE but has been provided 
by Voulle and Skogqvist to be used for this project. In this thesis, the wall 
has been tested and a guide for using it can be found in Appendix 14. 
The HMwall deﬁ nes the sorption curve based on the water content in the 
material at 80 and 100% RH and the diff usion resistances are calculated 
based on the dry- and wet cup vapour diff usion resistances.
As the wall can only be used in the advanced level of IDA ICE and is 
to be connected to a large number of parameters, it is currently quite 
time consuming to use. Furthermore, all variables have to be speciﬁ ed 
as single- or vector values for each parameter and each element. It is 
therefore not possible to just choose a material and then have the 
program automatically derive the values based on that choice as walls 
in the standard level of IDA ICE. The very large amounts of manual work 
related to the use of the HMwall did cause a potential risk of human error 
resulting in false simulation results or numerical errors.
To use the HMwall in the current version, where it can only be used 
in the advanced mode, is limiting the use as even very simple models 
are rarely made correct in the ﬁ rst try. To do very complex geometries 
would therefore be very challenging. However, as it should be possible to 
integrate it in the standard mode of IDA ICE in the future, the HMwall has 
a large potential. By accounting for moisture accumulation in walls, IDA 
ICE can be used for better simulations of e.g. museum galleries, storages, 
laboratories, hospitals or other building types where the RH is of great 
importance.
DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
Even though the concept of RH is relatively simple, calculating and 
controlling the RH can be complex due to both vapour and heat balances 
having an inﬂ uence. The steady temperatures of underground buildings 
can be utilized to keep a more steady temperature in the room and 
thereby steadier control the RH.
Moisture buff ering only had a limited eff ect on the RH in the calculation 
as the ventilation removed much vapour. The results of such calculations 
would of course diff er based on how dry the inﬂ ow air was, the room 
volume etc. But there is a potential for humidity buff ering lowering peak 
vapour loads in museums. For a moisture buff er cleaning and certiﬁ cation 
is needed in order to be used in practice. However, small scale humidity 
buff ering in display cases is a moisture buff ering technique which can 
potentially reduce the need for RH control of the AHU. It is easy to use 
in an exhibition and can be removed or altered easily. It is thereby a much 
more ﬂ exible moisture buff er than having the moisture buff er materials 
on the wall surfaces.
The use of the HMwall adds the possibility to make more detailed 
simulations and was therefore used for all IDA ICE simulations in this 
thesis.
==PAGE:36==
26
07  Visitors
The number of visitors at any given time in a museum will have a large 
inﬂ uence on the internal loads of a museum. Entrance data from 23 
museums wss used to study the user presence. Based on a series of 
studies was can be concluded that the number of visitors were aff ected 
by many factors such as day of week, weather, size of the museum etc.  
Humans are a signiﬁ cant source of heat and humidity in buildings. 
Furthermore, most climate systems are designed in order to ensure the 
comfort of the occupants. Thus, many systems can be turned off  or set 
to minimum capacity when no people are present and many visitors 
will result in a larger load on the systems. The usage pattern of the 
visitors is therefore of great importance for estimating the energy use 
of museums.
It is much more complex to determine how many and when visitors are 
present in a museum than estimating how and when people are present in 
dwellings or offi  ces. In offi  ces an average workday can be estimated and 
it can be safe to assume that the amount of people in the building rarely 
surpasses the number of employees. Dwellings are also designed for a 
certain number of people and will only surpass that at social gatherings 
such as birthdays. 
Museums however, are not designed for a speciﬁ c number of people. 
Unlike offi  ces where most are paid to show up and dwellings where most 
people will return every night, people will visit the museums when they 
want to within the opening hours. The diff erence between wanting to 
be in a building and needing to be in a building makes the user presence 
much more diffi  cult to predict. 
In offi  ces most employees will be at their desk or in meeting rooms during 
all working hours except at lunchtime. Whereas visitors at a museum can 
make very short or long visit or everything in between. Furthermore, it 
can be diffi  cult to predict where the users are situated during a visit. 
Diff erent users might prefer to spend more time in certain parts of the 
museum. At large museums it is not possible to see all of the exhibition in 
a single day and which forces the visitors to choose to only visit certain 
parts of the exhibition.
Standard schedules for user presence in many building types are 
developed for ASHRAE (Building Energy Codes Program, 2016) but event 
spaces such as museums with a more dynamic presence are notably not 
included.
Many factors can inﬂ uence the number of visitors including exhibition 
type, location of museum, holidays, weekday, commercials, weather etc. 
A research of the user patterns in a selection of Danish museums will 
form a basis for evaluating the nature of visitor presence in museums 
well knowing that this study will not give deﬁ nitive answers.
23 Danish museums provided visitor data at four diff erent levels of 
reﬁ nement. Daily, weekly, monthly or yearly. Unfortunately, no museums 
were able to document the entrance times of visitors at smaller time 
intervals, meaning that the daily entrance patterns cannot be determined 
based on available data.  The National Museum of Denmark is currently 
installing a system for more detailed logging of visitors, but unfortunate-
ly the system was not ready in time for this thesis.
When a public building is googled then a presence graph shows up next to 
the search results to make it possible to avoid the crowded times (Figure 
17). However, this feature is based on when people have “chosen to 
store their location information on Google servers” (Google, 2016). Only 
a certain segment of the visitors will leave an online trace of their visits 
and it is uncertain exactly how these data were derived. Furthermore, 
it is not possible to extract the data behind the graphs numerically and 
must, therefore, be estimated based on the graph. Additionally, it is not 
possible to detect the duration of the user's visit at the museum with 
this method. Therefore, the data is not regarded good enough to be the

base of further calculations without the collaboration of google. It is 
however, clear that a collaboration with big data companies like Google 
would provide a good insight on occupancy in museums as well as other 
building types. However, without the help of such a company, it is hard to 
ﬁ gure out how this data was collected and how it should be interpreted. 
As a consequence, another method for the actual presence of users of 
museums per day was made (section 08).
Many museums provided data about their yearly number of visitors to 
Statistics Denmark. In 2014, being the latest statement available at the 
time of this thesis, 410 museums provided data (Statistics Denmark, 
2016). By making an accumulated graph of this data it can be seen that 
the accumulation is an exponential function (Figure 18). The top two 
museums both had around 600,000 visitors, 92% off  all the museums 
had below 100,000 yearly visitors. The data is based on individual 
museums and will thus not sum up data from museums under the same 
organization. The National Museum is for instance in charge of a total 
of 13 Danish museums providing data. All together these museums had 
1,153,302 visits, whereas the National Museum accounted for 591,384 
of the visits. This makes The National Museum the second most visited 
museum in Denmark in 2014, only surpassed by the 647,857 visits to 
the art museum Louisiana.
In order to make a more detailed analysis of visitor behaviour, data from 
23 museums was analysed. Two diff erent analysis was carried out. One 
set was done on visitor data measured on a daily basis and a second 
analysis was done on data where the user presence was logged at a 
monthly basis.

The analysis using daily measurements was done based data from 
Cisternerne, Louisiana, M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark, Zoological 
Museum, Geological Museum and the Viking Ship Museum. Then the 
analysis on monthly and yearly behaviour patterns was done based on 
data from Thorvaldsen's Museum, The Natural History Museum of Aarhus,
 Little Mill, The Museum of Danish Resistance, The Royal Danish Naval 
Museum, The Open Air Museum, Brede Works, The Danish Music Museum, 
Trelleborg, The Royal Danish Arsenal Museum, The Victorian Home, 
Lützhøfts Old Grocer’ Shop, Museum of Tools, Lejre Museum and Roskilde 
Museum.
METHOD
As no speciﬁ c data about user presence in museums were available, the 
analysis were based on data from the number of entrances at museums. 
Since museums vary much in number of visitors it can be diffi  cult to 
detect any tendencies if the amount of visitors during a year is compared 
between diff erent museums. Furthermore, the number of visitors can 
diff er much from year to year at certain museums as to be seen in the 
following data. Thus, a method had to be developed in order to compare 
tendencies between museums and to be able to investigate the impact 
of certain days or months while ﬁ ltering out the inﬂ uence of the year 
and the size of the museum. To make this analysis, the visitor ratio was 
developed to be able to make a investigation of either daily, monthly or 
yearly visitor patterns. 
The daily visitor ratio is deﬁ ned as:
(9) 
where
The average Vr,day for a given year will, thus, always be 1. Values above 1 
is due to a larger amount of visitors the given day than the average daily 
amount of visitors the given year.
The Vr,month is similar to the daily Vr,day but is based on monthly registra-
tions of visitors rather than daily. The monthly visitor ratio is deﬁ ned as:
(10) 
where
In order to compare the yearly number of visitors between diff erent 
museums and between years the Vr,year was calculated using the total 
numbers of visits in 2014 as the index:
(11) 
Since the museums have diff erent number of yearly visits the Vr,year can 
be used to evaluate a growing and a decreasing tendency of visitors .
Seven museums provided data regarding number of visitors on a daily 
level. This data was used for a number of studies:
1) Vr,day was plotted for all the years to investigate the yearly pattern. 
Furthermore, large spikes in the plot were investigated to ﬁ nd out if any 
special occasions caused the large deviation from normal.
2) The Vr,day was summed up for each day of the week for each year. This 
was done in order to determine if any weekly visitor presence pattern is 
showing when a large number of days are investigated and to test if this 
pattern is similar for the diff erent years.
==PAGE:39==
29
3) Correlations between Vr,day and daily weather data of rain, temperature 
and sun hours were made during the three summer months. Thereby, it 
could be determined if bad weather in the summer will cause a tendency 
for more visitors to museums. Then, the correlation was found between 
the amount of visitors and all three types of weather data as well as the 
seven days of the week. Thereby, it was possible to estimate how much 
these parameters have an inﬂ uence of the running of a museum.
A linear correlation between the daily number of visitors and weather 
data was found to be ﬁ tted with the following formula:
(12)
 where:
This was found for rain (mm), temperatures (ºC) and sun hours (h). As data 
with daily measurements was received by 7 museums and a total of 14 
years, 42 simple correlation models was made.
Then a model was made for the correlation between the number of 
visitors and rain, temperatures, sun hours and days of the week (mon-sun). 
The data was ﬁ tted to the following model:
(13)
where:
This resulted in 14 models depending on 10 parameters each.
4) Vr,month was used to study monthly patterns at to study the development 
over a longer period of time
For all museums the Vr,year was calculated. This was used to determine if 
the total number of visitors were increasing or decreasing. The museums 
providing monthly data did typically provide data for more years than 
the museums providing daily data. The monthly data did therefore give 
a information of long term tendencies of up to 15 years whereas the 
maximum years with daily measurements was 3. As only one museum 
provided data with weekly measurements, this data was summed up and 
plotted as monthly data in order to compare with other museums.
It must be noted that the visitors numbers at the M/S Maritime Museum 
of Denmark was based on the ticket sales and not actual measures of 
visitors (as to be described in section 08) and is therefore less precise 
than the other datasets. 
==PAGE:40==

RESULTS
1) Visitor presence on a daily basis
As the 7 museums with daily measurements had only 1-3 years of data 
available, it is not possible to determine if the number of visitors are 
growing or decreasing in the long run. But, it can be concluded that the 
total amount of visitors per year in these 2-3 years is not stable (Figure 
19). From 2013 to 2014 the National Museum of Denmark went from 
1.22 to 1 and the Zoological museum went from 0.77 to 1. It was, thus, 
important to ﬁ lter out these yearly variations in the sum of visitors in 
order to be able to detect the visitor patterns on a monthly and a daily 
basis. As only one year of data was available for Cisternere no yearly 
change could be calculated and it was, thus, not included in the ﬁ gure.
Closing days are included in the graphs as values of zero (Figure 20- 
Figure 26). It can be seen that in general the visitor presence is a highly 
ﬂ uctuating factor with occasional very large peaks. Only 2-3 years of 
data was available, but in this short time period  peaks do not seem to 
occur at the same date every year. 
By researching the dates with very large peaks of it could be determined 
that these extreme cases were caused primarily by certain events at 
the museums. The Viking Ship Museum the 8th of August had a visitor 
ratio of 10.1 (Figure 23). On that day the museum was the start and 
stop of a professional bike race as well as for a ﬁ tness run for amateurs 
(Johannesen, 2014).
The National Museum of Denmark had a Vr,day of 6.4 for two days in a row 
during 17th-18th August 2013 (Figure 21). This happened during a two 
day viking market at the museum (The National Museum of Denmark, 
2013). 
The Geological Museum had a visitor ratio of 40.0 the 10th of October 
2013 during the Copenhagen Culture Night (Figure 26). The company 
10TONS exhibited life-like giant prehistoric reptiles. (Parkmuseerne, 
2014) Moreover, rather large visitor ratios of 8,8 and 6.5 occurred the 27th 
and 10th of March 2014. It has not been possible to ﬁ nd any particular 
events occurring on these dates. However, since the Geological Museum 
has provided quite detailed data, it can be seen that during these two 
speciﬁ c dates 97% of the visitors were primary and secondary school 
students. It is reasonable to assume that speciﬁ c events for students 
were happening these two dates.
The plot of Cisternerne has two noticeable outliers (Figure 25). The ﬁ rst 
is a Vr of 14.1 as a result of the Copenhagen Culture night. The second 
outlier was based caused by a large number of students or pensioners. It 
is known that this price group caused the peak based on knowledge from 
the ticket sales. However, no records could be found of any happening 
on this date but it must be assumed that an event of some kind occurred 
since the large increase of visitors was due to only one visitor category 
while the remaining categories were similar to most other days.

2 Weekly visitor patterns
When the Vr,day is summed up for each weekday during a year, very distinct 
patterns emerge. For all the museums except the Geological Museum, 
the curves of the 2-3 years look very similar. It must however be noted 
that the Geological Museum had noticeably less visitors than the other 
5 museums. Most museums tend to have closed on Mondays. However, 
none of the museums in this research had zero visitors during all Mondays 
in a year. This was due to either having the museum opened on Mondays 
in holidays, the summer or during special events.
The two large museums, Louisiana and the National Museum of Denmark, 
had similar patterns with a Vr,day of around 1 from Tuesday to Thursday 
and then increasing number of visitors until it tops at around 1.4 on 
Sundays. The plots of the Zoological Museum and the M/S Maritime 
Museum of Denmark did also look very similar. From Tuesday to Friday 
the number of visitors decreased slightly and peaked during the weekend 
with Saturdays being signiﬁ cantly busier than Sundays.

3 Correlation between visitors and weather
Many factors can inﬂ uence whether or not people choose to go to 
museums. Bad weather in the summer are by many believed to result in 
more visitors at museums (Hjortdal and Nepper-Rasmussen, 2015 and 
Hansen, 2015). To test whether this assumption holds, the correlation 
between weather data and the amount of visitors during the summer 
months of June, July and August were calculated.
The test was done based on three types of weather data: amount of 
rain, temperature and sun hours. If bad weather will cause less visitors, 
Table 6.  Correlation bewteen daily amount of visitors and daily weather data for rain, temperatures and sun hours. Signiﬁ cant values are marked with white
The Viking Ship Museum is the only one of the museums regularly opened 
on Mondays and the only one with a very ﬂ at visitor pattern. 
Besides having very few visitors during Mondays due to often being 
closed, very little tendencies can be spotted during the two years of 
data from the Geological Museum. 2015 looks more like the curves of 
the other museums, whereas 2014 is very much unique especially since 
the largest number was present on Fridays. This was partly caused by 
the large spike caused by the Culture Night Friday the 10th of October 
(Figure 26).

then the amount of visitors at the museums should increase with lower 
temperatures, more rain and/or fewer sun hours. Thus the correlation 
between visitors and temperature was expected to be negative, between 
visitors and rain to be positive and between visitors and sun hours to be 
negative.
The weather data for temperatures and rain was derived from the weather 
station at Kastrup Airport and Rosklide Airport (Weather Underground 
a and b, 2016) and sun hours was derived from Danish Meteorological 
Institute (2016). The formula for the models is:
The correlations are generally small (Table 8). The largest numerical value 
of 0.352 was found when comparing amount of visitors at the Zoological 
Museum with the amount of sun hours for 2014.
According to Evans (1996) a correlation of .00-.19 is “very weak”, 0.20-.39 
“weak” 0.40-.59 “moderate”, 0.60-.79 “strong” and 0.80-1.0 “very 
strong”. According to this scale none of the calculated correlations can 
be categorized as more than "weak". However, data was also tested with 
a two sided Student's t-test to evaluate if the correlations were statis-
tically signiﬁ cant. Only 9 out the 42 correlation proved to be signiﬁ cant. 
For the Zoological Museum 6 of the correlations was signiﬁ cant and was, 
==PAGE:46==
36
thus, the museum with the strongest relation between weather and 
number of visitors.
The Viking Ship Museum had a signiﬁ cant correlation between visitors 
and temperature for both years. The correlation was, however, positive, 
meaning more people tend to visit the museum during warm weather 
than cold. It must be noted that the museum is placed in the Roskilde 
marina and displays a range of outdoor activities during the summer 
months such as ship building and a blacksmith (The Viking Ship Museum, 
2016). Some museum like the Geological Museum had very low correlation 
values and all were positive even though the correlation between visitors 
and temperature and sun was expected to be negative. 
The Viking Ship Museum is the only one of the museums correlated to 
weather data from Roskilde Airport. The correlation was strongest using 
temperature data. for 2015. The correlation between number of visitors 
and temperatures was a bit too weak to be signiﬁ cant according to the 
students t-test. It is noteworthy, that the correlation was positive for 
temperatures for both years.
.
4 Correlation with several factors
The ﬁ rst correlation study was based on only one weather factor at the 
time. This was evolved by calculating the combined correlation of several 
factors. Using the same data, a study was made on the correlation 
between visitors and temperatures, rain, sun hours and the seven 
weekdays for each year. This resulted in 14 correlations as seen in Table 
7.
Louisiana is the museum with the user presence best described by these 
parameters as the adjusted R2 is 0.627 and 0.584. The National Museum 
of Denmark and Cisternerne also had values around 0.5 depending on the 
year. The lowest value of 0.063 were found for the Geological museum 
of 2014. However, the following year the value of 2015 was 0.24 being 
much larger.
Table 7.  The adjusted R2 of models based on rain, temperatures, sun hours 
and weekday for 7 diff erent museums
The Viking Ship Museum did generally not have a very good ﬁ t since the 
values for both years were low. The M/S Maritime Museum did also have 
relatively small values for both years.
It is thus clear that the user behaviour at certain museums had a much 
stronger correlation with these 10 parameters than other museums. 
Further values from the study such as estimate coeffi  cients, p-values are 
found in appendix 07.
Visitor presence on a monthly basis
A much larger number of museums were able to provide data with 
ﬁ gures regarding monthly number of visitors. Unlike the study based on 
daily measurements, the data stretches several. 2014 was used as the 
reference year in order to calculate Vr,year. Smaller museums tend to have 
larger yearly diff erences and more outliers. (Figure 34-Figure 36). By 
looking at the larger museums (Figure 34) it can be seen that there has 
been an increase in the number of visitors in comparison to 2001. Three 
museums provided data from 2002 and the Vr,yearly for the museums 
in 2002 was around 0.5 for the three museums. The total amount of 
visitors did thus double from 2002 to 2014. 
In museums with 5,000-50,000 yearly visitors only 5-6 years of data 
was available. In this period, no tendency was found as several values 
both above and below 1 is to be seen on both sides of the reference

42
It is evident that the diff erent museums had very diff erent visitor 
patterns. However, many museums had similar patterns when comparing 
the diff erent years for the same museums. This tendency was, however, 
less showing for the small museums. The two smallest museums, Brede 
Works and Little Mill, had highly ﬂ uctuating patterns. Neither of the 
museums have totally random patterns as peaks at around August was 
showing for both museums. But the patterns change much from year to 
year.
In general, small museums had larger deviations of the Vr values than 
larger museums. The largest value of 6.5 was found at Trelleborg being 
the third smallest museum in the study.
Many museums had peaks at the summer holiday in July and the Autumn 
holiday in October. At the Royal Danish Arsenal Museum values of above 
3 is seen for all three years of data. It must be noted that the Royal 
Danish Arsenal Museum has a tradition of hosting a large medieval knight 
tournament during the Autumn holiday (Thorsen, 2008).
 
DISCUSSION
Patterns
Even though some students might be forced to go to museums, a 
majority of the museum visitors are present because they want to be 
there. The presence is therefore often based on the visitor's wish to be 
at a certain place rather than the need to be there. This makes the user 
pattern much more ﬂ uctuating and more diffi  cult to predict. The plots of 
daily measurement of visitors were highly ﬂ uctuating and seemed to lack 
a pattern. However, based on this study, it is evident that even though 
the behaviour seen in a short timespan seems random it is often caused 
by certain factors if a large amount visits are analysed. Events proved to 
be the cause of large number of visitors at certain dates. Especially for 
many larger museums, a seasonal pattern was showing when studying 
the monthly summations. 
As seen in the graphs, Vr ,day tend to be of larger values than Vr,month. This 
was caused by averages of certain time steps removing peaks in data. If 
data at an hourly basis was available, then even larger peaks would be 
expected.
Even when the large events were not ﬁ ltered out and the monthly 
patterns were not taken into consideration, a model based on weather 
data and weekdays had a correlation with the visitor number in many of 
the museums investigated. For further research a model taken events 
into account as well as the monthly variations could be developed. 
Furthermore, user groups such as students are not inﬂ uenced by the 
same parameters since they often have scheduled visits. Data on these 
user types are stored by some of the museums included in this study. 
Accounting for user groups with diff erent behaviour could as well be in-
corporated in the model. 
The Geological Museum did not ﬁ t very well into a predictable pattern. 
The very large peak of 40.5 Vr did have an impact. However, based on data 
from Statistics Denmark,36% of the visitors at the Geological Museum 
were students whereas Louisiana only had 10% students. As student 
presence are based on school hours rather than the student's preference, 
this will cause some error in the prediction modelling. However, the 
Zoological Museum had 40% students and had a much more predictable 
pattern. The number of students was, thus, not enough to explain the 
presence pattern. What can have a signiﬁ cant inﬂ uence is the fact that 
the Zoological Museum had 5 times as many visitors during a year than 
the Geological Museum. But it is not possible to determine just one or 
two factors that can explain the behaviour at certain museums as it is a 
complex matter.
Data from larger museums often displayed a stable pattern when looking 
at data for the whole year. This did not happen to the same extend when 
looking at data from smaller museums. A reason for this is the law of large 
numbers. Having fewer visitors will cause less chance of the expected 
average to occur. Data from smaller museums did ﬂ uctuate much more 
==PAGE:53==
43
than the larger museums. This was caused by a group of people having 
a bigger impact on the total amount of people in a small museum than a 
big museum. Since the group sizes to a certain extend is not related to 
the size of the museum, large ﬂ uctuations will occur. For instance, a class 
of students will be of the same size no matter the type of museum. When 
dimensioning climate systems for smaller museums, it is, thus, important 
to take into account the fact that the actual number of visitors will often 
be much higher or lower than the average number of visitors.
Data
Unfortunately, it was not possible to get data from museums where 
smaller time intervals than days were registered. The National Museum of 
Denmark is currently installing a new system for counting visitors. When 
it is running, this data could be used for further research in this ﬁ eld. 
Beyond the National Museum of Denmark, at least one Danish museum 
has such a system installed at present but were unable to provide data 
for this study. Fortunately, the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark allowed 
for a study of the users as to be seen in the following section.
In general, acquiring data from the museums has been a challenge. The 
reasons for this are many but sometimes the museum had a policy that 
they couldn't provide data to external sources. Often data was received 
only after a very long correspondence. Several museums were not sure 
about which employee had access to such information and several 
employees had to be contacted. Certain employees responded that they 
were not allowed to use extra time on such tasks. It must be noted that 
many museums are under a large pressure due to the large ﬁ nancial 
cuts enacted by the government on museums and theatres (Højsgaard, 
2016). Fear of theft, terrorism and similar threats could also be a reasons 
for museums not being able to participate. 
A large number of museums delivered data for this study. However, the 
data was often in diff erent formats. Therefore, eff ective data handling 
has been a challenge during this study and will be a continued challenge 
if further searching this ﬁ eld is to be done. Since this data is measured by 
the museums, the sources of error are unknown and might diff er for the 
diff erent museums. However, since the number of visitors are important 
to all museums, it must be excepted that the values are very close to the 
actual number.
It must be noted that this type of entrance data does not specify how 
many people are present at a museum at a given time. It is only possible 
to determine when people enter the museum but not how long they tend 
to stay there. This is of course important since one visitor at a museum 
for 6 hours will cause the same total heat load as 12 visitors visiting for 
half an hour. Furthermore, this study does not provide data on where the 
visitors are situated. Visitors will rarely be evenly distributed throughout 
the museum and, thus, certain areas will be more crowded than others. 
Impact of visitors on the active systems
Research on user patterns can be used for dimensioning new systems 
for either an existing or new museum. When designing system for an 
existing museum or an extension to an existing museum, visitor data 
should be studied. Since the pattern, especially for larger museums, tend 
to be stable it should be taken into account. If strong weekly patterns are 
showing, as was the case in all but one museum in this study, weekends 
should be used for dimensioning the ventilation, cooling and dehumidiﬁ -
cation systems as the Vr,day were typically 1.4-1.6 in the weekend. 
Monthly patterns should as well be taken into account. Few visitors 
during winter months will result in smaller internal heat loads and more 
heating will be needed. Furthermore, many visitors during the summer 
will result in a larger need for cooling, ventilation and dehumidiﬁ cation. 
Additionally, when the visitor number correlates with weather conditions 
this will have an impact on the energy use. For some museums it could be 
seen that more people choose to visit museums during a rainy summer 
day. This results in a larger need for dehumidiﬁ cation. Even though many 
museums require that bags, coats and trolleys are left in the wardrobe, 
rain is still kept in pants, hair, hats etc. of the visitors. Thus, rainy days will 
==PAGE:54==
44
cause more people to visit and make them evaporate more water vapour. 
The dehumidiﬁ cation system should be able to cope with these regular 
spikes in humidity loads and would have to have a larger capacity. No 
research was found on the exact amount of humidity evaporating from 
wet pants, shoes and similar clothing and could be   the focus of further 
research.
Some museums had more visitors during sunny and warm days, others 
had less and for some the weather did not have a large impact. When 
designing systems for a museum it should be noted how visitors respond 
to sunny weather. As in the case of the Viking Ship Museum the internal 
heat and humidity loads are the largest at the same time as the external 
loads being large. This will of course put extra stress on the system's 
ability to contain the humidity and temperature levels. 
Due to the highly ﬂ uctuating pattern of visitors on a daily basis it will 
not be possible to design systems always keeping the humidity and 
temperatures in between tight thresholds. Doing so would cause over 
dimensioned systems most of the year, being both very expensive and 
space consuming. But as the very large peaks in Vr,day are caused by 
scheduled events by the museum, it is possible to take precautions. 
Additional dehumidiﬁ cation devices, increased air change with the use of 
natural ventilation, pre-cooled rooms etc. are some of the methods that 
could be used in order to account for the larger amount of humidity and 
heat introduced by many visitors. Some of these scheduled events, like 
the knight tournament at the Royal Danish Arsenal Museum, are partly 
taking place outdoors which decreases the loads. 
Future pattern of the National History Museum
As the new National History Museum of Denmark is expected to have 
around 400,000 yearly visitors the visitor pattern should be much 
diff erent and with smaller ﬂ uctuations than the patterns of the smaller 
Zoological museum and Geological museum. It would be reasonable to 
assume that the daily pattern will be more similar to the pattern of the 
National Museum of Denmark 
CONCLUSION
No matter the precautions, it will not always be possible to keep the 
indoor climate in museums within the set points for the active systems. 
Especially at smaller museums the people load is highly ﬂ uctuating and 
will at times be larger than what can be coped with by the systems. But 
by knowing and understanding the principles of the visitor patterns and 
the preservative challenges of the museum collection, better systems 
can be designed. As seen in the above cases visitor patterns are not 
completely unpredictable and entrances are one of the ways to study 
these patterns.
Vr,day values will result in larger values and more ﬂ uctuations than Vr,month. 
Vr,day is therefore preferable in order to make energy simulations. For the 
simulations in this thesis the Vrday patterns will be used for making more 
realistic simulations than having a steady visitor presence from opening 
to closing time. The pattern from the National Museum of Denmark will 
be used as the default pattern, but other types will be used in order to 
evaluate the inﬂ uence on the overall energy use.
Generally, few museums had very detailed measurements of visitors. It 
was not possible to study the daily patterns due to a lack of good data. 
In order to gain further knowledge of this subject a study was done at 
the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark as seen in the following section.
==PAGE:55==
45
08  Visitor presence 
AT M/S MARITIME MUSEUM OF DENMARK
No data provided by museums were logged at smaller time intervals than 
single days. Thus, in order to estimate the user patterns at museums 
during a day a study was done at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark. 
The research was based on one month's surveillance videos at 5 diff erent 
places in the museum as well as the sales ﬁ gures from ticket sales. Based 
on this data it was possible to detect the variety in people densities and 
user patterns in diff erent types of areas. The cafe and entrance area had 
much higher people densities than the exhibition. However, for all areas 
weekends were the busiest times.
No museums were able to provide data describing the daily distribution of 
visitors in a museum. In order to make realistic user pattern schedules for 
energy simulation such data is needed. Furthermore, the actual amount 
of visitors at a museum at any time step is only to a certain extend related 
to the entrance numbers analysed in section 08. Fewer visitors staying 
in museum for long time may result in similar heat and humidity loads as 
many daily visitors only staying for a short period of time. A study on the 
occupant density can be used to quantify this behaviour.
Knowledge of the behaviour of the users and on what happens outside 
of the opening hours of a museum is needed in order to make realistic 
energy simulations. A collaboration with M/S Maritime Museum of 
Denmark was done in order to study these usage patterns.
METHOD
Surveillance tapes from 10 Jan. to 9 Feb. 2016 was used to investigate 
the presence of visitors and employees. Five diff erent areas in the 
museum were selected. The areas were the Entrance/Shop, Auditorium, 
Café and two larger gallery areas. One camera in each area was used to 
determine a presence pattern. Many more cameras are available at the 
museum as well as in these rooms. It was considered suffi  cient with data 
from one camera per room when taking the time consumption for going 
through the material into consideration.
Figure 53. The auditorium, cafe and one of the galleries at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark (BIG a, 2016)
==PAGE:56==
46
The total sales of the year in relation to price groups were as well made 
to make an estimation of the user types.
In order to compare the number of people entering the museum based on 
sales and the amount of detections from the surveillance cameras a ratio 
between these two measurements was calculated as:
(16) 
Thus a high presence ratio indicates that a visitor is more likely to 
be detected by a camera in the given time interval than if the visitor 
presence ratio was low. A high presence ratio will also indicate that any 
visitor stays longer in that part of the museum. A value of one will mean 
that there is a visitor will be detected once in the investigated area for a 
given time period.
However, since the staff  at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark does 
not wear uniforms it was not possible to determine if the people detected 
was visitors or staff . Therefore, all people detected was registered.
Occupant density is a term used in ﬁ re engineering for calculating escape 
times (Spearpoint, 2008). But this term can as well be used in energy 
simulations as the heat and humidity loads of people are distributed over 
an area. The density was calculated per day as:
(17)
where:
During the recorded period the museum was open from Tuesday to 
Sunday from 11-17, but due to employees using the building outside of 
the opening hours the footage from 8.00-18.30 from Monday to Sunday 
was used. Based on an initial screening of the footage, this time range 
was evaluated to be able to capture most activity inside the museum. For 
every 15 minutes the number of people visible on every one of the ﬁ ve 
cameras was noted. Based on this, a large amount of data was generated. 
This data was analysed graphs comparing diff erent days, diff erent areas 
and diff erent times of the day. 
In the museum there were two types of users; visitors and employees. 
The number of employees at work was not noted, but it was typically 
2-3. Unfourunately, the museum did not have access to the daily number 
of visitors But as data of the amount of visitors per day was not available, 
data from the ticket sales was used. Three types of data were provided 
by the accounting. A total count of visitors for diff erent price categories 
for 2015 as well as a total income for each of the diff erent categories 
in 2015 was used to estimate an average price per visitor. Based on 
the daily ticket sales from the cash registers during the study and the 
account data from 2015, it was possible to make an estimation of the 
daily amount of visitors.
The museum had 22 diff erent price categories based on age, discount 
cards, group sales, campaigns etc. These ﬁ gures were used to calculate 
the average ticket fare per user:
(14)
The ticket sales from each of the cash registers could then be summed 
up to give an estimation of visitors per day:
(15) 
where:

RESULTS
The 22 price groups were divided into ﬁ ve major user types present at 
the museum during 2015 (Figure 54). The groups “Free” and "Campaigns" 
were used when no age were deﬁ ned in the speciﬁ c user group but 
only the sale price. Both the categories "Free" and "Children" would not 
have to pay for entrance and thus not inﬂ uence the sale numbers from 
registers. "Seniors" and "Campaigns" were allowed to pay discount prices. 
The average income per user was thus lower than the standard entrance 
fee. 
The estimated visitors per day based was plotted for the whole time 
period (Figure 55). The visitors were distributed in a repeating pattern 
over the month. After the closed Monday quite a lot visits were made on 
Tuesdays compared to the rest of the workdays. After a small decrease 
on Wednesdays the amount ramped up until Saturday after which a small 
decline on Sunday was showing.
The amount of square meters visible from the diff erent cameras were 
calculated based on CAD measurements and an evaluation of what could 
be seen on the chosen cameras (Table 8). 
==PAGE:58==
48
The occupancy densities were found for each day at each zone from 
10.30 - 17.30. Average, maximum and minimum values were plotted 
separately (Figure 56).  Very low occupancy densities were found in the 
galleries compared to the Cafe and Entrance/Shop area. A single day of 
high usage caused a large peak during Wednesdays for the Auditorium.

Wednesdays
The auditorium was used very little except for one Wednesday in which 
the usage was very high. On this speciﬁ c day 5 counting’s had higher 
results than 220 people and are thus not visible in the below ﬁ gure. This 
one day increased the average for Wednesdays signiﬁ cantly.
Only a few other organized presentations in the auditorium was visible 
during the 1 month of video provided, and none of these where able to 
ﬁ ll the auditorium. However, since the auditorium is open to the rest of 
the exhibition area a small amount of visitors often was present. The 
area was used by visitors to take a break for eating, talking or simply 
resting. Especially elderly people were often seen taking a short stop and 
sit in the seat of the auditorium. It must be noted that the auditorium is 
constructed as a bridge connecting the two sides of the museum and 
has large glass façades on each side. The view of the dock area from 
the auditorium is thus very good and the overall feel of the auditorium 
is open and ﬁ lled with daylight. Therefore, many entered to enjoy the 
view. Several children also used the room for playing. These unstructured 
activities resulted in most of the activity besides the one large presenta-
tion.

Entrance/shop presence ratio
Figure 62.  Presence ratio for the combined entrance and shop area
Figure 63.  Adjusted presence ratio for the combined entrance and shop area
The presence ratio for the shop/entrance for the diff erent days of the 
week are plotted Figure 62. During workdays, there are clearly larger 
values for the presence ratio as well as larger deviations than for the 
weekend. The boxes, indicating the 0.25 to 0.75 quartile, are also 
remarkably smaller in the weekend. Since measurements were done for 
one month, four days of data were available for each weekday, except for 
Mondays and Tuesdays where 5 days of data were available.
Since the presence ratio is aff ected by the employees working in the shop 
and ticket sales, an adjustment was made using an assumed number 
of employee detections. 64 counting’s per day was subtracted during 
weekdays and 94 counting’s per day was subtracted in the weekends 
(Figure 63). This is equal to 2 employees standing in a room from 11-17 
in workdays and 3 employees standing in the room in the weekend. This 
adjusted plot is seen in Figure 63. This adjustment lowers the maximum 
presence ratio from being 2.9 to 1.9 and lowers the maximum average 
presence ratio from 2 to 1.2. As no visitors are present on Mondays it is 
not included in these ﬁ gures.

This adjustment lowers the presence ratio, especially for the weekdays. 
The average values of the workdays are moved closer to the weekend, 
even though the workdays are subtracted by a lower number (64) than 
the weekend (94). Even with this adjustment there is a tendency for 
each user to stay in the shop for longer during weekdays than weekends. 
In the galleries there were a higher presence ratios during weekdays than 
the weekend (Figure 65 and Figure 66). Friday had lower presence ratio 
than Tuesday to Thursday and especially in gallery 1 Friday had values 
similar to the values of the weekend. As very little staff  was expected 
to be deteted in the galleries no adjustment of the gallery graphs was 
made. In general presence ratios for the galleries was smaller than the 
presence ratio for the shop/entrance.
DISCUSSION
Occupant densities
It is evident that the diff erent museum areas did not have similar 
occupant densities. The Entrance/shop area as well as the café had much 
higher values than the exhibition areas. The low occupancy density was 
partly due to the large area. This would, in many museums, be beneﬁ cial 
for the energy use as the galleries are the only areas where museum 
artefacts are traditionally exhibited and the indoor climate set points 
are set accordingly. A high people density will cause large loads to be 
minimized by the active and passive systems of the building. This will 
result in a larger energy use. Thus low people density in the exhibition 
areas will require less work by the systems in order to keep stable RH 
and temperatures and thereby less use of energy. As the M/S Maritime 
Museum does not have stricter requirements for the exhibition areas 
than for the rest of the museum this notion will not have an eff ect on 
this speciﬁ c museum.
User pattern
A similar usage pattern was visible in all room types except the 
auditorium. A similar usage pattern was visible in all room types except

==PAGE:66==
56
the auditorium.  Most visitors were present in the weekends, especially 
on Saturdays. Furthermore, Tuesdays did have more visitors than the 
following two days. The Auditorium however had constantly few users 
except for one day in which a very large number of users were present. 
The large amount of users in the auditorium did however not increase the 
amount of museum visitors on that particular day. If these patterns are 
repeated for the rest of the year they could be used for dimensioning the 
climate systems. In such a case, it would be reasonable to use Saturdays 
for determining heat and moisture loads. In case of the auditorium, very 
large climate systems would be needed, if the maximum loads were used 
for dimensioning the systems. But as a ﬁ lled auditorium is planned usually 
several days in advance, it should be possible to use measures that could 
provide additional cooling and ventilation at very speciﬁ c times. Increased 
use of natural ventilation or pre-cooled surfaces could be some of the 
possible means to cope with high loads. The one Wednesday in which 
the auditorium encountered a very high people density was caused by 
a yearly event for a school of marine and technical engineering. Thus all 
seats in the auditorium were used and some had to people were stand 
next to the seated area. In addition to being all full, the room was ﬁ lled 
with mostly male adults resulting in very large thermal and humidity 
loads. This event caused high values on Wednesdays on average, but 
it cannot be concluded that many people are likely to be present at 
the auditorium on Wednesdays. It can, however, be concluded that the 
auditorium was seldom used by many people, and when it was, very large 
loads occurred. It is important to note, that outside of the offi  cial use of 
the auditorium a small amount of people was often present. The reason 
for them being present could be curiosity, relaxing, eating lunch or the 
like. Similar behaviour could be expected in other museum auditoriums if 
they are not locked up or are located very isolated.
Although Mondays during this period was closed, there was still activity 
going on in all rooms, but much less than the rest of the week. When 
simulating museums Mondays should therefore be included with a low 
amount of users as maintenance and other types of work happens during 
this day. 
After a rather high amount of visitors Tuesday, the total activity per day 
was increasing during the week and peaked at Saturday after which a 
small decline occurred Sunday. This pattern of a many users during 
Tuesdays deviates from the study of user presence in other museums 
in this thesis. However, the reason for this pattern could be caused by 
the fact that the museum made a campaign for discounts for seniors 
on Tuesdays in which a free tour was included in the price as well as 
coff ee and cake from the café (M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark, 2016). 
This also explains the large number of people in the entrance area during 
Tuesdays. The tours were meeting up at this spot and thus created large 
people densities for small time periods. This also resulted in larger activity 
in the additional rooms of the museum.
The café area was used heavily throughout the whole day and not just 
during the traditional lunch times of 12.00-14.00. Especially during the 
weekend, a signiﬁ cant peak occurred from 13.00-14.00. Due to the 
relatively small area of the café in comparison to the other room types 
the average density was the highest among all room types investigated. 
The entrance and shop area had a high number of people detected. Since 
the area was relatively small, the people density was often very high. 
Since it was the ﬁ rst and last room the visitors would be in, the use was 
spread out over the entire opening period and at times late visitors or 
staff  could be detected at 18.15.
Based on the data it was not possible to make statistics that could 
determine how the user behaviour will be in other museums and at other 
times of the year. The data only describes the user behaviour at the 
museum in January. There might be diff erences in summer and winter 
behaviour and the type and placement of the museum might as well have 
had an impact on the ﬂ ow of visitors. The 15 min time step between 
measurements could cause large groups of people to be undetected 
since it is possible that a large group is present in a room for 14 minutes 
and still not being detected if the measurement pattern and the user 
behaviour pattern was out of sync. 
==PAGE:67==
57
Presence ratio
The average presence ratio at the entrance from Tuesday to Thursday 
was 1.9. It then dropped to 1.2 during Fridays and was down to 0.6 during 
the weekend. One reason for this could be that some of the visitors did 
spend more time in the shop in the weekdays than in the more crowded 
weekends. Another reason for this was that the amount of employees 
being present was only to a small degree related to the amount of visitors 
and that the employees were not accounted for in the visitor entrance 
numbers. But an employee in the shop would be present in that room 
most of the day, whereas most visitors would be constantly moving from 
room to room and may not even be counted once due to the timespan 
between counting’s. An employee in the ticket sales would cause a high 
presence ratio since he/she would be counted several times during a day. 
That would result in a high number in the numerator while not being 
included in the visitor entrances, leaving the denominator unchanged (in 
Eq. (16)). Therefore, days with few visitors, but with a similar amount of 
staff  resulted in a high presence ratio. 
For the galleries the presence ratio was smaller than in the entrance/
shop. That indicates that people did spend more time in the shop/
entrance than in either of the galleries. It is, however, important to note 
that many more galleries exist at the museum and therefore it cannot 
be concluded that people in spend more time in the shop than in the 
exhibition. The larger presence ratio in the weekdays indicates that even 
though less people visits the exhibition in the weekdays they tend to 
stay for a longer period of time.
The large deviations during the workdays could be caused by the lower 
amount of visitors. Therefore, a similar sized group would cause larger 
deviations in the workdays than in the weekend. It is important to note 
that the actual number of visitors was not known but only an estimation 
based on the income from ticket sales. If people on average got in at 
a lower price during workdays than in the weekend the presence ratio 
would as well be increased. Since the data on the amount of visitors 
per day is not of high quality then it is not possible to make any well 
based conclusions. However, if people in general spend more time in the 
museum in the weekdays than in the weekend it will be beneﬁ cial for the 
running of the climate systems since the total number of visitors is larger 
in the weekends. Thereby, the occupancy density will be decreased in the 
weekends and increased in the week days compared to an assumption of 
a ﬁ xed amount of time per visitor.
Data acquisition
This method required the footage to be viewed and registered manually 
at a 15 min time step which required a large amount of work. Since the 
museum made the surveillance gear available for one week, a smaller 
time step would result in either a smaller time period than a month or 
less cameras to be registered. But a smaller time period would give very 
little basis to evaluate which measurement was out of the ordinary if e.g. 
two Sundays had very diff erent patterns. Having fever room types would 
not be beneﬁ cial since the ﬁ ve areas chosen represents four of the major 
room types present in most museums. Furthermore, having double meas-
urements of the galleries areas made it possible to determine if similar 
patterns occurred. A motion detection function within the surveillance 
program was used to avoid watching large amount of footage in which 
no people was present. However, this function was not suitable for 
the exhibition area due to the large amount of video projections in the 
exhibition. Each visible area of the ﬁ ve cameras was measured using CAD 
drawings in order to be able to determine the density of people, when 
having the counted the amount of people present.
Not being able to determine the number of visitors per day precisely but 
only estimating it based on income is a large source of uncertainty. Ad-
ditionally, the actual counting’s was based on a large amount of manual 
work in which the risk of human error was greater than if an automatic 
system was used. Such a system could be based on registering the number 
of times a laser beam was crossed, or particle recognition software could 
be connected to the surveillance tapes.
==PAGE:68==
58
CONCLUSION
The day of the week had an inﬂ uence on the number of visitors. It was 
also evident that diff erent user patterns were connected to diff erent 
room types. If a user behaviour was to be simulated it would thus be 
reasonable to make diff erent user schedules for auditoriums, entrance/
shop areas, cafés and galleries as well as making diff erent schedules for 
each day.
The link between visitors per day and the amount of people present in 
each room was calculated using the presence ratio. Since employees and 
visitors were measured separately it did have an impact on the results 
from the entrance/shop area. But based on the visitor ratio from both 
the entrance/shop area and the galleries, it can be concluded that even 
though more people visited the museum in the weekend and that visitors 
did spend longer time in those areas in the weekdays than the weekend. 
However, since the number of visitors are larger in the weekend the 
occupany density were still higher in the weekend.
The ﬁ eld of user behaviours for energy simulation is a ﬁ eld in development 
and especially data about user behaviour in special buildings such as 
museums is almost non-existent. The data from this study only describes 
one museum for a short period of time. However, as very little data like 
this are avaible it can be used in future research as well as be the best 
basis for user schedules until further research is done within this ﬁ eld.
Due to this method being very time consuming it would be beneﬁ cial to 
develop an automated measuring method. This could either be done by 
sensors installed in the museums or by image detection systems working 
in combination with the recorded footage.
==PAGE:69==
59
09 Lighting loads
In order to determine realistic lighting loads for a new museum building, 
exhibition spaces from 6 museums were estimated. The study included 
spaces both above and below ground and showed that 10-15 W/m2 is a 
reasonable setting for the lighting loads.
Even though light is harmful for most collections it is needed in order for 
the visitors to experience the displays. Since daylight is often not desired 
due to preservation, electrical lighting is needed. The luminaries will 
aff ect the overall energy use of the museum due to the large electricity 
demand, but will also cause a signiﬁ cant heat load. Thereby, it will result 
in a smaller demand for heating, but can cause a larger cooling load. 
A small study was made on the amount of wattage that could theoret-
ically be used for diff erent exhibition spaces at the National Museum of 
Denmark, Thorvaldsens Museum, Glyptoteket, The Worker's Museum and 
the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark. In total, 53 exhibition spaces in 6 
museums below and above ground were researched.
Method
The six museums was visited an a number of of exhibition spaces in the 
diff erent parts of the museums was selected. For each room the width 
and length was either paced out or measured on plan drawings. Then 
the number of lighting ﬁ xtures was counted and divided into 9 diff erent 
categories. The wattage needed for similar new appliances were found 
and multiplied with the number of lamps. This resulted in an estimated 
wattage per area ratio in order to determine realistic values for museum 
lighting. Both rooms with and without daylight and rooms below and 
above ground were included. In Figure 67, a 10 of the spaces studied are 
shown. 
The lighting ﬁ xtures were divided into: small, medium and large spots, 
projectors, small pendant, large ﬂ oodlight, small and large ﬂ uorescent 
lamps and monitor screens. Additional emergency lighting and turned off  
lamps were not included. For each room it was noted whether the room 
was above or below ground and if the room had direct daylight, dimmed 
daylight or no daylight at all. Data on the assumed lighting ﬁ xtures as 
well as the calculations and images of all rooms in this study is placed in 
appendix 11
Figure 67.  10 of the 53 rooms investigated. The top ﬁ ve images are examples of exhibition spaces above ground and the bottom ﬁ ve are examples of spac-
es below ground. All images and references to museums can be found in appendix 11
==PAGE:70==
60
Results
The potential energy use diff ered greatly for the diff erent museums 
(Figure 68). The M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark had the largest 
average energy use of 15.9 W/m2 and Glyptoteket had the lowest of 5.4 
W/m2. There was a large variation between rooms in most museums and 
the National Museum of Denmark had spaces with a theoretical energy 
demand of 3.3 W/m2 and 43.2 W/m2. 
A large number of spaces both below and above ground used daylight 
but most tended to use either curtains, frosted glass or the alike to dim 
the light. A few spaces were only depended on daylight and used no 
electrical lighting at all. 
Discussion
It must be emphasised that these estimations are assumptions based 
on new energy effi  cient appliances. The actual use of the ﬁ xtures was 
not known but were sorted into a few standard categories. Data sheets 
from new products was used and resulted in a much lower energy use 
than older appliances. Incandescent lamps can have an effi  cacy of 9 lm/W 
whereas new light sources can have effi  cacies above 100 lm/W (Pohl & 
Zimmermann, 2003). The actual energy use of these museums could 
thus be signiﬁ cantly higher. 
The type of lighting chosen is, however, not just a question of using the 
newest and most effi  cient systems. Diff erent lighting sources will have 
diff erent colour rendering as well as diff erent Correlated Colour Tempera-
tures (CCT) (Jacobs, 2004). The lighting atmosphere will have a big impact 
on the light sources used in museums, as it has a great impact on the 
quality of the exhibition. Furthermore, it can be seen that museums have 
very diff erent types of collections, diff erent buildings and diff erent ways 
of using lighting to communicate with the visitors. These factors result in 
very diff erent lighting designs and thus diff erent lighting loads.
In some of the rooms, lighting was activated by sensors. This control 
feature can signiﬁ cantly decrease the energy consumption especially in 
less populated museums or in remote areas of larger museums.

==PAGE:71==
61
For some of the museums, the area was measured on plans. However, 
most rooms were paced out in order to estimate the dimensions which 
potentially caused some measurement uncertainty. A wide range of 
lighting sources were found during the investigation and having to put 
these into a few overall categories can cause some error.
From notes received from Rambøll, it can be seen that 20 W/m2 was used 
as heat load from light during the design phase as a basis for all areas 
at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark. Since these documents were 
made in 2009, where appliances were less effi  cient, it seems realistic that 
much lower values could be used in new museums. Based on conversa-
tions with EKJ staff , an assumed 10-15 W/m2 is to be used in the design 
of the National History Museum. Based on this small study, these values 
are realistic loads for many types of museums. However, since certain 
exhibitions like the ones at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark uses 
electrical lighting and projectors as an integrated part of the exhibition, 
much larger loads can be expected at some museums. According to 
Norconsult 45 W/m2 was used as heat load from lighting and equipment 
for indoor climate simulations of the new Ragnarock Museum in Roskilde.
The evolution in LED technology has had a signiﬁ cant inﬂ uence on the 
overall energy use in museums. Traditionally cooling in museums have 
been necessary mainly to cope with the heat from lamps. By switching 
to LED, the need for ventilation will be greatly reduced. However, for 
some existing museums, the costs of changing the lighting equipment 
to newer and more effi  cient appliances is considered too expensive even 
though the running costs will be signiﬁ cantly lowered (Padﬁ eld, appendix 
03)
Conclusion
Despite rather large sources of uncertainties in the measuring and 
diff erent aims for the diff erent exhibitions it can be concluded that 10-15 
w/m2 can be a quite realistic setting for simulation of the exhibition area 
of a new museum building. The value could be much higher or lower 
depending on the collection and the type of museum. If daylight is used. 
It can potentially be set to zero.  
Further research
It would be very interesting if an in depth analysis of the actual lighting 
and heat loads of museums was conducted. Such a study would have to 
be done in collaboration with a large number of museums in which the 
actual data sheet of each luminaire was used as well as more precise 
dimensions of rooms. Measurements of lux, lumen outputs as well as cat-
egorization of the diff erent museums and collection types would provide 
a knowledge of a ﬁ eld in which very little knowledge could be found.
Lighting in IDA ICE.
In IDA ICE, the use of electrical lighting will be added as a lighting 
load in zone. The energy consumption from this is measured with a 
separate meter called “lighting”. Additionally, the luminous effi  cacy and 
the convective fraction must be speciﬁ ed. The luminous effi  cacy is not 
relevant for the calculations of this thesis, since the amount of light 
emitted does not inﬂ uence the total energy consumption. The convective 
fraction, however, will determine if the wattage is applied as convection 
or radiation. This ratio will diff er depending on the type of luminaire to be 
modelled as well as the position in regards to air streams. The convective 
part will be added as heat to the room immediately whereas the radiative 
part is ﬁ rst absorbed by the surfaces and then later released to the room 
(Wijeysundera, 2016). The standard value for the convective fraction 
of 0.3 in IDA ICE is kept for this study being equivalent of recessed 
ﬂ uorescent light (Kreider, 2000).
==PAGE:72==
62
10 Ground temperatures
The following section will provide knowledge on how ground tempera-
tures are deﬁ ned in IDA ICE, how they can be calculated by hand and how 
to simulate ground temperatures using COMSOL. The type of soil as well 
as the surroundings will have an impact on the results.
To begin the study on how to calculate ground temperatures the standard 
methods in IDA ICE and hand calculations in DS 418 is studied.
When making calculations of the design transmission losses through 
the exterior surfaces based on DS418, the ﬁ rst 0-2 m of soil are to be 
calculated as -12 ºC (DS 418). Below this, all temperatures are to be set 
to 10 ºC. The transmission losses are therefore lowered signiﬁ cantly 
when the building is placed deeper in the ground than 2 meters. 
Ambient air temperatures can in IDA ICE be simulated with either 
synthetic weather data, or weather ﬁ les such as the Test Reference Year 
(TRY). When simulating ground temperatures, IDA ICE has the possibility 
to use either the ISO 13370 or theICE3 option. ISO 13370 is based on 
the average annual temperature and an amplitude in combination with 
a calculated time lag. The ISO 13370 is a reference to the Danish and 
European standard for “Thermal performance of buildings – Heat transfer 
via the ground – Calculation methods". To determine exactly how IDA ICE 
utilizes the standard cannot be done by viewing code in the advanced 
mode as key parameters are calculated in some underlying code of IDA 
ICE. However, in ISO 13370, calculations on the ground heat ﬂ ow rate 
can be done in a monthly basis. When doing so the ground temperatures 
are calculated as:
(18) 
where:
τ is set to 1 which is good approximation on the northern hemisphere (ISO 
13370, 2008). The standard provides a list of mean monthly external 
temperatures. Based on this list the amplitude of variations as well as 
the annual average temperatures can be calculated. When using the 
ISO13370 option in IDA ICE it can be seen that the same temperatures 
are used for underground walls and ﬂ oor.
ICE3 sets diff erent temperatures for the walls and ﬂ oors. Underneath 
the ﬂ oor, ICE3 will use the average temperature of the weather ﬁ le and 
the underground walls are connected to a crawlspace kept at ground 
temperature (IDA ICE, 2016).
No 2D or 3D heat ﬂ ows are supported in either of the methods. Addi-
tionally, the temperatures of the DS418, ICE3 and the ISO 13370 are 
not depending on depth. IDA ICE will calculate building parts just below 
ground with completely diff erent temperatures than just above ground. 
The temperatures will also not diff er with further distance into the 
ground as would be expected. 
The ambient temperatures in IDA ICE (TRY) have a high ﬂ uctuation pattern 
with large temperature deviations (Figure 69). The ICE3 walls follow the 
TRY pattern but with less variation whereas the temperature for the 
ﬂ oor is stable at 8 ºC. The ISO 13370 method results in a small wave 
curve with an average of 8 ºC. The ground temperatures from DS418 
are slightly higher than the ground temperatures from IDA ICE methods 
although the ambient temperatures from DS418 are very diff erent from 
the TRY. As DS418 is used for dimensioning the worst case scenario an 
external temperature of -12 ºC seems to be in accordance with the TRY. 
However, the soil temperatures are, compared to the IDA ICE method, too

63

tures for the TRY (DMI, 2013). It is based on calculating the ground tem-
peratures as a function of depth and time of year (Kvisgaard and Hadvig, 
1980):
(19) 
where:
Values of α=8∙10-7 m2/s, θ0m=9⁰C and θ0=3.15∙107 can were then used 
by Kvisggard and Hadvig to simply the formula (as shown in appendix 08):

(20) 
where:
It can be seen that further into the ground, the temperatures are aff ected 
by surface temperatures from earlier on in the year. Thus, the soil at 
1 m depth is calculated based on temperatures from the same month 
whereas the temperatures at 8 m is aff ected by the surface tempera-
tures 5 months earlier.

Neither the IDA ICE methods or DS418 are precise enough to use for the 
studies carried out in this thesis. To make a proper investigation of the 
thermal conditions of underground buildings, there is a need for a method 
taking into account the depth of the soil layer in the estimation of the 
ground temperatures as well as diff erent types of soil. COMSOL will, thus, 
be needed to make heat transfer simulations in order to estimate the soil 
temperatures. A formula is then needed in order to validate the method 
of using COMSOL for simulating the soil temperatures.
==PAGE:74==
64
Based on equation (20) the temperatures at diff erent depths for a year 
based on the average monthly temperatures of the ground surfaces 
was plotted (Figure 71). These temperatures correspond very well to the 
underground temperatures listed by SBI (SBI 139, 1984) (Figure 70). SBI 
does not provide the formula for calculating the plotted curves, but it can 
be used to compare with Figure 71 as part of a validation of Eq. (20). 
In both ﬁ gures, further depth results in the amplitude of temperatures 
being lowered and the peak of the curve being moved further into the 
year. At m depth this results in the temperatures of the ground will be 
warmer in the winter than in the summer. These tendencies correspond 
with measurements of temperatures done in the Copenhagen area at 
diff erent depths (Kristensen 1959). 
Whether the surface is covered by either soil or grass it will have an 
inﬂ uence on the ground temperatures. But these diff erences will be 
decreased signiﬁ cantly with further depth and is gone at 50-70 cm 
depth (Kristensen, 1959). The surface covering of the ground will not 
be studied further in this thesis, but it is important to remember that the 
upper layers of soil in a real case will be less precisely simulated if the 
heat transfer of the surface is not taken into account.
At 8 m, the amplitude is decreased signiﬁ cantly and an almost constant 
temperature can be seen in both ﬁ gures. It is thus relevant at which 
depth the walls of a building is placed. Close to the surface. The heating 
demand will be larger during the winter as the temperature variation of 
the soil is very close to the ambient temperatures. At greater depths, 
higher temperatures in winter could thus be utilized to lower the need 
for heating. The reverse situation is showing in the summer where more 
heating are needed 8 m below ground than above ground. 
Then a simulation model was set up in COMSOL in order to do heat 
transfer simulations. Eq. (19) was compared with results from COMSOL in 
order to validate the method.

GROUND TEMPERATURES IN COMSOL
Ground temperatures can be simulated in time using 2D models in 
COMSOL. In COMSOL, a heat balance during the year was set up and 
temperatures at the diff erent layers were measured. The inputs needed 
besides the geometry were the thermal conductivity, the density and 
the heat capacity of the soil and the temperature of the air. 
In COMSOL, the air temperature can be speciﬁ ed as a function of time 
but not as a matrix like the TRY. A way of specifying the air temperatures 
would be to use Eq. (18) with the average temperatures and amplitude 
given by the ISO 13370. But DS418 use a similar equation with diff erent 
constants. In order to determine which standard had the best match with 
TRY, a plot was made (Figure 72). DS418 speciﬁ ed a constant temperature 
Jun
Aug
Oct
Mar
May
Jan
Dec
TRY
ISO 13370
DS 418
Figure 72.  TRY temperatures for ambient air compared with ground temper-
atures with constants from ISO13770 and DS418
of 8 ºC and an amplitude of 8.5 ºC, whereas using the ISO13770 resulted 
in a constant temperature of 7.795 ºC and an amplitude of 7.05 ºC. In the 
ISO 13370 the constants are calculated based on a list of weather data 
(appendix 09). The two temperature functions were very similar, but the 
DS418 constants gave a better approximation to the TRY temperatures. 
The DS418 was, thus, used as boundary condition for the temperatures. 
Diff erent types of soil will have diff erent thermal properties but the 
moisture content also has a large inﬂ uence on the thermal properties of 
soil (Farouki, 1981). Since the properties of the soil can vary, diff erent 
types of soil is created in order to study the inﬂ uence of the soil type on 
the simulation results. 
The soil in DS418 is set to have a thermal conductivity of 2 W/(mK) and
ρ · c=2∙106 J/(m3K). The density and heat capacity is thus assumed to be 
1500 kg/m3 and 1333 J/(kgK). The soil used in Kvisggard and Hadivig (1980) 
is set to have a thermal conductivity of 2 W/(mK) and a thermal diff usivity 
of 8 · 10-7 m2/s. The density and Heat conductivity was assumed based 
on the equation for thermal diff usivity (Lienhard and Lienhard, 2008):
(21)
These were compared to other types of soil (Table 9) with diff erent 
properties from Hamdhan and Clarke (2010). The inﬂ uence of the soils 
on the ground temperatures was then tested in COMSOL.
The COMSOL model was made as layers of soil placed upon each other 
and the DS418 temperature variation were used as a boundary condition 
for the external temperatures. A description of the mesh and solver 
settings of the COMSOL model can be found in appendix 10.

As seen from this example of diff erent soil types, the thermal properties 
vary greatly depending on the soil type and water content. The soil types 
were investigated in COMSOL in order to ﬁ nd the impact it has on the 
ground temperatures at diff erent depths.
It is important to know the water content as the heat capacity of soil is 
mainly related to the porosity and water content (Kristensen, 1959). For 
these simulations, the water content will not change, but in reality the 
water content will change over time. A series of simulations were made 
using only one soil type for all layers. The nine types of soil from Table 9 
were then used for nine diff erent simulations. The average temperatures 
at diff erent depth was plotted as a function of time (Figure 73 - Figure 
76). The plots were made based on values after 4 years in order to get 
stable temperatures.
Table 9.  Thermal properties for diff erent types of soil from Hamdhan and Clarke (2010), DS418 (2011) and Kvisgaard & Hadvig 
(1980). Assumed values based on the thermal diff usivity and thermal conductivity are colored grey.
Results
At 0.6 m below the ground surface, the yearly amplitude was high and 
the soil type had some inﬂ uence as the results deviated slightly for the 
diff erent types (Figure 73). However, at 2.5 m the diff erence between 
the diff erent soil types were more clearly showing (Figure 74). Soils 
with low thermal conductivity like A, C, and F had very little amplitude 
whereas soils with higher thermal diff usivity like D, G and H had the 
largest amplitude. 
At 8 meter depth, the diff erence between the diff erent soil types is not 
very large (Figure 75). Soils with a large thermal conductivity had almost 
no amplitude whereas large thermal diff usivity will cause some amplitude.
It took some time for the yearly ﬂ uctuations to stabilize, especially at 
larger depths. The temperatures increased slightly for the ﬁ rst four years, 
after which a similar pattern is visible for the remaining 6 years (Figure 
76).

COMPARISON OF HAND CALCULATIONS AND 
COMSOL OUTPUT
Hand calculations based on Eq. (19) were compared to the COMSOL 
calculations with the soil types based on properties from Kvisgaard & 
Hadvig and DS418.
The hand calculations of soil type I used the thermal properties as well 
as the ambient temperature list provided by Kvisgaard & Hadvig. This 
resulted in Eq. (20) based on weather data with average temperature 
of 8.73 ºC and an amplitude of 10.5 ºC (weather data input are seen in 
appendix 09). By making a hand-calculation of H using the DS418 soil 
properties and Eq. (19) the following equation was derived (appendix 08):
(22) 
where:
The equation was used with the weather data from DS418 also used 
for the COMSOL simulations. This weather proﬁ le had a lower average 
temperature of 8 ºC and lower amplitude of 8.5 ºC than used for the hand 
calculations of soil I.
This resulted in the hand calculations of soil I having a slightly larger 
deviation and larger average temperatures as seen in Figure 77. The 
hand calculations of H and the COMSOL simulations of the same soil 
corresponds very well, being almost identical at both 0.6m and 8 m depth.
Both the hand calculations based on Eq. (19) and the COMSOL simulations 
can therefore be used in order to estimate ground temperatures. But Eq. 
(19) is only valid if the same type of soil is used throughout the whole 
depth. Additionally, using COMSOL added the possibility of including 
other energy sources than the ambient air and was thus the method to 
be used in the continued work of this thesis.
