COMBINATION OF SOIL TYPES
A small study in COMSOL was done to determine the eff ect of combining 
soil types. Six diff erent soil proﬁ les were created (Figure 79 and Table 
10) based on the soil types deﬁ ned in Table 9. Soil A had the lowest 
thermal conductivity and was thus used as insulation whereas soil type 
G was used as a heat store. Soil type H had properties in between these 
extremes. s1, s2, s3 consisted of just one type of soil whereas s4, s5 and 
s6 consisted of a combination of soil types with soil A as an insulation 
layer on top.
s2 and s3 followed a similar pattern having the highest amplitude of all 
soils at all depths (Figure 80). This was also expected since these proﬁ les 
did not have insulation of soil A in the top layers and thus would be able 
to gain more heat in the summer but would as well loose much in the 
winter. 
s1 solely consisted of soil A and s6 had a 2.5 meter thick layer of soil A on 
top. Both had large amplitudes in the upper layer. But at 2.5m depth this 
amplitude was very small and at 8 m depth it was almost gone.

s4 and s5 had a 0.6 m layer of soil type A on top of a soil with larger 
thermal conductivity. Thereby, the soil proﬁ les were able to store large 
amounts of energy and still being able to keep it. This resulted in small 
amplitudes even at 0.6 m depth. 
It can be concluded that the type of soil and the moisture content 
will have an inﬂ uence on the yearly temperatures of the soil. But with 
increased depth, the type of soil will have less of an inﬂ uence and at 8m, 
the temperatures will be very stable regardless of the soil type.
Soil with low thermal conductivity and mass will function as a insulation 
material. The accumulation of energy from the air in summer is hindered 
by the insulation soil but will contain more heat in the winter. Using dense 
soil, the opposite phenomenon will occur. Furthermore, adding dry sand 
on top of other soil types will results in much more stable temperatures.

=
HEAT FROM BUILDINGS TO THE GROUND
Not only the ambient air but also heat loss from buildings will add to the 
heat stored in the underground. With the use of COMSOL, the eff ect of 
the heat loss from buildings on ground temperatures was investigated. 
Setting all boundaries as adiabatic except for one heat source allowed 
for investigation of the isocurves from the speciﬁ c sources. By simulating 
the building heat loss and the ambient heat loss one by one, it could be 
seen that these sources had a very diff erent eff ect on the temperature 
proﬁ les. 
Figure 83.  Heat provided by the ambient air
Figure 84.  Heat provided by the building
Figure 85.  Interference of heat sources
The heat from the ambient air is evenly distributed perpendicular to the 
ground surface with the magnitude changing during the year (Figure 83). 
Thereby, the isocurves are straight lines moving up and down according 
to the season. 
As a building is relatively small compared to the ground volume, it can be 
considered as a point source. Very close to the building the isocurves will 
be straight, but with further distance from the building the isocurves will 
circular (Figure 84). The heat from the building can either be provided 
as a steady heat source or the internal temperatures of the building can 
change during the season. If a steady heat source is used, the temperature 
of the ground will increase until a steady state is reached. If the tempera-
tures inside the building is changing during the season, then the rounded 
isocurves will move back and forth depending on the season. 
When combining these two sources in the same simulation the isocurves 
will blend (Figure 85). Close to the building higher temperatures will be 
reached due to having heat provided by both the ambient air and the 
building. Further away from the building the heat loss will have less 
inﬂ uence on the isocurves. Thus, at a certain distance from the building 
the isocurves will be straight.
==PAGE:81==
71
THE EFFECT OF THE SURROUNDINGS
Six cases were studied in order to determine the eff ect of nearby 
buildings on the ground temperatures at diff erent depths. All simulations 
were performed using DS418 soil and simpliﬁ ed building structures in 
COMSOL.
The study based on simulating a case with two buildings placed in soil 
and a case with only one building. By measuring the temperatures from 
both cases and subtracting the results from the two simulations the 
eff ect of the extra building on the soil temperatures was found.
Two cases with no nearby buildings were simulated as a reference. The 
ﬁ rst reference case was a building of 5 m depth to be compared with 
case 1, 3, 4, 5 and 6 (Figure 87).  Case 2 was compared with a 8 m deep 
building. The average temperatures at diff erent depths were logged for 
a 20 year period. These measurements were taken at measuring lines at 
diff erent depths (Figure 86). The vertical measurements were done as 
average temperatures at lines placed 1 m away from the building. The 
horizontal measurements were done at a line placed 3 meters below the 
building. The six cases were chosen in order to investigate the inﬂ uence 
of depth, distance, symmetry and asymmetry.

Figure 88.  The diff erence of increase of the soil temperatures of case 1

It can be seen that near the surface the diff erence between the two 
building cases and the one building reference case was not very big, as 
line A was close to zero in all six cases (Figure 88- Figure 93). However, 
the diff erence increased with further depth into the ground and were 
most notable below the building (marked with red). All diff erences 
increased steadily overtime as the soil got heated. 
Placing both the nearby building and the measured building further into 
the ground increased the accumulated heat. Having buildings placed 8 m 
into the ground (Figure 88) rather than 5 m (Figure 89) resulted in a 0.2 
ºC diff erence rather than 0.12 ºC diff erence below the building after 20 
years.
By having the measured building placed 5 m into the ground and the 
nearby building 8 into the ground (Figure 90), caused results in between 
case 1 and 2. But if the nearby building was on ground away then very 
little eff ect could be measured (Figure 91)
If the buildings were placed close to each other the ground tempera-
tures was increased further (Figure 92 and Figure 93). Having a distance 
between buildings of only 15 m apart resulted in a 0.4 ºC diff erence in 20 
years in case 5 and 0.19 ºC in case 6. 
Both the distance to nearby buildings and the depth of the nearby 
buildings did have a large impact in the soil temperatures. Therefore, more 
buildings in contact with the ground will cause the underground temper-
atures in an area to increase. Having more buildings placed deeper into 
the ground and closer will additionally increase the heat accumulation. 
The surroundings can therefore to some extend be used to decrease the 
need for heating as buildings collectively will heat up the shared volumes 
of soil. As seen in all cases underground buildings will in time have to 
spend less energy on heating due to the soil temperatures increasing.

11  Combine COMSOL and  
IDA ICE
In order to utilize the potentials of both COMSOL and IDA ICE, a combined 
method for making energy simulations was made. Due to the lack of 
vertical heat ﬂ ows in IDA ICE walls some calculation error will occur. The 
error can be minimized by simulating everything on the outside of the 
insulation in COMSOL and export the temperatures to an IDA ICE model 
containing only the insulation layer and the interior walls.
One dimensional heat ﬂ ux simulations of IDA ICE and COMSOL give 
very similar results (appendix 12). However, COMSOL has the ability to 
calculate the heat transfer of the ground in 2D/3D in time, whereas the 
heat transfer in IDA ICE is only one directional in time. In IDA ICE. The heat 
transfer can only occur back and forth from interior to exterior normal 
to the surface plane. For simulations of buildings above ground, this 
simpliﬁ ed heat ﬂ ow will not have as large an inﬂ uence on the result as on 
below ground buildings as seen in this section.
Isocurves of the model is seen in Figure 95. In the soil layers near the 
wall, temperatures will change faster with further depth whereas the 
temperatures underneath the building is more constant. By studying 
the isocurves it is evident that temperatures will be larger closer to the 
middle of the building. 
The disregard of vertical heat ﬂ ows in IDA ICE results in no energy being 
transferred into the ground from the ambient air and no heat can be 
transferred from one layer to another as marked with black arrows in 
Figure 94. Heat from the building walls can only be transferred in the 
horizontal direction in a one dimensional ﬂ ow. That results in the heat 
have to travel a longer distance to reach the ambient especially for the 
upper layers. 
Thus, using IDA ICE for the simulation of the heat ﬂ ows underground will 
lead to very rough assumptions. However, in order to calculate the total 
energy consumption of a building, IDA ICE includes many parameters like 
ventilation, user presence, CO2 etc. that cannot be modelled in COMSOL. 
COMSOL is a tool for investigation of complex ﬂ ows in models with a 
Figure 94.  Heat transfer in soil
Figure 95.  Isocurves in soil (COMSOL screenshot)
4 m
==PAGE:85==
75
Buffer
x
y
Exterior

relatively small amount of parameters with a high precision. On the other 
hand, IDA ICE is capable of incorporating many parameters into a complex 
model in which each parameter is relatively simpliﬁ ed. 
A study was, thus, made on how to utilize the strengths of both programs 
in order to increase the quality of the simulations by linking the two 
programs into a work ﬂ ow. Studies have been made for storages placed 
on ground (Christensen and Janssen, 2009, and Bøhm and Ryhl-Svend-
sen, 2011). But as to bee seen in this section, walls is the part causing 
the largest simulation errors. It was not possible to ﬁ nd any standard or 
prescribed methods on making this link for underground buildings why a 
range of possible methods was developed and simulated to be compared 
against the ﬂ ux simulated in COMSOL.
METHOD 
ISO 13370 (2008) includes a method of calculatin the heat ﬂ ux through 
the ﬂ oor to use for dynamic simulation programs. In this method the 
ﬂ oor construction includes half a meter of soil and a thin virtual layer. 
However, this method is often not suitable for horizontal heat ﬂ uxes as 
thicker walls will result in calculations errors which will be explained later 
in this section.
A reference model with 1 m of concrete and 0.5 m of simulation was made 
in COMSOL in which the heat ﬂ ux and temperatures could be measured. 
The diff erent methods utilized the geometry and programs diff erently 
but in Figure 96 the deﬁ nition of diff erent areas of the model can be 
seen. The inner line is the ”Interior”, the outside of the building body is 
deﬁ ned as ”Exterior” and with an off set of 2 m a ”Buff er” line is created. 
The Insulation line is placed on the outside of the insulation layer 
The temperatures for the ambient air were set according to DS418 using 
(18), whereas the internal temperatures used the same function in order 
to have variations from 20 ºC to 25 ºC during the year synchronized with 
the peaks of the ambient temperatures. The study was done for walls 
and ﬂ oors separately since the heat ﬂ ows are signiﬁ cantly diff erent in 
these two areas and because walls in IDA ICE will lack heat transfer in 
the y direction whereas ﬂ oors will lack transfer in the x direction. The soil 
temperatures at year 11 of the COMSOL model were then imported into 
IDA ICE to make a simulation. In order to have similar interior tempera-
tures in both IDA ICE and COMSOl the walls in IDA ICE was disconnected 
to the zone at instead was instead linked to an excel ﬁ le with interior 
temperatures corresponding to COMSOL.
The connection between wall and ﬂ oor is not included in IDA ICE due 
to the calculation of heat transfer being in one direction and would 
have to be included as a line loss. A method for deﬁ ning line losses in 
underground buildings can be appendix 18.
The soil temperatures measured at the buff er line are not the same as 
the temperatures measured at the exterior line or the insulation line. It 
is diffi  cult to determine where to measure the temperatures in COMSOL 
to import into IDA ICE and how much soil should be modelled in IDA ICE in 
order to get the best approximation of the heat loss. In order to determine 
how to calculate the ground temperatures in IDA ICE for the further work 
Figure 96.  Reference model and deﬁ nitions
==PAGE:86==
76
of the thesis, a couple of diff erent methods were developed and tested. 
The ﬂ ux from COMSOL was used as reference values and the ISO 13370 
and ICE3 methods were included in order to determine whether or not 
these new methods was an improvement of the existing method used 
in IDA ICE. The same method was used for the wall and for the ﬂ oor in 
separate simulations. 
The transfer of data from COMSOL to IDA ICE was based on the principle 
seen in Figure 97. Data from the reference model in COMSOL was 
converted into a function in Excel and imported into IDA ICE. Only one 
year from the COMSOL simulation should be imported into IDA ICE in 
order to avoid numerical errors. This work ﬂ ow can be varied in a number 
of ways and the methods tested were as follows: 
When opening an underground wall component in the advanced level of 
IDA ICE, it can be seen that a wall placed under ground has 3 additional 
layers added. The ﬁ rst layer is a 0.1 m thick layer of with properties similar 
to an insulation material. The second layer is 0.5m of standard soil and 
the third layer is a 0.1 thick "virtual layer" with no heat capacity but a low 
heat conductivity. . In the following work, these layers were removed in 
the methods for combining COMSOL and IDA ICE, but kept when using the 
standard methods of IDA ICE. Removing these layers using the standard 
methods in IDA ICE caused very large changes in heat ﬂ ux and a larger 
deviation from the COMSOL reference. If these layers were kept when 
using the new methods, the ﬂ uxes were made to small. The methods 
tested for this study were:
COMSOL (reference)
The heat ﬂ ux from COMSOL was used as a reference for the above 
mentioned results. The ﬂ ux could be exported from COMSOL and imported 
into IDA ICE and used to overrule the ﬂ ux calculated by the walls of IDA 
ICE. However, this would interfere with other functions in IDA ICE based 
on the wall temperatures and the COMSOL ﬂ ux is only used for reference.
ISO 13370
The ISO 13370 method for underground temperatures in IDA ICE was 
used. This method does not involve the use of COMSOL or changing the 
mathematical model of IDA ICE.
ICE3
ICE3 is alternative underground simulation method included as a standard 
in IDA ICE. This method does also not include the use of COMSOL.
Buff er temperature 
In COMSOL, a reference model was generated with both heat from the 
ambient air and heat from the building. The temperatures at the buff er 
line were then exported from COMSOL and used as input for the wall 
temperatures in IDA ICE. In IDA ICE, the soil, concrete and insulation were 
modelled and the additional layers added by IDA ICE for underground 
simulations were removed. Thereby, IDA ICE has the capability of storing 
heat in both part of the buff er soil volume and wall. Beyond the 1 m of 
buff er-soil, the heat storage will be simulated by COMSOL.
Exterior temperature 
In COMSOL, a reference model was generated with both heat from the 
ambient air and heat from the building. The temperatures at the wall 
level was then exported from COMSOL and then used as an input for 
the wall temperatures in IDA ICE. The concrete and the insulation layer 
were modelled in IDA ICE and the 2 additional layers added by IDA ICE for 
underground simulations were removed. All heat storage and 2D ﬂ ows of 
the ground was, thus, simulated in COMSOL.
Insulation temperature
This method was similar to the two before mentioned but was based 
on measuring the temperatures at the insulation line in COMSOL and 
modelling the insulation in IDA ICE. Thus, all heat storage is done in 
COMSOL reference

COMSOL. All simulations were done simulating 11 years in COMSOL and 
using the 11th year for the IDA ICE simulation.
RESULTS
The results of the simulations were plotted with results for walls and 
ﬂ oors separately (Figure 98 and Figure 99). The ISO 13370 method 
deviated signiﬁ cantly from the reference values from COMSOL and 
resulted in very low ﬂ uxes for both walls and ﬂ oors. Some amplitude was 
showing but it was much smaller than the amplitude of the reference. 
The average of ISO 13370 was signiﬁ cantly higher for both the wall and 
the ﬂ oor. Furthermore, by looking at the peak of the curve it can be seen 
Figure 98.  Heat lux from wall with diff erent simulation methods
Figure 99.  Heat ﬂ ux from ﬂ oor with diff erent simulation methods
that the wave time seems to be 3 months behind for the wall and 11 
months behind (or one month to early) in the ﬂ oor simulation.
The ICE3 method provided a better estimation of the heat ﬂ ux in this test. 
For the ﬂ oor (Figure 99) the ICE was a good match with the reference 
from COMSOL, whereas the wall had to low ﬂ ux values and a too large 
amplitude of the curve (Figure 98).
The method of simulating temperatures from COMSOL and exporting 
data to IDA ICE did in general provide results much closer to the reference. 
Placing the boundary between COMSOL and IDA ICE at the buff er line 
caused larger deviations in ﬂ ux and a time off set of around 2 months for

the wall. For the ﬂ oor a similar off set time of 2 months were showing but

Using the method “Wall temperatures“ caused an off set for the wall ﬂ ux 
of around a month as well as the average values being too low compared 
to the reference. However, for the ﬂ oor, using Wall temperatures" resulted 
in values close to the reference.
With the use of the method “Insulation temperatures“, results very close 
to the reference were simulated for the ﬂ oor. For the wall, small deviations 
were present, but were much smaller than for the other methods  
DISCUSSION
None of the methods combining COMSOL and IDA ICE provided an exact 
match with the reference value from COMSOL. Though using “Insulation 
temperatures“ resulted in a good match with the reference. However, 
all the methods using COMSOL in combination with IDA ICE did create 
a better match than using the ISO 13370 and ICE3 methods. The 
"Insulation temperatures“ method was thus used in the further work of 
this thesis.
This study forced the IDA ICE indoor temperatures to be controlled by 
the sine curve in order to be able to compare the results. Normally, indoor 
temperatures in IDA ICE would ﬂ uctuate more and many more parameters 
would be included. In real simulations, it would therefore not be possible 
to have an exact reference value from COMSOL as the heat emitted from 
the building in IDA ICE. For better estimations the heat temperatures of 
the outer layer of insulation in IDA ICE could be used as a new boundary 
condition in COMSOL for a new simulation. The output of this simulation 
could then be used in IDA ICE for a more precise simulation. The iteration 
will thus form a closed loop (Figure 100).
The closed loop would require a need for a method for importing the output 
of IDA ICE into COMSOL. The method for deﬁ ning boundary conditions in 
COMSOL in this thesis has been a build in feature allowing for boundary 
conditions to be deﬁ ned as functions. The temperature patterns from 
COMSOL can easily be deﬁ ned as a function but the temperature output 
from most IDA ICE simulations will be diffi  cult to include in the COMSOL 
model since the temperature pattern is irregular and is hard to ﬁ t into the 
function-setup in COMSOL.
For most of the methods, it will in practice be needed to make an IDA 
ICE simulation with estimated values for the interior boundary conditions 
and then make a COMSOL simulation. The output will then be used for 
the IDA ICE simulation in an open loop work ﬂ ow as seen in Figure 101. 
As the internal temperatures in a museum are relatively strictly deﬁ ned, 
it will be possible to make a reasonably good estimation.

Figure 101.  Open loop dependence of programs
==PAGE:89==
79
It was clearly showing that the wall was more diffi  cult to simulate re-
alistically using one directional heat ﬂ ows than the ﬂ oor. This was due 
to most of the heat ﬂ ux in the ﬂ oor was happening normal to the ﬂ oor 
plate. Some heat is transferred horizontally but this amount is not large 
enough to cause large deviations. 
Better simulations were made when a larger part of the wall was done 
in COMSOL rather than in IDA ICE. The reason for the problems when 
simulating walls using one directional heat ﬂ ows are seen in Figure 102. 
The temperatures of the zone is the same from top to bottom and results 
in an even temperature boundary. However, due to the diff erences in 
temperatures from the soil, an uneven boundary is eff ecting the wall 
from the exterior. Some parts of the building component will then be 
heated more than other parts, causing a heat transfer in the wall in the y 
direction in COMSOL. IDA ICE cannot simulate heat transfer between the 
layers in the wall causing some diff erences between the COMSOL heat 
ﬂ ux and the simulation results from IDA ICE. As temperature gradients are 
larger near the ground surface, larger errors will occur when performing 
onedimentional calculations. Thicker walls will also cause larger errors as 
it will result in a larger area for the vertical heat ﬂ ows. 
If the temperatures of the diff erent levels of the wall are needed, then 
using a "wall part" in IDA ICE can be used for adding diff erent temper-
atures at diff erent levels. However, if only one room is below ground 
only there is no need to subdivide the walls and ﬂ oors. A test was done 
comparing a simulation with four walls parts of diff erent temperatures 
and one wall with an average temperature. The deviation between the 2 
simulations was between 0 and 10-5 at diff erent time steps as the small 
deviation was a result of numerical errors. 
Not including the concrete layer or any soil in the IDA ICE model removes 
a big part of the thermal capacity of the IDA ICE model. However, the 
thermal capacity can be split into two phenomenon’s: long term and short 
term thermal capacity. The long term thermal capacity was modelled in 
COMSOL and added to the IDA ICE model through the temperatures of the 
wall and ﬂ oor. This was calculated based on the boundary conditions of 
COMSOL and was thus not aff ected directly by the simulation in IDA ICE. 
The small ﬂ uctuations in temperatures of the interior would not be used 
in the long term thermal capacity of the soil. The rapid ﬂ uctuations in 
temperatures of the interior would have very little eff ect on the outside 
of the insulation due to the time it took for the heat to penetrate the 
insulation layer (appendix 13). This would especially be true with more 
thermal mass added to the interior side of the insulation. As the ﬁ nal 
simulations in this thesis contained internal concrete walls these walls 
would be used for the short term thermal capacity whereas the soil and 
concrete on the external side of the insulation would be used for the long 
term thermal capacity. 
For using this method, it is important that the internal temperatures of the 
building is known. Using a better estimation for the interior temperature 
proﬁ les in COMSOL will result in a more precise simulation. Therefore, 
Exterior
temperatures
Interior
temperatures
Figure 102.  Uneven temperature boundaries of a wall of insulation causing 
vertical heat transfer
==PAGE:90==
80
calculating unheated spaces are not suited for this method Additionally, 
not using insulation is therefore not possible for using this method as 
the distinction between long term and short term thermal storage will 
disappear. This method should only be used if the boundary conditions 
for the interior in IDA ICE and COMSOL corresponds well. 
Another thing to be aware of when using this method is making sure 
that the time from COMSOL, corresponds to the time in IDA ICE. IDA ICE 
uses hourly values for the time vector in the simulation. However, since 
at least 10 years of simulation is needed from COMSOL, hourly values will 
cause a large ﬁ le. Typically, only the data from the last year of a COMSOL 
simulation. But before importing data into IDA ICE it is important to note 
that IDA ICE uses the actual amounts of days in a given year where 
COMSOL years are always 365 days. Thus, these time issues needs to 
be sorted out in excel before importing data into IDA ICE. Having the data 
from COMSOL and the simulation in IDA ICE to be out of sync will have 
a large inﬂ uence on the simulation. As the programs are not automat-
ically linked, errors or changes done in COMSOL will result in a need to 
repeat the work ﬂ ow of exporting, preparing data in excel and importing 
into IDA ICE. Doing iterations or small changes in geometry will with this 
method result in signiﬁ cantly larger amounts of manual labour than if 
these simulations could be done in just one program. 
The destruction between what should be modelled in each program is 
seen in Figure 103. The red dotted line indicates the boundary between 
the IDA ICE and COMSOL geometry. The soil, the parts of the basement 
walls on the outside of the insulation, nearby basements and other 
below ground inﬂ uences are to be simulated in COMSOL. In IDA ICE, only 
the building parts on the interior side of the insulation as well as the 
insulation itself are to be modelled in IDA ICE for the below ground part 
of the model. Above ground all building components are modelled and 
nearby buildings are to be modelled due to the inﬂ uence of shading.
CONCLUSION
The fact that IDA ICE calculates the heat ﬂ ows in one direction will result 
in some errors when simulating walls with an uneven temperature dis-
tribution. It can be concluded that the method "Insulation temperatures" 
resulted in the best match with the reference heat ﬂ ux from COMSOL. 
The method is time consuming, but results in more realistic results than 
the build in functions of IDA ICE. Furthermore, the method allows for more 
factors to be modelled in COMSOL and used for the combined simulation.

IDA ICE
Figure 103.  Selection of parameters to be included in simulations for COM-

==PAGE:91==

12 Simulations
A series of simulations was done of a simpliﬁ ed museum building using 
dynamic user patterns. For museums above and below ground, the 
insulation thickness, indoor climate requirements and occupancy densities 
were varied in order to study the inﬂ uence on energy consumption. 
Simulations with only one parameter change was used to make further 
studies. These showed that underground museums do have a potential 
lower energy consumption but that thick insulation layers will minimize 
the diff erence. Futhermore,, control strategies had a larger inﬂ uence on 
the energy use than whether or not the museum is placed underground.
As seen in the previous sections, the museum building is a complex 
typology since many parameters has an inﬂ uence on the energy 
consumption. Furthermore, these parameters will have an inﬂ uence on 
each other. Especially the occupancy makes it diffi  cult to analytically 
determine an optimal solution as the amount of people is constantly 
changing. People are source of both CO2, humidity, and heat and thereby 
inﬂ uence both the ventilation system, the humidity control and the 
heating and cooling systems. As both RH and temperatures are to be 
within certain ranges the number of occupants will impact the energy 
use.
A
B
C
Figure 104.  Simulation cases
METHOD
A series of simulations was carried out in order to determine the possible 
advantages by placing museums underground. A simpliﬁ ed museum 
consisting of a 1000 m2 room with 6 m ceiling height was placed at three 
levels as seen in Figure 104. 
Type A was the building above ground which only had contact with the 
ground through the ﬂ oor.
Type B was placed just below ground. The walls were covered but the 
ceiling was in connection with the ambient air. Both the walls and ﬂ oor 
were in connection with the ground.
Type C was placed 4 meters below ground level, having all surfaces in 
connection with the ground.
==PAGE:92==
82
In order to make the types comparable, no windows were placed in either 
type. Identical walls, ﬂ oors and ceilings were used as only the type 
and placement of vapour barrier diff ered from below ground to above 
ground surfaces. Exterior walls were 1 m thick and made of concrete and 
the interior surfaces was a 250 mm thick layer of concrete. The ﬂ oor 
and ceiling consisted of 300 mm concrete slabs. The inﬂ uence of the 
insulation thickness on the result was included by simulating cases with 
150 mm, 300 mm and 500 mm insulation for the three diff erent types. 
Likewise, three diff erent control strategies for the RH was used (Figure 
105). Type "Loose" allowed deviations from 20-80% RH all year, whereas 
"Medium" allowed for 40-60% RH on a yearly basis. Lastly, "Tight" was 
controlled by a maximum of 5%-point change per day with set point 
changing from 55-60% RH in the summer to 40-45% RH in the winter 
in a sinus wave pattern. In all the cases, the internal temperatures were 
20-25 ºC during the year, also controlled by a sinus wave. 
In order to model realistic user patterns, yearly visitor data was used 
from the National Museum of Denmark in combination with investiga-
tions at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark. From this a user schedule 
was made. The amount of users in the museum was calculated based 
on the occupancy density. The average occupancy densities were set to 
six values in the range of 0.00125 to 0.04 pers/m2. Then the inﬂ uence 
of insulation, occupancy density, type of building case and control 
strategy was studied through parameter changes and a large number of 
simulations. 
Afterwards a series of one parameter changes was done. For these 
studies the occupancy density was set to 0.005 pers/m2 being the 
average occupancy density in the gallery in the study at the M/S Maritime 
Museum of Denmark. 300mm of insulation was used and the RH was 
controlled by the "tight" control. Simulations was done where the yearly 
pattern of the National Museum of Denmark was replaced by patterns 
from the Geological Museum and the Zoological museum. The same set 
of simulations was done for both Type A and B.
Another series of one parameter studies was made i order to study:
1) The inﬂ uence of using low weight concrete with good moisture 
buff ering capabilities
2) Not including moisture buff ering in the simulations of concrete. This 
was done by applying very large vapour diff usion resistances to the wall.
3) Low temperatures in combination with loose RH control. The set points 
for heating and cooling were set to 15 ºC and 20 ºC.
4) Using sandy soil in the simulations rather than the standard DS 418 
soil.
These simulations were, likewise, done for both type A and type B in 
order to determine the eff ect of the single parameter changes in relation 
to the museum being placed either above or below ground.
All energy calculations were based on the need for heating, cooling and 
ventilation. Energy factors are, thus, not included.

User pattern
Implementation
As a standard, IDA ICE does not contain the functions needed for these 
types of simulations. However, new custom functions were connected to 
the mathematical model of the zone in order to extend the capabilities of 
IDA ICE (Figure 106). Thus, in order to simulate a underground museum 
building the following changes were made of the mathematical model:
1) A dynamic user pattern based data from the national museum of 
Denmark and M/S Maritime museum of Denmark was added to the room. 
A room in IDA ICE has a ﬁ xed number of visitors but the user constant 
can be multiplied with a schedule in order to get a dynamic user pattern 
(Figure 107)
2) The HMwall component, described in section 06, was used for humidity 
buff ering.
3) Underground temperatures simulated in COMSOL was connected to 
the walls of the IDA ICE in accordance to the method described in section 
11.
4) A control system was designed in order to both humidify and dehumidify 
the air, as the standard AHU in IDA ICE were not capable of controlling 
humidity with both upper and lower limits (appendix 15). Furthermore, 
the system was made capable of changing the set points during the year 
according to a schedule.
These measures were connected to the zone component of IDA ICE 
as seen in Figure 106. Temperatures from COMSOL were connected to 
the HMwalls which were then connected to the zone. A user schedule 
connected to the zone would then determine the user pattern. Finally, 
the zone would be able to calculate the energy needed for heating, 
cooling and ventilation. Lighting was included in the simulations as a 
heat load, but the energy used for lighting (23.5 kWh/m2 per year) is 
not included in the calculated energy use of the buildings. Only heating 
cooling and ventilation needs are shown in the follow graphs. A more 
detailed description of the IDA ICE model is found in appendix 17.
==PAGE:94==
84
By combining daily patterns with monthly patterns a realistic schedule 
can be developed. The only source of Vr,day available was from the 
study at M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark. From this a user pattern 
for a week was made. Opening hours were 1100 to 1700 with some 
activity outside of this timespan as in the museum study. The schedule 
was therefore made from 8.00 to 18.00 to be able to include this 
activity. The Vr,hour was calculated as hourly values based on the 
8.00-18.00 timespan for each day of the week for one week. Thereby, 
the average Vr,hour value of each day (Figure 108) from 8.00-18.00 is 
1. This vector of values for one week is repeated for the whole year. 
Based on the visitor data from the National Museum of Denmark a 
normalised yearly schedule was calculated using Vr,day (Figure 109). The 
average value for the year is thus 1. By multiplying these two vectors 
a schedule was constructed including both the daily distributions from 
the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark and the yearly variation from the 
National Museum of Denmark (Figure 110). Afterwards, data from the 
Zoological Museum and the Geological Museum was used for additional 
user schedules.
User pattern method
One of the parameters in the simulation is the number of visitors present 
at the museum. Assuming that the number of visitors is constant can 
cause misleading results. The number of visitors will have a large eff ect 
on the cooling-, heating- and humidity loads. Therefore, large ﬂ uctua-
tions in the number of users present will result in a larger energy use if 
the indoor climate is to remain stable. 
Since very little data was available about actual occupancy densities 
at museums, the density of gallery 2 from the study at M/S Maritime 
Museum of Denmark was used as the reference value. The occupancy 
density parameter was then increased and decreased in order to study 
the inﬂ uence on the overall energy use of the building. The method for 
including the dynamic and highly ﬂ uctuation behaviour of visitors is seen 
in the below equation:

The visitor data from museum entrances did not account for employees. 
As seen in the study of the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark in section 
08, the employees can be a signiﬁ cant part of the people present at a 
museum especially during work days. The data from the study measured 
occupancy densities of both visitors and employees and by combining 
the datasets from the National Museum of Denmark with data from the 
M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark both employee presence, daily and 
yearly distributions were included. As the museum is closed on Mondays, 
it would result in zero values for most Mondays in the dataset from the 
National Museum. When multiplying the vectors, this would result in no 
activity on Mondays. A small amount of activity equal to an average 
Monday at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark was, thus, added to 
Mondays with no activity in the dataset from the National Museum.
However, since IDA ICE needs a schedule based on values of 0 to 1 a 
transformation of the schedule was needed. By dividing the values in all 
instances of time, with the highest total value during the year, Vr,max, a 
schedule for IDA ICE was made in which 1 is the highest value by:
(24) 
A value of 1 will then correspond to the highest number of people being 
present at the museum during the year. As the number of user in a zone. 
Since the area of the zone is known, the amount of users to be speciﬁ ed 
can be found by multiplying the area with the occupant density. The user 
constant for the zone is then calculated as:
(25)

The result of (24) and (25) will be multiplied in IDA ICE in order to deﬁ ne 
the number of users in the zone at any given time step.
(26)
When applying this method to full year datasets, large ﬂ uctuations 
occurs. This is caused by the instances where high ﬂ uctuations during 
a day is combined with high ﬂ uctuations during a year. This can be seen 
in the ﬁ gures on the following page. A week of normalised weekdays 
(Figure 112) with an maximum value of 4.9 is multiplied by the Vr,day for 
a year (Figure 113) having a maximum value of 6.4. The resulting vector 
(Figure 114) has a maximum value of 21.6. The Vr,max to use in both 
(24) and (25) is therefore 21.6. It must be noted that the Vr,max is not 
necessarily the product of the maximum value of each Vr schedule. In the 
end the Normalised schedule will be below 0.25 and only reach 1 once 
(Figure 115).

RESULTS
The results of the simulations are presented in a series of graphs on 
the following pages. The ﬁ rst series of simulations compared a building 
placed on the ground (type A), with a building placed just below ground 
(type B). With 150 mm insulation (Figure 116 ), the underground museum 
used less energy partly due to less cooling power needed, but mainly 
due to a lower heating demand. At 300 and 500 mm insulation (Figure 
117 and Figure 118) the diff erences between placing the museum on 
the ground or below was decreasing. At all insulation depths, the set 
point values had a big inﬂ uence which became increasingly inﬂ uential 
with more occupants. With 300 mm of insulation the diff erence between 
above or below ground was 2.1-3.1 kWh/m2 per year depending on the 
occupant density and the control system. In the case of using 150 mm 
insulation, few occupants caused a larger need for heating whereas many 
occupants resulted in a large need for ventilation. The occupancy density 
of around 0.01 pers/m2, therefore, caused the lowest total energy use.

When comparing the diff erences of being placed just below ground and 
four meters further into the ground (type C) large diff erences was seen in 
the case of having 150 mm insulation (Figure 119). At 300 mm insulation 
the diff erences were much smaller. Type C would cause a smaller energy 
use in most cases besides when having a very large occupancy density 
(Figure 120). However, with a insulation thickness of 300 mm the 
diff erences between type B and C were very small especially with larger 
occupancy densities. By using 500 mm, the diff erences between the two 
types was almost gone (Figure 121). With all insulation thicknesses, the 
set point strategy used as well as the number of occupants had a large 
inﬂ uence on the energy use. Additionally, the set points had a larger 
impact at larger occupancy densities.

The eff ect of increasing the amount of insulation from 300 mm to 500 
mm for type B was tested (Figure 122). At occupancy densities below 
0.023 pers/m2, 500 mm was the most energy saving option and it was 
increasingly beneﬁ cial with a lower number of people. However, above 
0.025 pers/m2 500 mm of insulation caused a larger energy use which 
increased with lighter occupancy density.
Single parameter variation
Simulations on type A and B buildings was done changing the yearly 
visitor pattern from the National Museum of Denmark to the Zoological 
Museum and the Geological Museum respectively (Figure 123). The daily 
pattern was in all simulations still based on M/S Maritime Museum of 
Denmark. Therefore, the mentioned changes would only impact which 
days people were present, not the number of people or the pattern 
during the day. For all single parameter test dense concrete, tight RH 
control, internal temperatures of 20-25 ºC and an occupancy density of 
0.005 pers/m2 was used.

The change of schedules did only have a limited inﬂ uence on the energy 
use. For all three cases the underground buildings used around 3 kWh/
m2 per year less than the above ground cases. Shifting museum pattern, 
however, only resulted in an increased use of around 0.7kWh/m2 per year. 
Using the original user pattern a series of single parameter analysis 
was done. For all cases, being placed below ground resulted in a smaller 
energy consumption than being placed above ground, but the magnitude 
of inﬂ uence varied depending on the parameters changed (Figure 124). 
Having loose requirements for the RH resulted in savings of 5.9 kWh/m2 
per year above ground and 6.1 kWh/m2 per year below ground and the 
diff erence between placing the museum above or below ground was 
kept at around 3 kWh/m2 per year.
The ability of LW concrete to absorb humidity caused less need for 
ventilation resulting in 1.2 kWh/m2 of energy saving underground.

However above ground the loss of thermal mass resulted in an increase 
of 9.4 kWh/m2 per year.
Not accounting for humidity accumulation in the dense concrete walls did 
in the standard case have very little inﬂ uence as only the above ground 
cased was changed by 0.1 kWh/m2 as seen in the "no buff ering" case.
What did have a large impact was lowering the internal temperatures to 
15-20 ºC all season in combination with a loose control of the RH. Both the 
above- and below ground buildings had a very low energy consumption 
of less than half of the consumption of the standard buildings.
Exchanging the standard soil with sandy soil caused a larger need for 
cooling but lowered the need for heating. In total, it resulted in energy 
savings of 1.7 kWh/m2 above ground and 0.8 kWh/m2 below ground.
DISCUSSION
Both above and below ground building were of the same construction. 
This resulted in the above ground buildings to have thick exterior concrete 
walls as well as interior concrete walls and thereby a large thermal mass. 
'
The large thermal mass can be a reason why the change of yearly user 
pattern did not have a larger inﬂ uence since the mass would even out 
the peaks of loads. It must be expected that in a lightweight structure, 
this parameter would cause larger diff erences. An underground museum 
in general will use less energy, based on the simulations. The magnitude 
of this energy saving will depend on the use and type of building. In the 
end, the adjustment of set points and number of users did have much 
larger inﬂ uence on the total energy use than being placed above or below 
ground. This notion of the importance of the set points for the indoor 
climate of the exhibition on the energy consumption is in accordance 
with a recent study (Kramer et al., 2015). 
==PAGE:101==
91
13 Discussion
From the simulations it can be seen that placing museums underground 
can potentially result in a lower energy use. Being placed further into 
the ground can as well be increasingly beniﬁ cial. But very thick insulation 
layers will minimize the eff ect of being placed above or below ground 
since the ambient conditions has less eff ect at lower U-values . 
All studies in this thesis has been done on the running energy use. A 
lower energy consumption during use might not be suffi  ciently energy 
saving to counter the potential large amount of energy used for placing 
the building underground. Such considerations are to be included in a Life 
Cycle Assessment (LCA) analysis in the design of speciﬁ c projects but are 
beyond the scope of this thesis. 
In some cases, parts of a museum are placed underground due to e.g. the 
lack of above ground space. In such cases, it is relevant to study which 
functions would beneﬁ t most from being placed underground. Above 
ground buildings have larger temperature ﬂ uctuations but a better 
access to daylight. Thus, exhibitions areas and storages can beneﬁ t from 
being placed underground. Functions such as auditoriums could beneﬁ t 
from the large thermal mass of the soil in order to minimize the large 
need for cooling. In such a case a small amount of insulation could be 
used as it would give a better access to the thermal properties of the 
soil and since low temperatures outside of the usage hours would be 
acceptable. Storage spaces has not been studied in this thesis but could 
as well beneﬁ t from the stable temperatures.
Boundary conditions
The method developed in this thesis could be used for many types of 
analysis. However, it is important to be aware of the shortcomings of the 
method. Since an estimation of the interior temperatures in COMSOL are 
needed, larger calculation errors will occur when the temperatures in the 
zone in IDA ICE are not similar to the assumed values. By making a new 
boundary condition in COMSOL, based on the IDA ICE results this error 
could be minimized. Unfortunately, this is a very time consuming method 
when performing the parameter analysis and it will also be diffi  cult 
to determine the inﬂ uence of the parameter changes if the boundary 
conditions are changed in the middle of a series of parameter changes. 
The method also has the limitation that the interior temperatures have 
to be estimated beforehand and controlled with rather small bandwidths 
in order for the interior temperatures in COMSOL and IDA ICE to be similar. 
Simulations could be done with larger diff erences between the interior 
temperatures used for the two programs, but it would result in more 
inaccurate results. Museums without any active systems to control the 
temperatures such as Cisternerne are thus not suitable for this method.
The use of COMSOL gives the possibility, when simulating a speciﬁ c 
project, to add additional heat sources to the ground as well as modelling 
soil proﬁ les in accordance to the building site. Thereby, much more realistic 
simulations can be done and the inﬂ uence of e.g.. nearby buildings can 
assessed.
Perfect mixing
It is important to remember that IDA ICE always has either a perfect 
mixing in the zone or a ﬁ xed stratiﬁ cation ratio for the whole simulation. 
However, in a real room, the air will never be perfectly mixed or be 
perfectly linearly stratiﬁ ed. Heat plumes above the occupants, surface 
temperatures of the walls, cold air from the ventilation systems, thermal 
stratiﬁ cation of the air etc. will result in temperatures never being the 
same everywhere in a room. Having a 5% allowed deviation in a real zone 
is therefore a much more strict requirement than having a 5% deviation 
in an IDA ICE model. 
Not having perfectly mixed air in reality is also an issue in regards to the 
control systems of museums. It is possible to have conﬂ icting signals 
from diff erent sensors in the same zone resulting in an enlarged energy 
consumption. Especially, if the set points for heating/cooling and humid-
iﬁ cation/dehumidiﬁ cation are very tight, then the uneven conditions in a 
room could result in an excessive use of the active systems and thereby 
an enlarged energy consumption. I order to ensure a better mixing, many 
==PAGE:102==
92
museums utilize very high ACH with a large amount of recirculated air. A 
previous study was based on an ACH of 7.5 h-1, with min 90% recirculated 
air (Kramer et al., 2015). Such a high ACH would even with a large degree 
of recirculation air result in a larger energy consumption. In the IDA ICE 
simulation no recirculation was used and the ACH varied depending on 
the CO2 concentration. That resulted in an ACH of 0.06-3.8 h-1.
Visitor data
The analysis of visitor presence was made based on entrance statistics 
from museums. But these data did not specify for how long visitors 
stayed and where they stayed during their visit. The study at M/S 
Maritime Museum of Denmark gave some indications of these questions. 
However, this study was only carried out at one museum for one month. 
Furthermore, the ﬂ ow of the occupants will be aff ected by the spacial 
planning of a museum. The exhibition area at the Maritime Museum is 
circular and it is possible to see the whole exhibition in one visit. Therefore, 
most visitors will walk the whole tour of the museum and only aff ect 
the occupancy density by staying in some zones for longer periods of 
time than in other zones. Larger museums such as the National Museum 
of Denmark with many rooms in many stories, it is not possible to see 
everything in one day. Visitors will have to choose what to see and this 
will make some parts of the museum more visited than others.

Preservation 
of exhibition

Figure 125.  Impact of the set points on diff erent professions in a museum
Building management data
It would have been relevant to analyse data from the active systems of 
museums. However, it has not been possible to acquire such data. For 
many of the museums contacted for the preparation for this thesis, no 
recording of the performance of the heating and ventilations systems 
were made. These museums had typically only access to a total energy 
bill. Some museums had to pay to get access to data of their energy use 
making the data acquisition more problematic. Other museums seemed 
to have better data but were not allowed to share it. Furthermore, it was 
experienced that asking for too much data resulted often in less chance 
of receiving any data from museums. Since visitor data was most crucial 
for this study, time and energy was in the end used on acquiring such 
data. 
It surprising, that many museums do not have any data on the performance 
of the building. Without proper data, it is diffi  cult to optimize the systems, 
potentially resulting in a too large energy consumption.
User schedule
In the simulations, the same weekly schedule is repeated throughout 
the year. Another method that could make even more realistic results 
would be to use stochastic variables to pick random examples of days 
of the week for each week. This method would result in a more random 
schedule being closer to real life behaviour. However, the use of stochastic 
parameters would result in a increase of the amount of simulations and 
thereby become too time consuming for the type of simulations done 
in this thesis. The user schedule used in the thesis did have a large ﬂ uc-
tuations and usefull in order to determine if museums consume more 
energy being above pr below ground. Another method speciﬁ c for each 
museum would be to further develop the statistical model for the eff ect 
of weather conditions on the number of visitors at museums with more 
parameters and a full year analysis rather than just the summer months. 
By including monthly patterns, events, user group patterns etc. a possibly 
more reﬁ ned model could be developed. 
==PAGE:103==
93
Figure 126.  Use of display cases in museums 
C
o
C
o
Scalability of methods
A problem with using a combination of two simulation programs with 
no automated link is that it is time consuming. To carry out a series of 
parameter investigations did result in much manual labour and could 
therefore be too expensive for an consulting company doing larger 
projects. Likewise, the HMwall needs to be incorporated in the standard 
level of IDA ICE to be usable for real building projects as the advanced 
level is very time consuming and makes it diffi  cult to go back and make 
changes in the model later on in the design process. However, the dynamic 
user schedules could easily be used in many other types of projects if 
data on the user behaviour exists. The in-build user schedule option in 
IDA ICE is only capable of specifying patterns one week at the time and 
therefore the method in this thesis are to be used if full year schedules 
are to be used. 
Set points
One of the interesting aspects of the set points for indoor climate at 
museums is, despite the fact that they can diff er depending on the type 
of exhibition, that the amount of diff erent ﬁ elds of work who are aff ected 
by these requirements is large. For curators the set points are relevant 
for the preservation but for the building management it is a matter of 
achieving the lowest energy use to achieve the required indoor climate 
(Figure 125). As the energy use is often a large part of the museum 
budget, it is highly relevant for accounting as well as the management. 
However, management could also have an interest in the resulting energy 
use from a standpoint of sustainability or PR values. Many curators will 
not have the knowledge on how much a change of the allowed ﬂ uctua-
tions will result in saved energy or saved money. This cross-disciplinary 
dependence on these speciﬁ cations makes optimizations more diffi  cult 
and could be an interesting topic for further research. During a design 
of a new museum or a new system even more interests are involved as 
advisors, entrepreneurs and architects could have diff erent agendas.
Creating diff erent zones with diff erent indoor climate requirements like 
in the competition brief for the Museum for Danish Resistance enables 
possible energy savings. Having these performance goals speciﬁ ed in the 
competition program rather than late in the process makes it possible for 
architects and engineers to implement them early in the design process 
and, thus, design more energy effi  cient buildings.
IDA ICE model
The model was a simpliﬁ ed construction with the same properties and 
control systems. The control systems could be optimized for the diff erent 
cases in order to lower the energy use. However, that would make it hard 
to determine if the changes of energy consumption were due to the 
parameter variations or diff erent control strategies. For the same reason, 
the construction was kept the same for all types and all parameter 
variations.
Relative humidity
Humidity buff ering only had a small eff ect in the exhibition area. Yet, in 
areas in which the RH is only allowed to ﬂ uctuate a few percent, even a 
small buff ering eff ect could be of inﬂ uence. But to be used in practice, full 
scale tests on easily cleanable humidity buff ering surfaces are needed 
as well as making products commercially available. However, humidity 
buff ering only has an eff ect if the ACH is kept relatively low. As a result, 
the humidity buff ering in the simulations had only little inﬂ uence in the 
total energy consumption.
==PAGE:104==
94
14 Conclusion
Placing museums underground can be used to lower the energy 
consumption. The stable temperatures of the ground results in a reduced 
need for the active systems to control the indoor climate. However, by 
increasing the amount of insulation, the eff ect of the exterior tempera-
tures will decrease and placing museums below ground has less inﬂ uence 
on the energy used. 
As seen in the COMSOL simulations the site will have an inﬂ uence on 
the performance of the museum. Diff erent soil types as well as nearby 
buildings will aff ect the temperatures of the soil. A newly build museum 
should have larger heating needs the ﬁ rst couple of years, but in time the 
adjacent soil will be heated and the need for heating by active systems 
will decrease.
User presence in museums are very diff erent from user presence in 
offi  ces and dwellings. Many factors will have an inﬂ uence on when 
people will visit museums and as a result the amount of visitors will vary 
greatly. It is, however, possible to detect patterns at diff erent museums 
and such studies could be used for dimensioning the active systems. A 
big challenge in doing these studies is the lack of data logged at small 
time intervals. The study at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark, thus, 
provided valuable knowledge, but is limited by the fact that the results 
cannot be compared to other measurements of similar type.
The term "museum" is used for a wide range of buildings where some 
focus on the preservation and exhibition of historical objects whereas 
others focus on user involvement and multimedia storytelling. This results 
in museums having very diff erent loads and indoor climate requirements. 
These requirements have proved to have a much larger inﬂ uence on the 
energy used than where the museum is placed.
Therefore, placing the museum underground is, one of the tools that can 
be used for providing a passive system capable of an indoor climate being 
as close to the requirements as possible. But the biggest step towards a 
sustainable museum is loosening the requirements for the indoor climate 
as well as utilizing eff ective active systems and control systems. Less 
stable temperature and RH conditions will have an eff ect on the museum 
collection. What this eff ect is and how large daily ﬂ uctuations can be 
allowed is not to be estimated by the engineer. To design a sustainable 
museum in regards to both energy use and preservation is a task in which 
specialist from the world of conservation and the world of engineering 
have collaborate in order to succeed.

01.  Programs
COMSOL
The COMSOL group was founded in 1986 in Sweden. The company 
developed COMSOL Multiphysics and in 1998 the ﬁ rst release of the 
program was made (COMSOL, 2016). This thesis work was done with 
the 5.1 version of the program in order to do heat transfer simulations. 
COMSOL is a validated program for heat transfer calculations (Gerlich 
et al., 2013) as well as being able to do a range of other types of cal-
culations like acoustics, chemical reactions, structural mechanics etc 
(COMSOL, 2016). The program can do these types of simulations in 4 
dimensions, being x,y,z and time, however only 2D simulations in time 
was used in this report.
IDA ICE
IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA ICE) is a building simulation tool 
developed by EQUA Simulation. It includes both active and passive 
systems, occupants, weather data and a large amount of other 
parameters. In this thesis the program will be used to make calculations 
of the total energy use and the indoor climate of buildings based on the 
ventilation, heating systems, user inﬂ uence etc. 
EQUA Simulation was founded and in 1998 and the ﬁ rst software from 
the company, IDA Indoor Climate and Energy, was released (EQUA, 2016). 
The version of the program used for this thesis was 4.6.2. IDA ICE has been 
validated in respect to a number of standards including EN 15255-2007 
a nd EN 15265-2007 (EQUA a ,2010) and ASHRAE 140 (EQUA b, 2010). 
Three diff erent user levels are available. The ESBO simpliﬁ ed level using 
a simpliﬁ ed interface with limited parameters, the standard level and the 
advanced level where the mathematical model behind the calculations 
can be changed (Kalamees, 2004)

Figure 128. user interface of IDA ICE

02.  Interview with Morten Ryhl-
Svendsen
Følgende interview af Morten Ryhl-Svendsen er foretaget af Mads Panﬁ l 
Jeppesen (indikeret med fed).
Der er forskellige setpoints for forskellige materialer og så er der 
indeklima standarderne. Hvad gør man når man har forskellige krav 
for brugere og udstillingsgenstande? Det virker som om at man pri-
oriter gæster ret højt i forhold til konserveringsindeklimaet?
Det er selvfølgeligt vigtigt at gæsternes komfort er i orden ikke. Man 
er der for gæsternes skyld. I det omfang hvor det ikke er i konﬂ ikt med 
bevaring af samlingerne, der er alt jo ﬁ nt. Og så har man nogen genstande 
der kræveret helt specielt klima som ligger helt udenfor alt menneskeligt 
komfort og det må man jo så fx løse lokalt, det kan være i nogen særlige 
montre. Det kan være nogen særlige genstande der skal opbevares tørt 
fx Det klarer man lokalt kan man sige. Men altså typisk vil det være sådan 
for en hel samling at 90% af genstandende de har det sådan set ﬁ nt 
indenfor komfortzonen. Menneskelig komfort det er nok primært tem-
peraturmæssigt i virkeligheden, og der har man et rimeligt stort spillerum. 
Hvis der så ud over det er genstande som har et fugtbehov, så spiller 
der ikke så stor en rolle at man også styrer dette brugerkomfortmæssigt. 
Det er bare en teknisk komplikation og koster lidt mere energi. Fordi det 
et i virkeligheden drejer sig om, der er ikke et universialklima som er det 
bedste for alt kulturarv i verden. Materialer og genstande er jo forskellige. 
Så det første, hvis maner på et museum, hvor der er en samling og man 
skal bygge et nyt hus og man skal ﬁ nde ud af hvad skal indeklimaet være 
der så skalman kigge på samlingen. Der skal være nogen fagfolk der 
kender deres samling. I det her tilfælde der er de så en kulturhistorisk 
samling. Det er genstande for Zoologisk museum, Geologisk museum og 
botanisk samling. Og der må de samlingsfolk der er der sætte sig ned og 
sige hvad der fællesnævneren for vores samling, hvad kan vi tillade os 
at stå frit i et rum hvor der er almindeligt indeklima og hvad skal have 
specielt indeklima i montre eller særlige sektioner. Man kan jo også 
designe et udstillingsmiljø op sådan at du har særlige klimazoner. Der er 
jo nogen steder hvor man går igennem en sluse eller lign og så kommer 
man igennem et område hvor der er særlig fugtsyring eller lignende hvor 
det kunne være man havde nogen meget sarte middelaldermalerier eller 
et eller andet. Så sådan noget kan du se eksempler på rundt omkring på 
museer i verden. Så man bruger det altså, i stor stil faktisk, indenfor visse 
grænser, der vil også stadigt være udsving, der vil være rigtigt mange 
mennesker og pludselig så er der ikke nogen. 
Hvor udbredt er det at man zoneopdeler det åbne rum som ikke er 
udstillingsbokse?
Hvis du går ind på Nationalmuseet inde i København og går ind i forhallen. 
Hvis du drejer til højre så går du ind i oldtidsudstillingen. Det første rum 
fungerer som en sluse lige når du kommer in i oldtidsudstillingen. Så er 
du så faktisk i en særligt fugtstyret zone. Og det er styret med glasdøre 
og sådan noget. Sådan så du går igennem det. Forlader det. Og når du 
går ud i publikumsarealer igen, gange osv så er du ude af den zone igen. 
Hvis du går til venstre og går ind i det der hedder Egmonthallen der hvor 
der er særudstillinger (man går sådan lige op af en trappe). Det er sådan 
et helt særligt rum hvor man kan styre et hvilket som helst klima man 
ville ønske. Det er til særudstillinger hvor man får alt muligt forskelligt 
ind. Det kan være at det ene er... Så låner man møbler fra Venedig som 
skal have forholdsvis høj luftfugtighed, og så har man det. Og året efter 
så er det noget kinesisk bronzestatue som skal stå meget tørt. Sådan et 
eller andet, det kan man så styre helt separat fra gang til gang. Så det er 
også en zone for sig. På Glyptoteket når du går ind i den franske impres-
sionistafdeling, der går du også igennem et sæt døre som deﬁ nerer en 
klimazone. På Louisiana når du går på den der gang, hvor man kommer 
ind i de første områder, der er en lang passage hvor man går langs haven, 
det er jo et almindeligt klima som i en villa eller hvad som helst, men hvis 
du så træder ind i udstillingsområderne så er der faktisk også nogen 
glasdøre, så træder du ind i en mere fugtkontrolleret zone. Og så det 
inderste skal, hvis man har noget der er helt specielt er helt følsomtog 
har et helt særligt klimakrav så vil det være i et montre så du kan styre 
det. Det vil typisk være noget der skal ligge meget tørt. Det kunne være 
==PAGE:117==
107
et mineral eller sådan noget hvor man bløder salte ud ved høj fugtighed 
eller andre sådan lidt mere specielle absurde ting. Faktisk på Zoologisk 
museum, hvis du går over i den der forholdsvis nye udstilling, der har de 
den der (er det ikke en iskerne?). Det har et eller andet frysemontre, et 
eller andet frossent apparat. Jeg har også set andre steder hvor man har 
en borekerne fra indlandsisen. Den kræver så selvfølgeligt et helt særligt 
klima og det må man så klare meget lokalt.
Jeg snakkede med EKJ for at høre hvad de havde tænkt omkring zon-
er, med at dele det op alt efter hvad der var. Og de virkede ikke helt 
som om at Naturhistorisk museum var nået langt nok til at man ville 
tage det med ind i sine overvejelser så man kunne programmere 
bygningen i forhold til det. Så det de lidt søgte var noget hvor man 
havde mulighed for det hele, overalt. Men det giver selvfølgeligt 
mere energibesparelse hvis man kan lave rummet efter hvad det 
skal kunne.
Ja man kan så sige, at selvom jeg sagde at der ikke et klima i hele verden 
der bare dækker alt. Så er der selvfølgeligt alligevel visse klimaområder 
der dækker bedre end andre. Hvis man tager det som udgangspunkt at 
man skal opfylde temperaturkomfort for mennesker, så er det så givet 
at man skal det der 20, 23 måske 25 grader om sommeren, hvis man 
så ligger der. Så fugtmæssigt så kan langt, langt de ﬂ este materialer i 
denne verden, så har de det udmærket et eller andet sted imellem 40 
og 60% relativ fugtighed. Så så længe man er indenfor det der bånd, så 
går det ikke helt galt. Og der kan du smide 95% af alle samlinger ind. Og 
det har noget at gøre med hvis du kommer meget over de der 60% så 
nærmer du dig grænsen for skimmel ved en 70 måske 75% og der er 
også sådan noget som salte og nogen kemiske reaktioner der begynder 
at gå stærkere når der er meget vand til stede. Og hvis det bliver for tørt 
så er der nogen fysiske forhold: ting der bliver for sprøde og udtørret 
simpelthen, mister noget mekanisk ﬂ eksibilitet. Så sådan et sted mellem 
40-60% så er man sådan ca. hjemme og så er det godt hvis det er stabilt, 
men det må godt svinge på årstidsbasis. Du kan godt tillade at du har 
40% om vinteren og 60% om sommeren. Ligesom har den der årsvaria-
tion, i stedet for fuldstædigt kritikløst aff ugter og befugter til 50%.
Ja jeg kan se at der er nogen af de der standarder, eller guidelines, 
der har en ”deviation” for 24 timer. Men jeg tænkte hvem er det så 
der kommer til at vælge om vi siger 45-55 eller 40-60.
Det er museet. Det er museet som ejer samlingen. Dem som betaler festen 
bestemmer musikken. De kender deres samling, må man gå ud fra og de 
beslutter. Så er der ikke nogen lov. Det er guidelines. Der er ikke nogen 
standarder der er ophøjet til lovtekst. Hverken i dansk standard eller in-
ternationalt. Så nu kender jeg bedst nationalmuseet, da det er der jeg har 
arbejdet tidligere. Der har man det der hedder bevaringsafdelingen og der 
har man nogen ansvarlige. Det er dem der skal høres i byggesager eller 
nyopstiller udstilllinger eller lign. som kendersamlingerne og som tager 
stilling til: ”Den her samling der er det møbler, der tager vi stilling til det 
og det” og ”den her samling der er det udstoppede dyr og der skal vi være 
opmærksom på det og det”.
Så I havde 40 til 60% relativ fugtighed generelt og så havde i nogen 
enkelte zoner hvor i...
Ja det er udgangspunktet i nationalmuseets samlingsmanual. De har 
sådan en udstillingsmanual. Sådan et internt arbejdsdokument. Når man 
haren ny sag, så har man den at køre efter Det er udgangspunktet. Trin 
2 på manualen der er at se på det aktuelle genstande der skal udstilles. 
Er der nogen der kræver noget særligt? Og så vil man så rette ind efter 
det. Jeg mener at på Nationalmuseet der siger man temperaturer max 
25 grader og 40-60% relativ fugtighed og så mulighed for specialklima 
hvis man har behov for det. Og det er også det man i de her guidelines på 
en eller anden måde ﬁ nder som fællesnæver. Typisk når man kigger ud, 
også hvis man kigger på museers egne krav. Så vil du se at kunstmuseer 
de har strengere krav end naturhistoriske eller kulturhistoriske, men 
undtagelse af selvfølgeligt de allerﬁ neste skatte, men der er sådan en 
tendens til at kunstmuseer der skal være lidt ﬁ nere eller man skal passe 
lidt bedre på tingene og det er måske også sartere materialer. Det har i 
hvert fald været præget af en verden med auktioner og gallerier og hvor 
tingene også har en pris. En høj pris. Så typisk så vil du se at kunstmuseer 
de vil sige 45-55% og det kulturhistoriske museum de siger så 40-60 
==PAGE:118==
108
eller 35 til 65. De er sådan lidt mere ”relaxed”. Selvom de i princippet kan 
være fuldstændigt de samme materialer der er udstillet begge steder. 
Men det har også lidt at gøre med pris og værdi, forsikring. Man vil også 
typisk kræve strammere krav når man låner en ting ud end man egentligt 
opfylder hjemme i ens eget museum for at være på den sikre side. Så 
det er lidt en anden historie. Jeg vil tro at når man kigger ned over det 
så ligger fællesnævneren et eller andet sted mellem 40 og 60% relativ 
fugtighed. Og så med det forbehold: Specialklima for specielle genstande. 
Og specielle genstande det kan være noget som fx mineraler der skal 
opbevares meget tørt ellers så smelter de nærmest, saltene løber ud. 
Der er et eller andet med Pyrith eller… Ja der er sådan nogen særlige 
ting. Det kan også være noget som ting af elfenben og tænder. Tand-
materiale sprækker hvis det bliver for tørt. Det kræver egentligt sådan 
ret stabil relativ fugtighed. Det kræver lidt sådan noget. Og så kan det 
være nogen materialer som er nedbrudt i forvejen. Hvis man har noget 
som er meget gammelt eller som tidligere har været angrebet. Der er fx 
den her øvre grænse på 60-65% det er for at man skal være sikker på at 
der ikke kommer skimmelsvamp at der ikke gror mug på tingene og det 
kan der sagtens gøre hvis det har været for fugtigt et stykke tid. Den 
gælder egentligt for nye friske materialer, hvis nu du har en genstand 
som har oplevet skimmeludbrud før: en bog eller et eller andet. Så hvis 
den ikke er blevet renset ordentligt, desinﬁ ceret ordentligt så sidder der 
sporer i som ret nemt vil kunne blomstre op igen. Så den er faktisk mere 
følsom for høj luftfugtighed. Så hvis man har ting som tidligere har været 
angrebet. Så vil man typisk sætte den øvre grænse lavere for at være på 
den sikre side, 55% fx Det kan også være andre typer af skade. Og så er 
der, nu snakkede vi om de her klimagrænser for en ting af gangen, men 
i den virkelige verden så arbejder ting jo på en gang, du har jo ikke kun 
temperatur og relativ luftfugtighed. Du har også lys og luftforurening. 
Du har 117 klimafaktorer, skadedyr. Og der er ting der kan gå i synergi 
og forstærke hinanden. Så hele lysområdet, det er jo et… Jeg ved ikke hor 
meget du kommer ind i?
Nej jeg har levet et undersøgelser eller litterature review for at ﬁ nde 
ud af hvad er det forskellige parametre og hvad er de vigtigste. Og 
eftersom det virker som om de ﬂ este museer virker til gerne vil sty-
re lyset elektrisk i stedet for dagslyset, så er det en faktor som jeg 
skriver om, men ikke kommer så meget videre med. Men det virker 
som om at man nogen steder (og det ved jeg ikke hvor meget man 
bruger) men at det at man ganger lux op med timer og så fordeler 
dem ud.
Jo men det arbejder man meget med. Fordi altså den nemme måde det 
er jo bare at man sætter nogen faste lysgrænser. Hvor det så kun er in-
tensiteten. Der har man så traditionelt snakket om meget lysfølsomme 
materialer. Det er så 50 lux som er meget mørkt og medium det er så et 
par hundrede lux og så er der materialer der kan tåle meget, sten eller 
sådan noget. Det kan så bare få fuldt blus.
Men bare for at være sikker, for at være 100% på at jeg forstår det 
rigtigt. Så man tager et materiale fx der til 200 lux (1 peger i stand-
arden). Tager man så bare det? Og siger at det er hvad den kan få lov 
til i 24 timer?
Det er nemlig et meget godt spørgsmål for der   er nemlig forskel på om 
det er i 12 timer eller 24 timer. De har grænser de er jo typisk sat under at 
man har sådan en normal museumssituation, dvs der er lys på ca 8 timer 
om dagen, 6 dage om ugen, 52 uger om året. Så det er en lysdosis for et 
museums åbningstid.
Så jeg skal bare gange det her med 8 så ﬁ nde ud hvad er det tillade-
lige lux i løbet af et døgn?
Ja dosis. Og så er det helt rigtigt som du siger at udstiller man det i kortere 
tid så kan man egentligt godt tillade sig at skrue op for intensiteten hvis 
man forkorter tiden. Hvis man nu siger den her gwnstand er faktisk kun 
udstillet 4 timer om dagen så kan man have 400 lux i stedet for 200 lux i 
princippet. Men det har jo indﬂ ydelse på dit klima på den måde at det er jo 
en ret betydelig varmekilde. Nu er der selvfølgeligt LED lys mere og mere, 
men det er noget man har kæmpet med førhen. Ikke bare varmeafgivels-
en fra lys men fra alt muligt AV udstyr. Sådan et moderne udstillingsrum 
det er jo fyldt med monitorer og hvad ved jeg. Og der er altså en eff ekt.
==PAGE:119==
109
Ja jeg var oppe i Søfartsmuseet her i forrige uge og snakke med 
deres driftsmand. Og arkitekterne havde arbejdet med at få rene 
overﬂ ader uden for mange ting og ventilationselementer. Men så 
når det blev taget i brug så blev det jo smadret til med projektorer 
og lydanlæg og ting og sager. Så der er jo godt nok meget gear der 
bliver hægtet på sådan et rum.
Ja det er jo en stor eff ekt som så måske skal køles. Fjernes i hvert fald på 
en eller anden måde.
Jeg syntes genetligt det er lidt sjovt. Du har jeg så godt nok også ta-

meget velisoleret skal og så har man jorden som varmelager og kuldelager. 
Og det er uisoleret (imod jorden). Så det her er buff er (jorden) og det her 
er forsinker (klimaskærmen) mod klimapåvirkninger udefra. Det er ikke et 
problem med bygningsreglementet så længe bygningen ikke er opvarmet. 
Og det er nemlig hele humlen her at magasinrummet er uopvarmet, så 
har man en lille aff ugter der recirkulerer og træder til hvis det bliver for 
fugtigt. Belastningen er ikke så stor, det er ikke så mange timer om året. 
Og det bliver ikke for tørt fordi temperaturen falder passende. Det er 
noget med hvis man i Danmark, hvis man har en temperatur som er 6 
grader over udetemperaturen, så passer luftfugtigheden ca. til muse-
umsniveu året igennem. Det betyder så også at om sommeren så skulle 
man varme op til 6 grader. Det ville være lidt tosset så der aff ugter men 
i stedet for. Men så længe du ikke varmer kommer du ikke i klemme med 
bygningsreglementet. Det ville man gøre hvis nu man sagde at man ville 
have 12 grader derinde. Så skulle man i hvert fald argumentere på en helt 
særlig måde.
Det var i hvert fald noget jeg tænkte gav menig at kigge på. At se 
hvad betyderisoleringsmængden i forhold til at bruge den kapacitet 
og temperatur som ligger i jorden
Så det her er simpelthen beton på jorden og så med et kapilarbrydende 
lag.
Lidt ligesom Tegners museum som også har lidt af det samme og så 
endda også uden lys. Bare med nogen ovenlysvinduer.
Ja det er vidst rigtigt. Det er godt nok mange år siden jeg har været 
deroppe. Ja men sådan nogen glasfriser oppe i taget.
==PAGE:125==
115
Ja noget translucent, hvide ﬂ ader i loftet hvor lyset kommer ind. 
Men er selvfølgeligt også en anden udstilling end så mange andre, 
og de har også lukket ind over vinteren. Ligesom Cisternerne og så 
har de ting der kan tåle en høj luftfugtighed. Det er jo så meget rum-
met der deﬁ nerer hvilken udstilling der kan være.
En sidste ting. Vi er enige om brugerne vægtes meget højt i forhold 
til selve konserveringen.
Det kommer an på museet. Forskellige museer vil give dig forskelligt svar. 
Nogen siger at vores gæster det er det vigtigste mens andre siger at 
samlingen er det primære og så må gæsterne ﬁ nde sig i at der er mørkt 
og koldt og alt muligt ikke. Så det er der ikke entydigt (svar på). Men jeg 
er da sikker på at i sådan et moderne museum der vægter gæsternes 
komfort meget, men samlingen er også vigtig ikke. Nu kan jeg ikke huske 
de der tal, men der er jo en af de 5 eller 7 største samlinger i verden 
ikke. Det er jo en meget speciel ﬁ n samling vi har i København, især den 
Zoologiske. Så den vil de sikkert syntes er vigtigt også.
Interviewet afsluttes.
