03.  Interview with Tim Padﬁ eld
The following is an excerpt of the conversation with Tim Padﬁ eld, the 
freelance consultant in preventive conservation and author of several of 
the articles referred to in this thesis.
Usually in the museum design you would start with the speciﬁ cation 
And one part of the speciﬁ cation is the climate, and until recently the 
energy consumption hasn’t been part of the speciﬁ cation, it is something 
new because eventually museums were prestige operations where the 
money for running them, didn’t matter, it just ﬂ owed in by some magic 
mechanism. But now we think energy matters, ﬁ rstly, based on the 
conscience. Saving the planet from itself and from us. And that actually 
that’s just a sentimental argument because the number of museums 
there will ever be in the world compared to dwellings like this is very tiny. 
That they use a disproportional amount of energy does actually not have 
any inﬂ uence at all. What I have encountered is that energy matters now 
because of running costs and therefore they have a much more pragmatic 
obsession with energy because if the save energy they can hire another 
curator or another guard or whatever they are short of. So my view is 
that you have a sort of moral speciﬁ cations in museums, being rather 
pompous institutions in their nature. And therefore the have a reason to 
show the world how they can save energy.
….
The conservation atmosphere is rather easily stated really. It has to be 
around 50% RH, except Cisternerne that seems to have around 100% 
humidity. But most museums museum speciﬁ cations that come your way 
as an architect and engineer. A rule of thumb can therefore be 50% RH 
and temperature, because it is an exhibition space, must be for human 
comfort. Being from 18-25 or so. In that range. Also museum speciﬁ ca-
tions have had a strange dogma about range of change, so you ﬁ nd that 
many standards or guidelines states that you should rely on RH of more 
than 10 percentage points in a day or more than 2 degrees. 
…
You have to understand that the scientiﬁ c underpinning of museum 
conservation is quite slender, at that’s not because there aren’t any 
good people in the ﬁ eld there’s just very few. So you have to look with 
some scepticism on what is stated to be good practice. Unfortunately, 
the standards and guides tend to be taken as speciﬁ cations. Because 
the person who decides is a manager or director who is not a technical 
person. So he takes the advice and turns it into a must have. And this 
is currently a problem, museums have a variety of standards, some with 
more authority than others. … Basically you end up with 50% +/- 10 if 
that’s is what you are being asked to do.
…
There a good deal of model thinking in museums. The national museum 
for instance, when they lend out they put in much tighter speciﬁ cations 
than in their home. That’s the ﬁ rst thing. When these objects come back 
from the exhibition they don’t trust the other institutions. They are afraid 
of insects. So they take the object a put it into a box at plunge the tem-
peratures very fast to minus 35 degrees for a day, and then up again 
to room temperature. So the fun thing is that their speciﬁ cation for an 
exhibition might plus or minus one degree. But when the object comes 
back its plus or minus 40 degrees. That’s the diff erence. Their treatment 
is in a good cause. It’s not careless, its deliberately bad. You have to face 
this complete mental confusion that seems to permeate the profession. 
There’s much mythology almost medieval thought of things.
…
I do have some experience with the control algorithms, and they can 
be extremely wasteful. Even when they are trying not to be, that is the 
interesting thing. Some are just plain mistakes.
… 
There is a lot of risk analysis (of museums) where you cannot follow the 
logic, but the outcome is always the same. The main risk is thieving of 
staff  and then ﬁ re and ﬂ ood. By the time you have done that its 99% of 
the risk. So the climate control going wrongs turns out is nothing at all. 
...
There a thin that makes the museum odd, its only occupied for 8  hours 
a day, when we talk the exhibition. And that has a eff ect because you 
do have a considerably load but it’s for a very distinctive amount of time, 
==PAGE:127==
117
and usually in the middle of the day. So the load on the air-conditioning 
system, that’s is from human CO2 and water as well as heat and lighting. 
… That has some interesting side eff ects. Mainly where do you get the 
energy from. Solar voltaic panels are getting so cheap. And they mesh so 
well with museum metabolism, because the museum are mostly visited 
in the summer. That’s when people move around. Many museums lose 
completely in the winter. So solar panel output coincides beautifully with 
demands of museums particularly.
…
The other matter is as you said connected to the thermal inertia of the 
museum. As you say that’s completely uninsulated to the ground. Then it 
will maintain very stable climate (temperature) which will be uneconomic 
for the ﬁ rst ﬁ ve years because here we start off  with ground at 9 
degrees and you're minimum for human comfort is 18 and you get build 
yourself up from there. And the normal thoughtless way would be to 
pour on insulation at the economical solution. But in fact it is not as it 
heats up the temperature gradient gets smaller and smaller and heat 
ﬂ ow becomes smaller and smaller. A ﬁ ne example of this is Svalbard. It’s 
a storage and it’s the international seed bank it runs a -18 degrees. But 
Svalbard underground is minus 5. So it’s actually cooled, its mechanically 
cooled and costs to begin with. That have to have the most enormous 
cooling capacity to get it done. But gradually this fog of cold spread out 
through the rock … So the Svalbard engineers gradually removed their 
cooling operators and its pottering along with very little cooling and no 
insulation.
…
I have tried to introduce the concept of design limit that people don’t 
understand. They tend to resort to items of unacceptable risk because 
these are internationally renowned irreplaceable objects there the 
slightest exertion over 25 is intolerable. And when you ask them what 
will happen. Well nothing actually. The things you keep at a museum even 
the wax ﬁ gures can go up to 32 degrees and it slightly increases the 
degree of chemical reactions very often, but that gets lost in the noise 
when it gets cold in the winter. So there’s a large element of misunder-
standing. Also an element of being a bit holy an absolutist, because its 
irreplaceable. But chemistry applies to everything independently. It is 
entirely careless of your sentimental values.
…
Most museum objects are in glass cases and this protects them not so 
much against temperatures changes but against humidity change. If you 
get a spike in temperature it will get right through. But the spike of water 
vapour from a lot of people, that won’t. Therefore, the speciﬁ cations of 
the air conditioning will have to include a speciﬁ cation of the enclosures. 
So you have the three environments. You have the outside weather, 
then you have you’re inside building and then you will have your object 
in however is displayed. Very few objects are now displayed in open. It’s 
largely physical security, not so often climatic security…
You get more stability in the underground museum especially if you make 
it with massive rock that gives you massive thermal inertia. And that 
leaves with the humidity control … that’s always a one way process its 
always removal. There are only sources of water vapour indoors there are 
no sinks. So you will have this very unsymmetrical system. And you can 
do it with ventilation …
…
For most of the year the outside temperatures are very low and therefore 
you bring that air in the RH will be very low because you will be heating 
it up. The problem is in summer thing will work against you. Since you 
have an underground museum is cool. And therefore any air taken will 
risen RH because it is cooled at the same it is the time where people 
are coming in. … In principal you can’t take warm air in and get condensa-
tion on the way in (if air is taken through a long underground ductwork)… 
there are buildings for thousands of year that have been using earth 
cooling tunnels.
…
The underground museum could almost be self-heating depending on 
the amount of people, I don’t know.
…
Museum design goes to the star architect, Storage we can manage 
because its basically a dull building. We are good at that. But museums 
==PAGE:128==
118
are basically designed in a very impractical way because they are prestige 
buildings. And there you regard them as, the building is a sculpture on 
its own and then the facilities will have to ﬁ t in and the energy. Many 
modern museums are unsuited to be museums. It’s a trip of the architect. 
Added by the committee. There are some ﬁ ne exceptions really of 
modest museums that work very well and they have gone the election 
committee.
…
There been a recent revolution called the light emitting diode. The main 
reason for museums in a temperate climate like Denmark is air conditioned 
is heat generated by light. Artiﬁ cial light or indeed by daylight coming in. 
And the light effi  cient diode is so eff ective in relation to its predeces-
sors. But it’s a revolution that has not really worked its way through yet. 
Museums still look at it with a certain degree of suspicion.  … But looking 
5 years ahead it will be universal. The National museum has been toying 
with it forever but they say “Well we can’t aff ord to replace the existing 
lamps”. The problem is the capital investment and the running costs. They 
can’t aff ord to have something that costs less to run.
…
Lighting in risk analysis is called a risk factor … but it’s not a risk it’s a 
certainty. 
…
If it’s too simple, it upsets to many things like standards. Standards are 
changed but not a people haven’t noticed. And there are all of these 
engineer’s defensive of their everyday income, maintenance, contracts 
etc. By the time you add all of this together the entire success rate (of 
simplifying the systems) is very small.
…
My guess is over the whole year there is actually no relative humidity 
load. The problem is that we have no of buff ering the relative humidity 
in the way we do with temperature... Heat store happens on a scale 
10 times the moisture storage. In other words, if you have a brick wall 
that you use as a heat sink. And you calculate it to even it out then out 
need that much brick and if you’ll have no more brick it won’t help, that’s 
enough. If you do a similar calculation with humidity, you’ll ﬁ nd out it not 
possible with the materials we know about. It limited by the diff using of 
the humidity of where it’s going to be absorbed and desorbed… As a rule 
of thumb you can say that humidity buff ering is ten times less eff ective 
than thermal buff ering. And thermal buff ering works. It’s an everyday 
phenomenon that’s does happen. Humidity buff ering remains an exotic 
concept. There's no catalogue item called a humidity buff er. The green 
people say these earth buildings are wonderful because the control the 
moisture but that’s just an illusion really when you measure it. It’s just the 
outer millimetres that receives the variation from day to day. So it true in 
principal but in practice it does not have any eff ect.
You basically have two rates of reaction for humidity buff ering. The slow 
rate is useless, there’s no way of doing it. But the fast rate does work. So 
if you'll have a shower in the bathroom its does make a diff erence on the 
condensation on the mirror if you got a porous surface in the shower. If 
you use exposed paper, wallpaper you’ll deﬁ nitely get less condensation 
so it’s having an eff ect to that peak load. But the problem with that is it 
not really quantiﬁ able enough yet. 
==PAGE:129==
119
04.  Resume of conversations with 
Michael Krabbe Kiær
The following is a resume of conversations with Michael Krabbe Kiær, 
building service manager at the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark.
The systems of the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark is only controlled 
by temperatures. But that is a result of the type of objects being exhibited. 
If it were an art museum or the alike there would be stricter set points. 
The museum has been surprisingly good at keeping the same tempera-
tures without the systems running. The heating for many areas in the 
museum is turned off  in the night, but still the temperature has often only 
dropped about one degree when the systems are turned on again next 
morning. The museum does therefore not have run the systems with a 
at low rate, as is often done, but can turn it completely off . The notion 
off  the large variation in the user presence is especially interesting since 
maximum user load is dimensioning for some of the system components. 
The weekly pattern of user presence from the National Museum of 
Denmark is similar to what is experienced at the M/S Maritime Museum 
of Denmark. They have closed many Mondays and recognizes Tuesday 
as the day with the least visitors. The amount of visitors is then slowly 
increasing during the week and tops in the weekend. They do, however, 
not make schedules for the employees based on user statistics or some 
museum presence standard but rather use gut feeling. A certain amount 
of employees is assigned to certain task like ticket sales, café etc. to 
handle an average load of visitors. They cannot just have people working 
at certain hours, but need to make regular schedules for the employees. 
In holidays when more visitors are at the museum, more staff  is taken in 
to a number of tasks like ticket sales. Since the museum is very new and 
thus a lot of people are visiting it, as to be expected with any new public 
area. The admission is decreasing for each year and are still to fall in to a 
regular pattern.
The museum is divided into 5 zones and as activated based on the sensor 
measuring the condition furthest away from the set point. Michael 
is wondering if it would be beneﬁ cial if the museum was divided into 
more zones. Since the zones are rather large, heating for a large area is 
thus engaged even if only a small part of that area is actually in need of 
heating. By having smaller zones, it would thus be possible to have the 
systems run less and thus save energy. The challenge when designing 
indoor climate in the M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark is that the public 
area of the museum is basically one big room with no doors or walls to 
isolate any area. It is only possible to such a museum due to the type 
of exhibition and it wouldn’t be possible if it was and art museum with 
sensitive objects like paintings. It can however be a challenge to control 
3500 m2 of museum using 5 AHU. There is no control of the systems 
based on CO2. It would however be interesting especially when dealing 
with larger groups. If all visitors are walking together in one group, then 
the need for ventilation is very local. CO2 detectors could detect the group 
presence and lower the ventilation rate of all other zones in the museum 
to a minimum level. This could save a lot of energy. In general Krabbe Kiær 
has experienced that the way to measure and where to measure can give 
very diff erent results in regards to whether or not the set points are met.
The bridges going across the dock is a sensitive area. The surface to 
volume ratio is rather high and is connected to the ambient all the way 
around since it is not touching the ground. This means these bridges 
are often much colder in the winter than the rest of the exhibition area 
“inside” the dock, but are still connected to the same AHU. Therefore, 
the diff erent conditions of the bridges and the exhibition area leads to 
excess heating due to these areas being in the same ventilation zone. 
Michael notes that more zones are more expensive when being build, 
but will lower the running costs of the system. But the zones have to 
be thought through in terms of the climate of each zone. Maybe this is 
relevant even more for underground than above ground buildings, since 
the diff erence of climate between areas towards the ground and areas 
towards windows are very diff erent. Especially the cuts through the 
building like the bridges are very exposed to the ambient conditions. The 
bridges cause another challenge due to the ﬂ ooring and ceiling being 
==PAGE:130==
120
inclined. Since hot air goes upward the top of these rooms are much hotter 
than the bottom. Since the auditorium is placed in one of the bridges it is 
prone to big temperature diff erences as well as large deviations in users 
present. If the auditorium is full then the AHU will have problems to keep 
the set points. Having all systems running on maximum load will as well 
cause much noise which is not wanted especially during a presentation 
where the acoustics is very important. Since the ﬂ oor in the museum 
is slightly sloping downwards through the museum certain thermody-
namically challenges occur. The required throw length diff er all the way 
through the museum due to diff erent ceiling heights.
In regards to light there is a tendency for the museums to prefer electrical 
lighting since it can be harmful to certain objects. The M/S Maritime 
Museum of Denmark does not have a lot of sensitive objects on display, 
but there is examples of the design being altered due to the presence of 
daylight. One painting placed by the exhibition designer was moved by 
the conservator in order to get away from the windows. An estimated 
60% of the museum area is not exposed to daylight and the light can 
thus be determined 100% by the designers and conservators. The lux 
level used varies through the exhibition. Spotlights are used to be able 
to vary the light levels and to minimize spill light from one display to 
another. Florescent light tubes are placed in the perforated celling, but 
is only used for when the museum is being cleaned, a new exhibition 
is set up or in case of ﬁ re. The perforated plates in the ceiling are used 
to hide all installations and cause a decrease of the performance of the 
ventilation and work lighting of around 30%. This is done according to 
the wishes of the architect to get a cleaner look. However, it results in 
a higher energy use and since the exhibition designer either way place 
spots, loudspeakers, projectors, mounting hooks etc., this clean look is 
not showing when the museum is in use. 
The display boxes are used to protect the objects from the audience. 
There is no individual control of the temperature, RH, smoke detectors 
or the alike.  
During the summer months most energy is needed. This is due to the 
museum being open for more hours during the day and are open in the 
Mondays as well. Little energy is used on cooling and is mainly provided 
by ground water. The systems is prepared for seawater cooling but it 
was considered to expensive. Michael Krabbe Kiæer have been told from 
colleagues that sea water cooling is very high maintenance, since the 
heat exchanger and ﬁ lters in the sea often needs to be cleaned.

06.  Humidity calculation in IDA ICE
The following is an explanation of the code used in IDA ICE for calculating 
humidity. In IDA ICE humidity calculation is done in the zone, being a very 
long code calculation many parameters. The zone code has as well very 
little description of what is being calculated and why. Thus 3 parts of 
the long zone code (in bold) has been put together to show how IDA 
ICE calculates humidity and a description has been made (in italic). The 
moisture calculations are made based on ISO7730 and is to a certain 
extend explaned by Voulle in the paper “AN NMF BASED MODEL LIBRARY 
FOR BUILDING THERMAL SIMULATION “(Voulle 1999).
Part 1
nOccAct[i] := schedOcc[i] * nOcc[i]; 
The number of occupants present (n) is calculated by multiplying 1) The 
occupancy schedule with (-) 2) the number off  occupants speciﬁ ed in the 
zone (n)
 
QCvOcc[i] :=  nOccAct[i] * 1.8 *(fCl[i] * hCl[i] * (TClLoc[i] - TOp) + 
0.0014 * M[i] * 58 * (34 - TOp));
The convective load from occupants is calculated by 1) the number of 
occupants (n) *1.8 (surface area of an adult man m2,  * 2) factor for area 
increase through clothes (-) * 3) heat transfer coeffi  cient from clothes to 
air (-) * 4) the temperatures of clothes (ºC) – 5) the operative tempera-
tures (degrees) +0.0014 * the metabolic rate (1 Met) * 58 (w *m-2) * (34 
(ºC) -The operative temperature (ºC). This equation is based on ISO7730
QLwOcc[i] :=  nOccAct[i] * 1.8 * TCl4[i];
Radiant load from occupants (W) is calculated by the number of occupants 
* 1.8 (surface area of an adult man) (m2)* Radiation(w/m2)
QOccVap[i]:=  nOccAct[i] * 1.8 * ( 3.05E-3 * ( 5733 - 6.99 * (M[i]*58 - 
W[i]) - PVap) + 1.7E-5 * M[i]*58 * (5867 – Pvap)      
Vapor load from occupants (W) are calculated by 1) the number of 
occupants * 1.8 (surface area of an adult man) (m2) ( 3.05E-3 * ( 5733 - 
6.99 * (The metabolic rate (1 Met) * 58 (w *m-2) - the eff ective mechanical 
power (w/m2) – the vapor pressure (Pa) + 1.7E-5 *  (The metabolic rate (1 
Met) * 58 (w *m-2) * (5867 – the vapor pressure (Pa). This equation is 
based on ISO7730 
QOccMet[i] := nOccAct[i] * 1.8 * M[i] * 58 ;
Total load from occupants (W) are calculated by the number of occupants 
(n) * the area of a person (m2)*the metabolic rate (met) * 58 (W *m-2)
QSweat[i]:= IF (QOccMet[i] - (QCvOcc[i] + QLwOcc[i] + QOccVap[i])) < 
0 THEN 0 ELSE (QOccMet[i] - (QCvOcc[i] + QLwOcc[i] + QOccVap[i]))
The sweat from occupants (W) is calculated as follows: 
If The total load from occupants (W) is smaller than the convective-, 
Radiant- and Vapour load then the sweat from occupants (W) is 0.
However if that is not the case then the sweat from occupants is 
calculated as the total load from occupants subtracted by the convective-
, Radiant- and Vapour load
Part 2
VapFOcc2Zone := SUM i=1, nOp
QOccVap[i] + QSweat[i]
END_SUM / 2430000
The vapor ﬂ ow from occupants (kg/s) is calculated as the sum of the 1) 
Vapor load from occupants (W) and the 2) Sweat from occupants (W) 
divided by 2430000 (j/kg) being the latent heat evaporation of air (GENÇ 
and Portier, 2003)
Part 3
PVapSat := SATPRES (TAirMean); 
The saturated pressure is found based on the zone air temperature   
RelativeHum = RELHUM ((P+10**5), PVapSat, XHum); 
The relative humidity is found based on 
1) the Zone air pressure (Pa) 2) The saturated pressure (Pa) and 3) The 
humidity in the zone  (kg/kg)
==PAGE:136==
126
HumFact*MassAir * XHumLoc’ = VapFOcc2Zone 
                  - SUM i=1, nLocUnit Wf[i] END_SUM
                  + SUM i = 1, nUnit 
         
      schedEquip[i] * (VapFSrcEquip[i] + EvapVapFSrcEquip[i]) 
 
   END_SUM                         
 
   + VapF_0 + SUM i=1, nTerminal VapF[i] END_SUM; 
1) The mass of the air (kg) * 2) a factor the increases the humidity storage 
capacity of air (-) * 3) the humidity in the zone (XHUMLOC) (kg/kg)
Is equal to:
1) The vapour ﬂ ow from occupants (kg/s) (VapFOcc2Zone) - 2) the 
sum of condensation from the AHU (kg/s) – 3) the humidty load and 
evaporative humidity load from equipment * the equipment schedule 
(kg/s) +/- the vapourﬂ ow from the terminals (kg/s)
HumSat:=HumRat((P+10**5), PVapSat); 
The saturated humidity (kg/kg) is calculated based on:  1) the Zone air 
pressure (Pa) 2) The saturated pressure (Pa)
 XHum = MIN(HumSat, XHumLoc)
The humidity ratio in the zone (kg/kg) is then calculated as the minium of 
either 1) The saturated humidity (kg/kg) or 2) the humidity in the zone 
(XHUMLOC)
References
Genc and Portier (2003) Sensible and Latent Heat Productions from 
Broilers in Laboratory Conditions [online] Available at: http://journals.
tubitak.gov.tr/veterinary/issues/vet-05-29-3/vet-29-3-10-0305-4.pdf 
[Accessed 30 May 2016].
ISO7730 (2006) Ergonomics of the thermal environment- Analytical 
determination and interpretation of thermal comfort using calculation 
of the PMV and PPD indicies and local thermal comfort criteria. Danish 
Standards Association.

07.  Correlation between weather 
and amount of visitors
The data concerning daily visitors in museums was provided by the 
diff erent museums.
Daily data of rain and temperature was taken from Weather underground 
from Roskilde and Kastrup Airport as the Airport measurements was to 
be expected to be of high quality. The data from Kastrup was used for 
the tests on all museums except the Viking Ship Museum. Louisiana   and 
M/S Maritime Museum of Denmark was correlated with data from Kastrup 
due to the lack of reliable data in these areas. However as many visitors 
come from the Copenhagen area it was regarded that this weather data 
might be as inﬂ uential on the presence of the museums as the actual 
weather near the museums.
The daily sun hours were daily summations at DMI for the whole northern 
part of Zealand. It was thus not stated where this data was derived from.
The correlation between amount of visitors and either rain, temperature 
or sunhours was done using Excel whereas Rstudio was used for 
calculating R2 based on weekdays and weather data. The output from 
Rstudio calculations are shown on the following pages:
It can be seen that the results diff er much from the diff erent museums.

10.  COMSOL settings
Comsol settings for soil simulation
The soil was calculated using a 2D time depended model. The geometry 
consisted of a set of 35 m wide rectangles with diff erent heights, placed 
at diff erent depths. Smaller rectangles were placed near the top and 
larger towards the bottom.
One boundary condition for the external temperature was applied in 
the top. The rest of the boundairies was calculated as adiabatic. The 
temperature was set with a convective heat ﬂ ux using the formula: 
281.15+8.5*sin((2*pi*(t/(24*(365/12)*60*60)-4)/12)) since comsol 
uses second as units while eq (18) is using months. The heattransferco-
effi  cientis set to 1/0.04 in accordance to DS418
The tetrahedral mesh was set as physics controlled mesh with normal 
size as seen in Figure 1. The minimum element quality is advised by 
COMSOL to be no less than 0.1 (COMSOL, 2014). With these default 
settings the minimum element quality was 0.3676 and thus no further 
mesh reﬁ nement was needed.
The solver type was changed from BDF to Generalized alpha since this 
is a more precise but also less stable method (COMSOL, 2013). In order 
to get a precise solution the absolute tolerance was lowered from 0.1 
initially to 0.000001. This would however still result in simulations in 
which the curves was very jagged with diff erent amplitudes. Thus the 
time steps taken by the solver was changed to “intermediate” from “free” 
which resulted in a much smoother solution with less deviation between 
the years.
Figure 1: The mesh of the soil simulation model for soil
==PAGE:145==
135
Figure 2: the mesh of the soil simulation model for building placed 
in soil

for

This simulation is building as an extension of the previous model and 
thus the same settings. The upper layer of the soil model was made 
shorter in order to make space for half a building consisting of an outer 
layer of concrete and an inner of insulation. The depth of the building 
was varied as well as the thicknesses of the building layers, but the same 
method and settings was used. In addition to the boundary condition for 
the external temperature two extra boundary conditions was applied. A 
vertical boundary of 20 °C with a was set for the inner
The tetrahedral mesh was set as physics controlled mesh with normal 
size as seen in Figure 2. The minimum element quality was 0.4322 and 
thus no further mesh reﬁ nement was needed.

12.  Heat transfer comparison
In order to combine heat transfer simulations from COMSOL and IDA ICE 
a comparison of heat ﬂ ux calculations was done. It was concluded that 
transient heat transfers caused larger deviations but that a combined 
method could be used.
A comparison of calculated heat ﬂ ux between IDA ICE, COMSOL and 
hand calculations with DS418 was made. Four diff erent wall types were 
created (Figure 1). The wall was 40 m2 and the interior and exterior tem-
peratures was 20 ºC and -12 ºC. Heat flux is calculated by the following 
formula (DS418):
(1)
Where:
It can be seen that the temperature diff erence between the exterior and 
the interior is the driving force for the heat ﬂ ux. Having a larger surface 
area or larger U-value will increase the ﬂ ux. The U-value can be found 
with the following formula depending on the thermal resistance:
(2)

A large thermal resistance will thus result in a low U-value. The thermal 
resistance can be found using the formula:
(3)
Where:
As seen in the formula heat loss can be minimized by either using a thicker
material layer or materials with a higher thermal conductivity.
Temperatures of 20 ºC and -12 ºC are used in accordance to DS418, but 
the test could as well be made with another set of temperatures.
The COMSOL model is set up based on the DS418 and the deviation 
between COMSOL and hand calculations is very small for all four cases. 
A slightly larger deviation is showing when comparing DS418 with IDA 
ICE. Heat transfer coefficients are not user defined in IDA ICE and tem-
peratures are not naturally steady. However, steady temperatures can 
be created as seen in the end of this section. The largest deviation is 
however a mere 0.481% and these three methods thus correspond well.
However these heat transfers seen in Table 1 were all done based 
==PAGE:155==
145
 Figure 2: Variations in temperature proﬁ les on both sides of an building component
 Figure 3: Heat ﬂ ux from type B
Figure 4:  Heat ﬂ ux from type C
on steady interior and exterior temperatures. In real life and thus in 
simulations this will rarely happen, as temperatures will rarely be steady 
on any side of a building component. Especially the external tempera-
tures will change. The steady temperatures of the ﬁ rst test corresponds 
to type A in Figure 2. 
A comparison is thus made on the diff erences of COMSOL and IDA ICE 
when temperatures are ﬂ uctuating on both sides of a 500 mm thick layer 
of insulation. Type B is uses an external temperature ﬂ uctuations being 
smaller than the interior, but with the same period, whereas the period 
off  type C is off set in comparison too the inner temperature curves.  The 
temperatures are set to deviate from 20 -25 ºC for the interior and 0 -10 
ºC for the exterior. In Type C a 4 month off set of the sinusoidal wave has 
been made.
Type C deviates from the COMSOL simulation by an average of 1.4% 
whereas type C deviates with an average of 1.3%. At the peaks the 
deviation is generally much smaller. Simulation transient heat transfers 
thus results in higher deviations between the COMSOL and IDA ICE 
method. As seen in Figure 3and Figure 4 COMSOL tends to  change 
slightly faster than IDA ICE. This could be caused by diff erences in heat 
transfer coeffi  cients from air to insulation. But as these coeffi  cient aren’t 
visible in the code of the wall in IDA ICE it is not possible to change them. 
However deviations are small enough to make combined simulations 
using COMSOL and IDA ICE.

In COMSOL the simulation is setup according to DS418. The interior 
and exterior temperatures of 20 º C and 12 ºC is set up as boundary 
conditions. The heat transfer coeffi  cients is as well used in accordance 
to the standard. 
In IDA ICE steady internal and external temperatures does not ”naturally” 
occur. Although a  very narrow dead band  will result in the air temperature 
being very close to 20 ºC, the radiant temperatures will be off . Additional-
ly external temperatures will change in time as well as the wall being able 
absorb and emit energy. The method used in this  test to force stable 
temperatures in this test is as follows:
1) Synthetical weather was choosen and the amplitude was set to zero 
as well as the average temperature being set to -12 ºC.
2) The emittance and arbsorbtance of the wall was set to 1E-11.
3) The wall was disconnected from the zone and a excel sheet was set 
as a source ﬁ le. The ﬁ le contained a row of time indications and a row of 
values of 21 ºC.
==PAGE:158==
148
13.  Inﬂ uence of short term interior 
heat loads on the long term thermal 
storage
A test  wall was made based on a wall consisting of 0.15m concrete interior 
layer of concrete and a 0.3 m exterior layer of concrete. The thickness of 
the insulation varies from 150mm to 500mm.  A 10 m2 room was made 
with no people all week besides 2 people from 9 to 17 Saturday. This 
is thus a high density of people at 0.2 pers / m2 and long period with a 
constant load. The exterior is set to a stable temperature by adjusting 
the synthetic weather and all windows are removed. Furthermore all 
emission and absorption of the external wall is removed. Thereby only 
the change of internal loads will cause diff erences in the wall tempera-
tures. 
Additional nodes are put into each material layer in order to be able to 
measure the temperatures through the materials. Therefore the interior 
walls consists of 2 nodes, the insulation 3 nodes and the exterior wall 2 
nodes. Each node in the wall is logged during the simulation as seen in 
Figure 1 - Figure 3. The brown line of node 4 thus represents the exterior 
surface of the insulation. Even though the internal loads are relatively 
large only a very little peak on the line is showing. Rapid internal load 
changes will thereby only have a minor inﬂ uence on the long term 
thermal storage on the outside of the insulation. 
Figure 1: Temperature curves of wall with 500 mm insulation

14.  Use of the HMwall component
The following is a small introduction to the use of the HMwall component. 
It is only possible to use it in IDA ICE at the advanced level. When used in 
an above ground building the use of HMwall will looks as seen in Figure 2
1) The HMwall is placed either as a new wall or to replaced with the 
existing walls. It must be noted that unlike the standard walls in IDA ICE all 
parameters are to be deﬁ ned manually. The walls include 15 parameters 
to be deﬁ ne. Some parameters are single variables and other are vectors.
2) The connection with the zone is done with three diff erent interfaces. 
The ﬁ rst connects ZONE_A with a term from the zone. This will function 
as a leak transporting energy and humidity. 
The second interface connects RELHUM_A with a humidity sensor 
(READHUMIDITY) in the zone.
The third interface connects the temperaturs of the zone and the wall. 
TQwall from the zone is thus connected to TERM_A from the HMwall
3) The temperature on the B side of the wall is taken from the TQFACE 
if the wall is above ground. Otherwise the connetction could be made to 
the TQground or other methods of determening the undergound temper-
atures as described in this thesis
4) The ambient RH is multiplied with 0.01 in order to get the data in the 
right format. Therefore, place a multiplier and connect a constant of 0.01. 
When opening the multiplier, the window shown in ﬁ gure 1 will appear. 
To connect the ambient ﬁ le click on ”Interfaces” (A), then ”INSIGNALLINK 
1:2” (B) and then doubble-click the unconnected in-signal link to open the 
in-signal link interface (C). Then double-click the name of the in-signal link. 
When the in-signal link interface is open the right click the variable and select 
”binding” (D). It is important that one does not right-click and select 
A
B
C
D
binding in the multiplier interface as the weather-ﬁ le will not be available. 
In the climate ﬁ le connect to ”Rel humidity of air”
5) In interfaces of the HMwall select ZONE_B and connect to OUT2LEAK 
under f$Building body. The connection will not be visible in the zone 
scheme
6) In EMETRQ_WACOBRI the Hmwall will be needed to be connected. 
Select the unconnected ”INPOWER” right-click and select binding. In the 
zone select HMWall and connect to ”QA”
7) With the same method as in ”5” connect the walls in EMETERQ_WALLS.
8) If the HMwall is used for the roof, it must be connected to EMETERQ_
ROOF
 9) Additionally If the HMwall is used for ﬂ oors it must be connected to 
EMETERQ_FLOORS

15.  RH control system
The following is a description of the control system made in order to 
control RH. No standard system in IDA ICE is capable of humidiﬁ cation 
and dehumidiﬁ cation thus a further development of "extra heating 
coil (dehumidiﬁ cation" has been made (see Figure 1). Room tempera-
tures are controlled by a ﬂ oor heating system and the volume of air is 
controlled based on CO2 concentrations are to be kept below 900 ppm 
being category II according to DS15251.The control system functions as 
follows:
1) The set points for humidiﬁ cation and dehumidiﬁ cation are typed in. As 
in seen in Figure 1 having set points of 53 and 50 will result in a dead 
band of 3% RH.
2) The heat exchanger retains part of the heat from the interior. Due to 
the large volume of the rooms the effi  ciency has a great inﬂ uence. The 
As standard the effi  ciency is set to 0.7.
3) A schedule deﬁ nes a yearly change of set points. Values from 0-1 will 
change the two set points speciﬁ ed in 1). Values of 0.85 is used from 
November to March. From April to October the value is 0.96 except from 
June to August where 1 is used. Thereby the RH will be largest in the 
summer.
4) A schedule can turn of heat exchanger if needed.
5) RH is measured from return air.
6) A p controller activates humidiﬁ er if the measured value from 5) is 
lower than the lower set point in 1)
7) Air can be preheated or precooled
8) The supply air will be humidiﬁ ed based on the signal from 6)
9) A thermostat compares the upper value of the set point from 3) with 
the RH of the return air
10) A switch will shift between 9 and 21 ºC depending on a need for 
cooling in order to the RH
11) In case of dehumidiﬁ cation the air will ﬁ rst be cooled to 9 ºC by the 
cooler and then reheated to 20 ºC by the heater thus providing air with 
50 %RH
12) A schedule controls the cooling set points
 
Many other systems could be made in order to control RH and more 
energy effi  cient ways of dehumidifying could be modelled. However this 
is based on the standard components of IDA ICE. If components such as 
desiccant dehumidiﬁ ers were used lower energy consumptions of the 
system could be obtained.
Figure 1 Standard AHU system for dehumidiﬁ cation in IDA ICE

16.  Dynamic user schedules
The following is a description of the method used for this thesis in order 
to add dynamic user schedules by connecting a prn ﬁ le to the zone rather 
than using the schedules from IDA ICE. The problem by using IDA ICE 
schedules is that the are made one week at the time and have to be 
typed in manually into the program. Having a 1 year dynamic schedule 
as used in this thesis would take very long time to type in and would be 
prone to typing errors. Thus another method using the advanced level of 
IDA ICE was developed:
1) The SETOCC is disconnected from the zone as seen in Figure 1. 
 
A ) The zone interface is opened
 
B) Left-click on the SETOCC to open the menu
 
C) Right-click on "Binding". In the connection interface select  
 
"DISCONNECT"
A
C
B
2) Create an excel-ﬁ le with a row of hours and a row containing an 
occupancy schedule with values from 0 to 1. Export the Excel ﬁ le as prn. 
3) Add a source ﬁ le (placed under utilities). Click "browse" to ﬁ nd the prn 
ﬁ le. Then press "take the list of variables from ﬁ le" and press "OK"
4) Connect the source ﬁ le with the Zone as an interface for the SETOOC 
should now be open.
This method can be used for creating other inputs in excel an bypass the 
original values of an IDA ICE variable

17.  Settings of the IDA Model
The IDA IDE model consists of simpliﬁ ed building parts based on early 
sketches of the new Natural History Museum of Denmark. The only 
diff erence between above and below ground buildings are the placement 
and type of vapour barrier. Both above and below ground buildings thus 
have a large thermal mass, especially on the outside of the insulation 
layer. This layer is for the standard case 300 mm but is varied for a 
parameter analysis. 
Properties for materials are based on the DS/EN ISO 10456 standard 
(2008).
For above ground "Icotæt" is used as vapour barrier and Below ground 
Reﬂ ex is used as vapour barrier (Icopal 2016). Being a extremely tight 
barrier to account for the tightness needed to keep out moisture. The 
vapour resistance factor is calculated based on the Diff usion resistance. 
 
High density concrete was used as the 250mm interior layer for all 
simulations except for the two simulations of low weight concrete. the 
exterior layer was high density concrete for all simulations.

Ventilation
The ventilation ﬂ ow was VAV controlled by CO2 to keep the level below 
1100 ppm. 700 ppm  was set as the lower threshold for ventilation. A 
min of 0.1L/s m2.  was used. The humidity was controlled by the AHU as 
seen in appendix 15. Inﬂ ow air was based on schedules synchronized 
with the heating set point schedules.
Humidity control
RH humidity was controlled by the AHU. 
For "loose" settings lower and upper limits for RH control was set to 20 
and 80. The schedule for yearly variation was set to 1 
For "medium" settings lower and upper limits for RH control was set to 
40 and 60. The schedule for yearly variation was set to 1 
For "tight" settings lower and upper limits for RH control was set to 
55and 60. The schedule for yearly variation varied in a sinusoidal pattern 
during the season with a max of 1 and min on 0.82. therby the control 
would be 40-45 in the winter and 55-60 in the summer.
Heating/cooling
The internal temperatures was set according to monthly schedules 
deﬁ ning higher and lower limts. An off set of the maximum and minimum 
was set to 2 degrees to control daily deviation. For Most of the models no 
additional cooling was needed than provided by the ventilation air but for 
most 500 mm cases more cooling than was provided by the ventilation 
system was needed. This cooling was provided by a standard ﬂ oor-heat-
ing/cooling system of 40 W/m2 for both heating and cooling. The off set 
for water based cooling room units was set to 1 ºC. 
Loads
The lighting loads was set to 10 W/m2. The occupant loads was varied 
based on the method described in the thesis. 
27, 54, 108, 216 432 and 864 pers was assigned to the zone io order 
to obtain average occupant densities of: 0.00125, 0.0025, 0.005, 0.01, 
0.02 and 0.04 pers/m2. In order to properly utilize the schedule the 
degree of automatic schedule smoothing was lowered to 1 from the 
standard value of 5.
Simulation method
The calculation was made as a dynamic simulation based on TRY Værløse 
(Copenhagen) weather data using Copenhagen as location with the 
default urban wind proﬁ le. A periodic start-up used and initial temper-
atures in the Hmwalls was set to 20 degrees in order to avoid to large 
initial temperatures.
In Comsol the average temperatures on the exterior side of the insulation 
was measured for the walls and the ﬂ oor. The 11th year of data was in 
excel exported to prn to be added as the exterior temperatures for the 
walls in IDA ICE. Above ground components was conneted to the ambient 
temperatures from the TRY. 
Figure 4: Tempeartures for a year with 500 mm insulation
==PAGE:167==
157
Several COMSOL models had to be made whenever:
- The insulation thicness was changed
- The placement of museum was changed in relation to the ground
- Material properties were changed
- Boundary conditions was changed.
 
Change of boundary condition
By using the DS418 temperatures in COMSOL for boundary conditions 
and compare with above ground buildings in IDA ICE based on TRY will 
give misleading results. The reason for this is that wall temperatures 
in IDA ICE is not only based on air temperatures but also recieved heat 
from radiation. An adjustment on the boundary conditions for COMSOL 
soil was thus made in order to provide similar boundary conditions. The 
input values for the external temperatures of an horizontal surface in 
IDA ICE was measured. This was used to alted the boundary condition in 
COMSOL used ofr the soil (Figure 5).
Figure 5: Adjustment of the boudary condition

18.  Line loss
Line losses are included in the IDA ICE. simulations at all connections 
between building parts in connection with the ambient. The extra ﬂ ux 
that line losses represent are caused by the geometry of the connection 
causing thermal bridges or less insulation. Furthermore corners have 
larger outer surfaces than inner surfaces causing  an increased ﬂ ux.
Line losses will in diff erent buildings diff er due to the types of connections 
not being the same. DS418 does not give a deﬁ nitive answer on how to 
calculate the line loss in an underground building. However all line losses 
in the standard are calculated by subtracting 1 dimensional ﬂ uxes from 
building parts with 2 dimensional ﬂ uxes and dividing by the temperature 
diff erence. This method is seen in ﬁ gure 1. One dimensional ﬂ uxes are 
created by making adiabatic surfaces and measuring the ﬂ uxes at the 
walls. The temperature are then measured around the corner. By diving 
the average ﬂ ux of a year with the average temperatures of a year the 
line loss can be found. 
2D ﬂux
1D ﬂux
Corner temperatu-
-
=(
(
/
Lineloss
This can be done in COMSOL where time, ﬂ uxes and temperatures can 
be exported as vectors into excel. With 300 mm insulation, 1000 mm 
concrete and a 10 year pre-run period the line loss at the connection 
between wall and ﬂ oor are calculated based on average values for year 
11 as:
0.23W/11.39 ºC = 0.020W/K
When increasing the amount of insulation to 500 mm the line loss are 
calculated as:
0.18W/10.13 ºC = 0.018W/K
Due to the constructions are idealized with no connection details the 
line loss is very small. IDA ICE has included line losses in the program for 
losses at diff erent connections. These connections are shown as a the 
value and are graded from "None" to "Very poor". the value of 0.02 W/K 
for a connection between external slab and external walls would be rated 
somewhere in between "None" and "very good".
For the use of simulation values equivalent to "Good" are used at all 
connections in order to use more realistic values for the line losses. In 
reality the values will change from above and below ground buildings, but 
the largest impact will be the choice of connection type.  
Figure: Graphical formula for calculating the lineloss of an underground building
